In particular, | would like to\nthank Jeanne McCarten at Cambridge University Press, and my colleagues and students in the\nEnglish for International Students Unit at the University of Birmingham.\n\nFor this new edition, | have been equally lucky in the support I have received from a number of\nknowledgeable, professional, and generous people. Alison Sharpe had guided the project to\ncompletion with constant encouragement and great diplomacy. Also at Cambridge University\nPress | would hike to thank Kerry Maxwell, Xanthe Sturt Taylor, Jean Hudson, and Anna\nTeevan. Drafts of the reference material were also read by Sylvia Chalker, Frances Eaves-\nWalton, Carmina Gregor: Signes, Carita Paradis, Richard Smith. Their comments were\ninvaluable in helping me to revise and clarify this part of the book.\n\nThanks to Gillian Martin, Roger Penwill and Lisa Smith for the illustrations and to Kamae\nDesign for their work on the finished product. 1 would also hke to thank Cambridge University\nPress for allowing me access to the Cambridge International Corpus.\n\nA number of students and teachers who used the first edition sent me suggestions on how it\nmught be improved, and these have been very useful in preparing this new edition. Thank you\nfor using the book and taking the trouble to write to me.\n\nFinally, my gratitude, as always, to Ann, David and Suzanne.\n\nvii\n\nvil\n\nWho the book is for\n\nThe book is intended for advanced students of English. It is written mainly as a self-study book,\nbut might also be used in class with a teacher.\n\nHow the book is organised\n\nThere are 100 units in the book. Each one looks at a particular area of grammar. Some sections\nwithin each unit focus on the particular use of a grammatical pattern, such as will be + -ing (as\nin will be travelling). Others explore grammatical contrasts, such as whether to use would or\nused to to report past events, or when we use except or except for. The 100 units are grouped\nunder a number of headings such as Tenses and The future. You can find details of this in the\nContents pages.\n\nEach unit consists of two pages. On the lett hand page are explanations and examples; on the\nright are practice exercises. The letters next to each exercise show you which sections of the left\nhand page you need to understand to do that exercise. The Grammar Review presents examples\nand explanations on areas of grammar that you are likely to have studied already at earlier\nstages of learning English. Although terms to describe grammar have been kept to a minimum\nsome have been included, and you can find explanations of these terms in the Glossary. Four\nAppendices tell you about passive verb forms, basic question forms, quotation, and irregular\nverbs. A number of Additional Exercises are included for further practice of particular areas. You\ncan use the Study Guide to help you decide which units to study and which areas of grammar to\n\nrevise in the Grammar Review. You can check your answers to the practice exercises, Additional\nExercises and Study Guide in the Key. The Key also comments on some of the answers. To help\nyou find the information you need there is an Index at the back of the book.\n\nHow to use the book\n\nIt is not necessary to work through the units in order. If you know what grammar points you\nhave ditficulty with, go straight to the units that deal with them, using the Contents or Index to\nhelp you find the relevant unit. If you think that it would be useful to revise more basic\ninformation before you read the reference material in a unit and do the exercises, many units\nhave links at the bottom of the reference page pointing you to the section of the Grammar\nReview where you can find this. In some units you will also find links to the Grammar Review\nin the explanations; for example ‘(see GR:B1)’.\n\nYou can use the units in a number of ways. You might study the explanation and examples first,\ndo the exercises on the opposite page, check your answers in the key, and then look again at the\nexplanations if you made any mistakes. If you just want to practise an area of grammar you\nthink you already know, you could do the exercises first and then study the explanations for\nany you got wrong. You might of course simply use the book as a reference book without doing\nthe exercises.\n\nAdvanced Grammar in Use was written as a self-study grammar book but teachers might also\nfind it useful for supplementing or supporting their classroom teaching.\n\nThe book will probably be most useful for advanced level students for reference and practice.\n‘The Grammar Review towards the back of the book is a reference-only section which presents\n\nbasic knowledge on a number of areas of grammar. This will be useful for students who wish to\nrevise a particular area before moving on to the more advanced material in the units.\n\nNo attempt has been made to order the units according to level of difficulty. Instead you should\nselect units as they are relevant to the syllabus that you are following with your students, or as\n\nparticular difficulties arise.\n\nThere are many ways in which you might use the book with a class. You might, for example,\npresent the explanations on the left hand page of a unit, and use the exercises for classroom\npractice or set them as consolidation material for self-study. Alternatively, you might want to\nbegin with the exercises and refer to the left hand page only when students are having problems.\nYou could also set particular units or groups of units {such as those on Articles or The future)\nfor self-study if individual students are having difficulties.\n\nThere is a set of Additional Exercises, most of which can be used to provide practice of\n\ngrammar points from a number of different units\n\nA ‘classroom edition’ of Advanced Grammar in Use is also available. It has no key and some\nteachers might prefer to use it with their students.\n\nAdvanced English Grammar in Use Second Edition\n\nIf you have already used the first edition of Advanced Grammar in Use, you will see some\nmajor changes in this new edition. The more basic areas of grammar have been moved out\nof the units into a reference section at the back, called the Grammar Review. All of the\nreference pages have been revised, some substantially, and some new units have been\nadded. Most of the exercise pages have entirely new exercises or many new questions.\n\nThe book is now available with or without a CD-ROM. On the CD-ROM you will find\nmore exercises on all of the units (different from those in the book). There are also hundreds\nof test questions, and you can make your own tests. The CD-ROM can also be bought\nseparately,\n\nix\n\nAdvanced\nin Use\n\nWe can use the present continuous with some state verbs (e.g. attract, hike, look, love, sound)\nwhen we want to emphasise that a situation is temporary or for a period of time around the\npresent, Compare:\n\n) Jean stays with us quite often. The children love having her here. and\n\n) Jean’s with us at the moment. The children are loving having her here.\n\nState verbs which we rarely use with the present continuous include believe, consist of, doubt,\nown. {For more examples see GR:A4.}\n\nSome verbs have different meanings when they are used to talk about states and when they\ndescribe actions. With their ‘state’ meanings, they usually take simple rather than continuous\nforms. With their ‘action’ meanings, they may take simple or continuous forms, depending on\ncontext, Compare:\n\nThe new treatment for influenza doesn’t appear to work. (appear: state = seem) and\n© Madonna is currently appearing in a musical on Broadway./ She often appears in\n\nmusicals. (appear: action = take part)\n\nDo you think it’s a good idea? (think: state = about an opinion) and\n\nI'm thinking of going in August/ Your trouble is you think too much.\n\n(think: action = consider)\n\nOther verbs like this include anticipate, cost, expect, feel, fit, have, imagine, measure, weigh.\n\nWith some verbs describing mental states (e.g. find, realise, regret, think, understand) we can\nuse the present continuous to emphasise that we have recently started to think about something\nor that we are not sure about something. Compare:\n\nI regret that the company will have to be sold. (= | have made the decision and | am sorry\nabout it) and\n\nI'm regretting my decision to give her the job. (= | am increasingly aware that it was the\nwrong decision)\n\nWhen it means ‘think carefully about’ the verb consider is only used with the present\ncontinuous:\nHe’s considering taking early retirement. (not He considers taking early retirement.)\n\nSome other verbs describing preferences and mental states (e.g. agree, believe, conclude, know,\nprefer) are rarely used with the present continuous:\n© [believe you now. (not I'm believing you now.)\n\nWe use the present simple with verbs which perform the action they describe:\n© Ladmit I can’t see as well as [ used to.\n) We apologise for not replying earlier.\n\nOther verbs like this (sometimes called performatives} include acknowledge, advise, beg,\nconfess, congratulate, declare, deny, forbid, guarantee, name, order, permit, predict, promise,\nrefuse, remind, request, thank, warn. Some verbs used as performatives in affirmative i\n{= positive} sentences (apologise, deny, guarantee, promise, suggest) have a similar meaning with ]\neither the present simple or the present continuous in negative sentences:\n\n» I don’t deny/ I'm not denying taking the books, but Andy said it would be okay.\n\nNote that we can use modals with performatives, often to make what we say more tentative or\npolite:\n\n© We would advise you to arrive nwo hours before the flight leaves.\n\n™ I must beg you to keep this a secret.\n\nGrammar review: present continuous —* Al-A?; present simple — A3-AS\n\nExercises Unit 1\n\nWw\n\n12\n\nComplete the sentences with the verbs given, using negatives or questions where necessary. This can make them more\ndirect and exciting and hold people's attention:\nShe goes up to this man and looks straight into his eyes. He’s not wearing his glasses, and\nhe doesn’t recognise her...\nThis man’s playing golf when a kangaroo bounds up to him, grabs his club and hits his\nball about half a mile...\nThe main events are usually described in sequence using the present simple and longer\nbackground events are described using the present continuous.\n\nIn narratives and anecdotes the present simple can be used to highlight an event. Often it is used\nafter past tenses and with a phrase such as suddenly or all of a sudden:\n© [was sitting in the park, reading a newspaper, when all of a sudden this dog jumps at me.\n\nWe also use the present simple and present continuous in live commentaries (for example, on\nsports events) when the report takes place at the same time as the action:\nKing serves to the lett-hand court and Adams makes a wonderful return. She’s playing\nmagnificent tennis in this match....\n\nWe can use the present simple in phrases such as It says here, I hear, I gather, I see, I understand\nand They say, (Someone) says, (Someone) tells me to introduce news that we have heard, read,\nseen (e.g. on television), or been told, We can also use past tenses (e.g. It said here, I heard):\nI gather you're worried about Ken. © Jane tells me you're thinking of emigrating.\nProfessor Orto is at the conterence and I hear she’s an excellent speaker.\n\nThe present simple is often used in newspaper headlines to talk about events that have\nrecently happened:\n\nQUAKE HITS CENTRAL IRAN i FOREIGN MINISTER RE. CNS |\n————_—_—__—.\n\n— —_—.\n\nSCIENTISTS FIND BRIGHTEST STAR ) FIRE BREAKS OUT IN HOTEL ROOM\nSk eS ee\nWe can use the present simple to refer to the contents of books, films, newspapers, etc:\n‘Thompson gives a list of the largest European companies in Chapter Six.\nAt the beginning of the book, three men find $4 million in a crashed plane.\nIn the film, Joan Smithson takes the role of a private detective.\n\nWe can use the present continuous with adverbs such as always, constantly, continually or forever\nto emphasise thar something is done so often that it is characteristic of a person, group or thing:\na: Lthink I'll stay here after all. 6; You are constantly changing your mind.\nTony is a really kind person. He’s ahvays offering to help me with my work.\nWe often use this pattern to indicate disapproval. The past continuous is used in a similar way\nwith these adverbs (e.g. Was Kath always asking you for money, too?).\n\nWe can use the present continuous to describe something we regularly do at a certain ume:\nAt 8 o'clock I’m usually driving to work, so phone me on my mobile.\n7 o'clock is a bit early. We're generally eating then.\n\nWe can use the present (or past) conunuous rather than the present (or past) simple with the\nverb wonder if we want to be especially friendly or polite, particularly it we are unsure about\nthe other person's feelings towards something or how they will react to what we say:\nYou said that there were only 50 books in the boxes. I’m just wondering/ | was just\nwondering whether you counted them all? Perhaps we could meet at nine\na controversial view of Britain's role in the war in\n\nTime expressions that refer to the present, such as this morning/weck/month and today, can be\nused with cither past simple or present perfect verbs. If we think of this morning (etc.} as a past,\ncompleted ume period, then we use the past simple; if we think of this morning (etc.) as a ume\nperiod which includes the present moment, then we use the present perfect. Compare:\n\nI didn’t shave this morning. (= the morning is over and I didn’t shave) and\nThaven't shaved this morning. (= itis sull the morning and I might shave later}\n\nIn news reports, you will often read about or hear recent events introduced with the present\npede, and then the past simple or other past tenses are used to give details:\n\nuN “A teacher from Oslo has\n\nbecome the first woman to cross\nthe Antarctic alone. It took her\n42 days to make the crossing\nwith her dog team...\"\n\n‘The film star Jim Cooper has\ndied of cancer. Be was 68 and\nlived in Texas..\n\nO ‘The US space shuttle Atlanus\nhas returned safely to earth. It\nlanded in Florida this\nmorning...”\n\nIn a sentence which includes a time clause with sizce, we generally prefer a past simple verb in\nthe ume clause and a present perfect verb in the main clause. The time clause refers to a\n\nparticular point in the past:\n1 Since Mr Hassan became president, both taxes and unemployment have increased. (rather\nthan ...has become...}\nShe hasn't been able to play tennis since she broke her arm. (rather than ...has broken...)\n\nNotice, however, that we use the present perfect in the time clause if the two situations\ndescribed in the main clause and time clause extend until the present:\nHave you met any of your neighbours since you've lived here? (mot ...you lived...)\n\nAfter the pattern It/This/That is/wull be the first time...\nthe next clause:\nThat's the first time I've seen Jan look embarrassed. (reporting a past event)\n© It won't be the first time she has voted against the government in her long career. (talking\nabout a future event)\n\nwe generally use the present perfect in\n\nNotice, however, that after I/This/That was the first time... we generally use the past pertect\n(see Unit 5):\nIt was the first time I'd talked to Ella outside the office.\n\nWith time clauses introduced by after, when, until, as soon as, once, by the time and the time\nexpressions the minute/second/moment the past simple refers to past, completed events and the\npresent perfect refers to future events. She’s washing her hair.\n\n© As you're not using your car at the moment, can I borrow it?\n\n0 This time yesterday I was flying over the Pacific.\n\n© Was she wearing that red dress when you saw her?\n\nWe use simple forms to talk about general situations, habits, and things that are or were always\ntrue:\nC When I worked as a postman I got up at 3 o'clock every morning.\nC Miguel doesn’t play golf very well.\n(2) These birds build their nests on the ground.\nThe earthquake struck the area at midday yesterday. (past simple for completed events)\n\nWe use simple forms with verbs that describe unchanging states; that is, things that stay the\nsame:\n\n© She intends to work hard at school and go on to university.\n\n© Did you understand the instructions we were given?\nHowever, we can use continuous forms with these verbs when they describe something\nhappening or changing:\n© She was intending to talk to Tony about the idea, but she didn’t get the opportunity.\n© I'm understanding physics much better now that Mr Davies is teaching us.\n\nPerfect\n\nWe use perfect verb forms to describe one event or state from the point of view of a later time.\nThe present pertect suggests a connection between something that happened in the past and the\npresent time. Notice, however, that the situation or event does not have to continue until the\ntime of speaking, only to have some connection or relevance to the present time:\n\n© I've finished the new Harry Potter book now, so you can borrow my copy if you like.\n\n© Have you turned the gas off? I don’t like it to be on when I'm not at home.\n\n© Your nose is bleeding. Has somebody hit you?\n\nThe past perfect is used to locate a past event before another past event:\nft? Linvited him out to dinner, but he said he had already eaten,\n© By the time I picked up the phone, they had rung off.\n\nCombinations of perfect and continuous\n\nWe combine the perfect and continuous forms in the present perfect continuous to describe an\nactivity in progress cither at or recently before the time of speaking, and possibly beyond it:\nc Ihave been following the discussions with great interest.\n\nWe can also use the present perfect continuous to talk about activities that have recently\nfinished with some result that can be seen, heard, ete.:\n© Look at the dirt on your clothes! Have you been digging in the garden again?\n\nThe past pertect continuous has a similar meaning. However, the point of reference is not ‘now’\n(as it is with the present perfect continuous} but a point in the past:\n\n1 When we met Simon and Pat, they had been riding.\n\nOC It had been snowing heavily for hours and when I went to the door [| couldn't open tt.\n\nGrammar review: Section A\n\nExercises Unit 8\n\n8.1 Complete this letter from Australia using the following verbs. There’s going to be a storm.\n© ‘What's the matter with her?’ ‘It looks like she’s going to faint.’\n\n‘To predict the future we often use will with [ bet (informal), | expect, | hope, | imagine,\nI reckon (informal), I think, I wonder, and I’m sure, and in questions with think and reckon:\nI imagine the stadium will be full for the match on Saturday.\n1) That cheese smells awful. 1 bet nobody will eat it.\nWhen do you think you'll finish work? ) Do you reckon he'll say yes?\nBe going to can also be used with these phrases, particularly in informal contexts.\n\nWe use will when we make a decision at the moment of speaking (see GR:B1) and be going\nto for decisions about the future that have already been made (see GR:B5). Compare:\nPil pick him up at 8.00. (an offer; making an arrangement now) and\nI'm going to collect the children at 8.00. (this was previously arranged)\n‘Pineapples are on special offer this week.” ‘In that case, I'll buy two.’ and\n© When I’ve saved up enough money, I’m going to buy a digital camera.\n\nHowever, in a formal style, we use will rather than be going to to talk about future events that\nhave been previously arranged in some detail. Compare:\n© Are you going to talk at the meeting tonight? and\n> The meeting will begin at 9.00 a.m. Refreshments will be available from 8.30 onwards.\n\nWe can use will or be going to with little difference in meaning in the main clause of an if-\nsentence when we say that something (often something negative) is conditional on something else:\nIf we go on like this, we'll/we’re going to lose all our money.\nYou'lWYou're going to knock that glass over if you're not more careful\n\nWhen the future event does not depend on the action described in the if-clause, we use be going,\nto, not will. This kind of sentence is mainly found in spoken English. Compare:\n© I'm going to open a bottle of lemonade, if you want some. (= I'm going to open a bottle\nof lemonade. Do you want some?) and\n© I'l open a bottle of lemonade if you want some. (= If you say you want some, I'll open a\nbottle.)\n\nHowever, we use will, not be going to, when the main clause refers to offers, requests, promises,\netc. and ability:\n© If Jack phones I'll let you know. (= an offer; *..., 'm going to let you know’ suggests ‘I\nintend to let you know when Jack phones’)\n© If you look to your left, you'll see the lake. However, we prefer the present simple\nfor fixed, unchangeable events. Compare:\nDoes the sale finish on Thursday or Friday? (or Will the sale finish...?) and\n> The sun rises at $.16 tomorrow, (more likely than The sun will rise...)\n\nWe avoid the present simple when we talk about less formal or less routine arrangements, or\npredictions. Instead we use will, be going to, or the present continuous:\n© Are you just staying in to watch TV tonight, or are you coming dancing? (not Do you\njust stay to watch TV tonight, or do you come...)\nIt’s only a problem in Britain now, but it will affect the rest of Europe soon. (not... butit\naffects the rest of Europe soon.)\n\nWe use the present simple, not will, to refer to the future -\n% in time clauses with conjunctions such as after, as soon as, before, by the time, when, while,\nuntil:\n) When you see Dave, tell him he still owes me some money. (not When you will see Dave.\n) I should be finished by the tame you get back. (not ...by the ume you will get back.)\n\n% in conditional clauses with if, in case, provided, and unless:\nProvided the right software is available, | should be able to solve the problem.\n2 I'll bring some sandwiches in case we don't find anywhere decent to eat.\n\nw when we talk about possible future events with suppose, supposing, and what if at the\nbeginning of a sentence, Notice that the past simple can be used with a similar meaning:\nSuppose we miss the bus ~ how will we get home? (or Suppose we missed...)\nWhat if the train's late? Where shall | meet you then? (or What if the train was late?)\n\nPresent continuous\n\nWe can often use either the present continuous or be going to with a similar meaning to talk\nabout planned future events. This use of the present contunuous indicates that we have a firm\nintention or have made a definite decision to do something, although this may not already be\narranged:\n\n) Are you seeing the doctor again next week? (or Are you going to see...?}\n\n) I'm not asking Tom to the party. (or I'm not going to ask...)\n\nHowever, we don’t use the present continuous tor the future -\n% when we make or report predictions about activities or events over which we have no control\n(we can’t arrange these):\nI think it’s going to rain soon.\n( Scientists say that the satellite won't cause any damage when tt falls to Earth some time\nthis afternoon.\n\nvy when we talk about permanent future situations:\n2 People are going to live/will live longer in the future.\nHer new house is going to have/will have three floors.\n\nMany people avoid be going to + go/come and use the present continuous forms of go and\ncome instead:\n\n2 I'm going to town on Saturday. (rather than I'm going to go to town...)\n\n3 Are you coming home for lunch? (rather than Are you going to come...?)\n\nGrammar review: present simple for the future > 86; present continuous for the future — B7\n\nExercises\n\n10.1\n\n10.2\n\n10.3\n\nUse the present simple of one of these verbs to complete the sentences if possible. Often it is the result of a\nprevious decision or arrangement:\n\n© This time next year this part of the garden will be looking beautiful.\n\n( She will be taking up her place at university in October.\n\n0 When it goes into orbit, the spacecraft will be carrying 30 kilos of plutonium.\n\nWe can also use the future continuous to talk about a future activity that is part of the normal\ncourse of events or that is one of a repeated or regular series of events:\nDr Jones will be giving the same talk in room 103 at 10.00 next Thursday.\nD Will you be driving to Glasgow, as usual?\n\nWe can often use either the future continuous or the present continuous when we talk about\narranged activities or events in the future (see also Unit 10). Compare:\n(0 We will be leaving for Istanbul at 7.00 in the evening. (timetabled; or ...are leaving...) and\n() When the race starts later this afternoon the drivers will be hoping for drier weather than\nlast year. (not ...are hoping...; not reporting the details of a programme or timetable)\n\nThe future continuous is sometimes used to indicate that a future activity is pre-arranged. Using\nwill can indicate willingness, intention, invitation, etc. Compare:\n© Ann will be helping us to organise the party. (suggests a previous arrangement) and\nAnn’ll help us organise the party. (suggests she is willing to help)\n\nWhen we don’t want to indicate willingness, intention, invitation, etc., we prefer to use the\nfuture continuous instead of will. For example, if guests have stayed longer than you wanted,\nand you don’t know when they are leaving, you might ask:\n\n© Will you be staying with us again tonight? (asking about their plans) rather than\n\nCO Will you stay with us again tonight? (they might think this is an invitation)\n\nFuture perfect and future perfect continuous: | will have done and | will have been doing\n\nWe use the future perfect to say that something will be ended, completed, or achieved by a\nparticular point in the future:\nBy the time you get home | will have cleaned the house from top to bottom.\n© I'm sure his awful behaviour will soon have been forgotten. (= passive form)\n\nWe use the future perfect continuous to emphasise the duration of an activity in progress at a\nparticular point in the future:\n\n© On Saturday, we will have been living in this house for a year.\n\n© Next year | will have been working in the company for 30 years.\n\nWith both the future perfect and future perfect continuous we usually mention the future time\n(By the time you get home..., On Saturday..., etc.).\n\nThe future continuous, future perfect and future perfect continuous can also be used to say\nwhat we believe or imagine is happening around now:\n© We could ask to borrow Jim’s car. He won't be using it today - he went to work by bike.\n© Most people will have forgotten the fire by now.\nG Tennis fans will have been queuing at Wimbledon all day to buy tickets.\n\nWe can use the future perfect continuous to say what we think was happening at a point in the past:\nUO Motorist Alan Hesketh will have been asking himself whether speed cameras are a good\nidea after he was fined £100 last week for driving at 33 mph in a 30 mph zone.\n\nGrammar review: future continuous — 68\n\nExercises Unit 11\n\n11.1 Choose a verb that can complete both sentences in the pair. (\n\nForay enterained write ne’s\n\nBe to + infinitive is commonly used in news reports to talk about events that are likely to\nhappen in the near future:\n\n( Police officers are to visit every home in the area.\n\n© The main Rome to Naples railway line is to be reopened today. (passive form)\n\nIt is used to talk about formal or official arrangements, formal instructions, and to give orders:\nYou are not to leave the school without my permission.\n© The European Parliament is to introduce a new law on safety at work.\nChildren are not to be left unsupervised in the museum. (passive form)\nPassive forms are often used to make orders and instructions more impersonal.\n\nNotice that we only use be to + infinitive to talk about future events that can be controlled by\npeople. Compare:\nU0 In the next few years, thousands of speed cameras are to appear on major roads.\n(or ...will appear...) and\nO Scientists say they can’t predict when or where the disease will appear again. (not ...the\ndisease is to appear again; the appearance of the disease can’t be controlled)\n© The President is to return to Brazil later today. (or ...will return...) and\nThe comet will return to our solar system in around 500 years. (not The comet is to\nreturn...; the movement of the comet can’t be controlled)\n\nHowever, when be to + infinitive refers to the future from the past (see Unit 14B), we often use\nit to describe what happened to someone, whether they were able to influence events or not:\nO Matthew Flinders sailed past Tasmania in 1770, but it was to be a further 30 years\nbefore he landed there.\n(2 Clare Atkins was to write two more books about her experiences in Africa before her\ndeath in 1967.\n\nWe often use be to + infinitive in if-clauses to say that something must happen first (in the main\nclause) before something else can happen (in the :f-clause):\n\nO If the human race is to survive, we must look at environmental problems now.\n\nO The law needs to be revised if justice is to be done. (passive form)\n\nCompare the use of be to + infinitive and the present simple for the future in if-clauses:\nO If Jones is to win gold at the next Olympics, he needs to work on his fitness. and\nO If Jones wins gold at the next Olympics, he has said that he will retire from athletics.\n\nNotice how the order of cause and effects in if-sentences is reversed with these two tenses:\n© If Jones is to win gold... (= effect), he needs to work... (= cause) and\n(2 If Jones wins gold... {= cause), he has said that he will retire... (= effect)\n\nWe can use be about to + infinitive to say that something will (not) happen in the very near\nfuture:\n\n© I’m about to start work on my second novel.\n\n© Appearing on TV might make her famous, but it’s not about to make her rich.\n\nNotice that while be to + infinitive is mainly used in news reports and formal contexts, we often\nuse be about to ¢ infinitive in conversation:\n\n© We're just about to eat. Do you want to join us?\n\n© 1 was about to go to bed when my brother turned up.\n\nExercises Unit 12\n\n12.1\n\n12.2\n\nComplete these newspaper extracts using the verbs given. We can use be on the verge of.../ brink of.../\npoint of... (+ -ing or noun) to say that something will happen soon:\n© People are on the verge of starvation as the drought continues.\n© Scientist are on the brink of making major advances in the fight against AIDS.\nI looked for my car everywhere in the car park but couldn’t find it. | was on the point of\nphoning the police, when I remembered that I'd walked to work that morning.\nBe on the brink of usually refers to something important, exciting, or very bad.\n\nWe use be due to (+ infinitive) to say that something is expected to happen at a particular time,\nbe sure/bound to (+ infinitive) to say that something is likely or certain to happen, and be set to\n(+ infinitive) to say that something is ready to happen.\n© The company’s chief executive is due to retire next year, but following today’s\nannouncement of further losses she is sure to be asked to leave sooner.\n“Will there be somewhere to get a coffee at the station?’ ‘Oh, yes, there’s bound to be.”\nHer new film is set to be a great success.\nNotice that we use due to + noun to give the reason for something, not to talk about the future\n(e.g. Due to illness, Pavarotti is unable to perform tonight).\n\nNote that past tense forms of be can be used with these phrases to talk about future events seen\nfrom the past:\n© It was his 64\" birthday in 1987 and he was due to retire the following year.\n\nWe use some verbs with a to-infinitive to talk about intentions:\n© Do you mean to work any harder in your second year at college?\n© We guarantee to refund your money if you are dissatisfied with the computer.\n\nOther verbs like this include agree, aim, expect, hope, intend, plan, promise, propose, resolve,\nundertake, and want. The present continuous + to-infinitive or present simple + to-infinitive can\nbe used with aim, expect, hope, intend, plan, propose, and want to talk about intentions:\n\n© I’m aiming to get to Bangkok by the end of June. (or I aim to get...)\n\nSome people, particularly in speech and in journalism, use be looking + to-infinitive to mean\nplanning a course of action:\nWe're looking to create 3,000 jobs in the city over the next year.\n\nWhen these verbs are used with past tense forms, they are concerned with future events seen\nfrom the past (see also Unit 14):\n1 Jack had resolved to become fluent in Spanish before his 30° birthday.\nShe was expecting to mberit her father’s fortune when he died.\n© The new management had been looking to create 20 new jobs.\n\nSome people use shall (and shan’t) instead of will (and won't) in statements about the future with\nI and we. However, it is more common to use will (particularly its contracted form ‘Il) and won't\n= He was a good friend and we shall miss him greatly. (pore commonly ...we'll miss...)\n> I definitely intend to visit Canada, but I shan’t go for the next five years. (more\n\ncommonly ...1 won't...)\n\nIn current English we don’t usually use shall/shan’t with other subjects (it, she, they, etc.) to talk\nabout the future, although this is found in formal rules and in older literary styles:\n\n© The match referee shall be the sole judge of fair play.\n\n© All people of the world shall live together as brothers.\n\nExercises Unit 13\n\n13.1\n\nExpand the notes to complete the sentences, using the phrases in A.\ndue - announce verge-become —_ bound — forget brink - go set - make\npoint — phone sure — prove set - rise brink - sign point - turn back\nverge — quit due - undergo\n\n1 The government's tax increases are S4r€ © prove unpopular, especially among low-paid\n\nworkers.\n\nwv\n\nThe snow was getting heavier, and I was when I saw lights from a house across\n\nthe fields.\n\n3 [have always wanted to own a sports car, and now my dream is a reality.\n\nThe Finance Minister is his economic plans for the year to the public later today.\n5 The number of new jobs in London is increasing and is even more dramatically\nin the next few years.\n\n6 We were a multi-million dollar contract with the oil company when it was taken\n\nover by its main rival\n\n7/8 Can you bring some paper plates when you come to the party tonight? However, we avoid\nbe able to -\nwv when we talk about something that is happening as we speak:\nWatch me, Mum; I can stand on one leg. (not ...I’m able to stand on one leg.)\nw before passives:\n© CDs can now be copied easily. (rather than CDs are now able to be copied...)\nx when the meaning is ‘know how to’:\n= Can you cook? (rather than Are you able to cook?)\n\nIf we talk about a single achievement, rather than a general ability in the past, we usually use be\nable to rather than could. Compare:\n© Sue could play the flute quite well. (or ...was able to...; a general ability) and\n> She swam strongly and was able to cross the river easily, even though it was swollen by\nthe heavy rain. (not She swam strongly and could cross...; a specific achievement)\n\nHowever, could is usually more natural —\nxt in negative sentences:\n> I tried to get up but I couldn’t move. (rather than ...1 wasn't able to move.)\n\nx with verbs of the senses — e.g. feel, hear, see, smell, taste — and with verbs of ‘thinking’\n\n- e.g. believe, decide, remember, understand:\n\n© Lcould remember the crash, but nothing after that. (rather than | was able to remember...)\nvw after the phrases the only thing/place/time, and after all when it means ‘the only thing’:\n\nAll we could see were his feet. (rather than All we were able to...)\n\nx to suggest that something almost didn’t happen, particularly with almost, hardly, just, nearly:\n\n© l could nearly touch the ceiling. {rather than | was nearly able to...)\n\nCan and could: talking about possibility\n\nTo talk about the theoretical possibility of something happening we use could, not can. However,\nwe use can, not could, to say that something is possible and actually happens. Compare:\n© It could be expensive to keep a cat. (= if we had one, it could or it may not be expensive) and\nO It can be expensive to keep a cat. (= it can be, and it sometimes is)\n\nWe use can't, not couldn't, to say that something is theoretically or actually impossible:\n© There can’t be many people in the world who haven't watched television.\n© The doctor can’t see you this morning; he’s busy at the hospital.\n\nWe use can to indicate that there is a very real possibility of a future event happening. Using\ncould suggests that something is less likely or that there is some doubt about it. Compare:\n) We can stay with Jim in Oslo. (= we will be able to stay) and\n© We could stay with Jim in Oslo. possible; if he’s there)\n\nCould and be allowed to: talking about permission\n\nTo say that in the past someone had general permission to do something - that is, to do it at\nany time — we can use either could or was/were allowed to. However, to talk about permission\nfor one particular past action, we use was/were allowed to, but not could. Compare:\n() Anyone was allowed to fish in the lake when the council owned it. (or ...could fish...) and\n0D Although he didn’t have a ticket, Ken was allowed to come in. (not ...could come in.)\nIn negative sentences, we can use either couldn't or wasn'/weren't allowed to to say that\npermission was not given in general or particular situations:\n© We couldn't/weren’t allowed to open the presents until Christmas morning.\n\nGrammar review: can, could — C1-C4; be able to —» C5-C6; be allowed to —> C7\n\nExercises Unit 15\n\n5.\n\n1\n\nUnderline the correct or more natural answer. However, we\nprefer may in academic or formal language to talk about the characteristics or behaviour of\nsomething:\nThe seeds from the plant may grow up to 20 centimetres in length.\nand in speech we prefer might to say what we will possibly do in the future:\n© I might paint the kitchen purple.\n\nWe don't use may to ask questions about the possibility of something happening. Instead we\nuse, for example, could(n’t) or the phrase be likely:\nCould it be that you don’t want to leave? (not May it be that you ...?)\n0 Are you likely to be in Spain again this summer? (not May you be in Spain...?)\n\nIt is possible to use might in this type of question, but it is rather formal:\n© Might they be persuaded to change their minds?\nNote that we can use may in formally asking for permission and offering help:\nMay I leave now? © May I help you?\n\nMight (not ‘may’) + bare infinitive is sometimes used to talk about what was typically the case\nin the past. This is a formal or literary use:\n\n= During the war, the police might arrest you for criticising the king.\n\n© Years ago children might be sent down mines at the age of six. (passive form)\n\nWe can also use could + bare infinitive in examples like this to talk about past ability (see Unit\n15). For example, ‘During the war, the police could arrest you...’ means that the police were\nlegally able to arrest you.\n\nWhen we say that a person or thing compensates to some extent for a limitation or weakness\nhaving another characteristic, we can use a pattern with may/might not + bare infinitive\n..-but... or may/might not have + past participle...but.\n© The painting may not be a masterpiece, but you've got to admit that the colours are\nstriking.\n) She might not have danced very gracefully, but she had a lot of energy and enthusiasm.\n\nWe use may/might (not ‘can’) + have+ past participle and may/might (not ‘can’) + be + -ing to\ntalk about possible events in the past, present and future:\n© Do you think Jean may/might have completed the report by now? (past)\n© His maths may/might have improved by the time the exam comes round. (future)\n© Malcolm isn’t in his office. He may/might be working at home today. (present)\n) When I go to Vienna I may/might be staying with Richard, but I’m not sure yet. I said I'd call him last night, but I forgot.\n\nTo draw a conclusion about —\n% something that happened in the past we use must + have + past participle:\n© That’s not Kate’s car. She must have borrowed it from her parents.\n# something happening at or around the time of speaking we use must be + -ing:\n\n(2 Lean’t hear anyone moving around upstairs. You must be imagining things.\nx something that is likely to happen in the future we use must be going to or must be + -ing:\n© ‘What are all those workmen doing?’ ‘I think they must be going to dig up the road.”\n© I was wrong about the meeting being today. It must be happening next Friday.\nw% a present situation we use must be, or have (got) to be in informal speech:\n© Their goalkeeper has got to be at least two metres tall! (or ...must be...)\n\nWe can use must have to to say that we conclude something based on what we know about a\npresent situation and must have had to to conclude something about a past situation:\n© [can’t start the computer. You must have to know a password. (= a password is necessary)\n© John wasn’t at home when I went round. He must have had to go out unexpectedly.\nNote that we can’t say ‘must've to’ or ‘must have got to/ must've got to’ (but we can say\nmust’ve had to).\n\nIn questions that hope for or expect a negative answer we prefer have (got) to, although in\nformal contexts must is sometimes used:\n© Do we have to answer al] the questions? (or Have we got to. Must we...? is also\npossible but rather formal)\n\nWe use have to in questions that imply a criticism. Must can also be used, although some peo,\nthink this is rather old-fashioned. We usually stress have and must in sentences like this:\n© Do you haye to play your trumpet here? It’s deafening me! (or more formally Must you\nplay...?)\n\nSometimes we can use cither have to or have got to. However —\nw we use have to with frequency adverbs such as always, never, normally, rarely, sometimes, ¢\n© 1 often have to work at the weekend to get everything done.\nxx with the past simple we use had to especially in questions and negative sentences:\n© When did you have to give it back? (not When had you got to give it back?)\n© We didn’t have to wait too long for an answer. (not We hadn't got to wait too long...)\nwr if have is contracted (e.g. I’ve, He’s, It'd) then we must include got:\n© The experiment has failed twice before, so it’s got to work this time. (not ...so it’s to\nwork this time.)\nx we don’t use have got to with other modal verbs:\nEmployees will have to accept the new conditions or be dismissed. (not Employees will\nhave got to accept...)\n\nNotice also that have got to is often preferred in informal speech.\n\nGrammar review: —> C20-C24\n\nExercises Unit 18\n\n18.1\n\n18.2\n\n18.3\n\nComplete these sentences with one of these forms: must have + past participle; must + bare\ninfinitive; must be + -ing; or must have (had) to. As a\nmodal verb it doesn’t change its tense and doesn’t add *-s’ for the third person singular,\nCompare:\n© I needed to leave early. or = She's thirsty, She needs a drink. (= ordinary verb) and\n» You needn't speak so loudly. (= modal verb)\n\nWhen it is a modal verb need is most commonly used in negative sentences, often with verbs\nlike bother, concern, fear, panic, worry:\n© I’ve already cleaned the car so you needn't bother to do it.\n© Judges in England need not retire until they are 75.\n© I was very nervous before the interview, but I needn't have worried. Everyone was very\nfriendly and I got the job.\n\nIt is sometimes used in questions, but we prefer to use need as an ordinary verb or have to:\n© Need you go so soon? (= modal verb; less common and rather formal}\nDo you need to go so soon? (= ordinary verb) or © De you have to go so soon? |\n\nIt is rarely used in affirmative sentences (that is, not questions or negatives), but is sometimes\nfound in written English, particularly in fiction:\n) We need have no fear for Nicole, she can take care of herself.\n\nIn other styles of formal written English it is used in this way with negative words such as\nhardly, never, nobody/no-one, and only:\n© The changes need only be small to make the proposals acceptable. (less formally The\nchanges only need to be...)\n) Nobody ever need know about the money. (less formally Nobody ever needs to know...)\n) ‘L don’t want my parents to know.’ ‘They need never find out.’ (less formally They never\nneed to find out.)\n\nTo give permission not to do something we can use either needn’t or don’t need to:\n© You needn't cut the grass, I'll do it later. (or You don’t need to cut the grass...)\n\nTo talk about a general necessity, we prefer don’t need to:\n© You don't need to be over 18 to get into a disco. (rather than You needn't be...)\n\nWe can often use either needn’t or don’t have to with little difference in meaning to say that it is\nunnecessary to do something:\n© You needn't whisper. Nobody can hear us. (the\nspeaker’s decision) and\n© We've been told that we don’t have to be at work until 10.00 tomorrow. (reporting\nsomeone else’s decision.)\n\nWe can use needn't (or don’t have to) to say that something is not necessarily true. We don’t use\nmustn’t in this way (see also Unit 18C):\n> Volcanoes needn't erupt constantly to be classified as ‘active’. Ought\n\nto is used particularly in speech and most often to talk about obligation rather than probability.\n\nNotice also the following details —\n\n+ we prefer should when we say what an outside authority recommends:\n\n(1 The manual says that the computer should be disconnected from the power supply before\nthe cover is removed. (rather than ...ought to be disconnected...)\n\n+ we use should (or would), not ought to, when we give advice with L...:\nOI should leave early tomorrow, if 1 were you. (or | would leave...; or I'd leave...)\n\nve we prefer should in questions, particularly wh-questions:\n© What should I do if I have any problems? ©) Should I ring you at home?\nSome people might use ‘What ought I to do...?’ and ‘Ought I to...?’, but this is rather formal.\n\nNote that when we conclude, on the basis of some evidence we have, that something is certain\nor very likely we can use must (see Unit 18) but not should/ought to:\n© It’s the third time she’s been skating this week. She must really enjoy it.\n\nWe use should/ought to + have + past participle to talk about something that didn’t happen in\nthe past and we are sorry that it didn’t:\n\nCO We should/ought to have waited for the rain to stop. (I'm sorry we didn’t)\nWe often use this pattern to indicate some regret or criticism and the negative forms\nshouldn’t/oughtn’t to have are almost always used in this way.\n\nWe also use should/ought to + have + past participle to talk about an expectation that\nsomething happened, has happened, or will happen:\nO If the flight was on time, he should/ought to have arrived in Jakarta early this morning.\n2 The builders should/ought to have finished by the end of the week.\n\nWe can use should in questions that are offers or that request confirmation or advice:\n\n© Should I phone for a taxi for you? © Who should I pass the message to?\nNote that in sentences like these we can also use shall with a very similar meaning, and ought\nis also used in questions, although less commonly.\n\nCompare the use of shall and should in sentences such as the following, where ‘I shall’ means ‘IT\nintend to’ and ‘I should’ means ‘I ought to’:\n\n0 I shall read the script on the train tomorrow. (or I'll read...)\n\n© I should read the script on the train tomorrow but I know that I'll be too tired.\n\nWe can use had better instead of should/ought to, especially in spoken English, to say that we\nthink it is a good idea to do something:\nIf you're not well, you’d better ask Ann to go instead. (or... you should/ought to...)\nalthough we don’t use it to talk about the past or to make general comments:\n= You should/ought to have caught a later train. (not You had better have caught...)\n© I don’t think parents should/ought to give their children sweets. (not I don’t think pare\nhad better give their children sweets.)\n\nWe prefer had better if we want to express particular urgency and in demands and threats:\n© There’s someone moving about downsrs. We'd better call the police, quickly.\n\nNotice that the negative form is had better not. In question forms the subject comes after had,\nalthough many people avoid questions with had better:\n\n(1 He'd better not be late again or he'll be in trouble.\n\n© Hadn't we better get a taxi? (or Shouldn't we get...?)\n\nGrammar review: should and ought to — C29-C32\n\nExercises Unit 20\n\n20.1 Complete these sentences with should/ought to + infinitive (active), should/ought to be + past\nparticiple (passive), or should/ought to have + past participle using each of these verbs once\nonly. () She seemed unable to concentrate. © The house became Peter's in 1980.\n\nThe most common linking verb is be. Others are to do with ‘being’, e.g. keep, prove, remain,\nstay; ‘becoming’, e.g. become, come, end up, grow, turn out; and ‘seeming’, e.g. appear, look,\nseem, sound. Most of these verbs can be followed by either an adjective or noun phrase (e.g. It\nsounds nice/a nice place), However, when they are used as linking verbs, come and grow (e.g.\ncome to know, grow thoughtful) can’t be followed by a noun phrase, and keep is only followed\nby a noun if an adjective follows it (e.g. It kept him awake).\n\nAfter the verbs appear (= seems true), look (= seem), prove, seem, and turn out we can often\neither include or omit to be:\n\nOC The room appears (to be) brighter than when I last saw it.\n\nO She proved (to be) an extremely enthusiastic teacher.\nHowever, following these verbs to be is usually included before the adjectives alive, alone,\nasleep, and awake, and before the -ing forms of verbs:\n\nO I didn’t go in because she appeared to be asleep. (not ...she appeared asleep.)\n\nO The roads seem to be getting icy so drive carefully. (not The roads seem getting...)\n\nBefore a noun we include to be when the noun tells us what the subject is, but can often leave it\nout when we give our opinion of the person or thing in the subject. We tend to leave out to be\nin more formal English. Compare:\n\nG He walked into what seemed to be a cave. (not ...what seemed a cave.) and\n\n© She seems (to be) a very efficient salesperson.\n\nWe use the linking verb become to describe a process of change. A number of other linking\nverbs can be used instead of become, including come, get, go, grow, turn (into).\n\nWe use get rather than become: in informal speech and writing before difficult, ill, interested,\npregnant, suspicious, unhappy, and worried; in imperatives; and in phrases such as get changed\n(clothes), get dressed, get married/divorced:\nOI first got suspicious when he looked into all the cars. (more formally ...became suspicious...)\n© Don’t get upset about it! © Where did you live before you got married?\n\nWe prefer become to talk about a more abstract or technical process of change with words such\nas adapted, apparent, aware, convinced, infected, irrelevant, obvious, and recognised:\n© He became recognised as an expert. O Their bodies have become adapted to high altitudes.\n\nWe use become, not get, if there is a noun phrase after the linking verb:\nO Dr Smith became an adviser to the government. © She became a good tennis player.\n\nWe use go or turn, not usually get or become, when we talk about colours changing:\nO The traffic lights turned/went green and I pulled away.\n\nWe often use go to talk about changes, particularly to unwanted situations. We use go, not turn\nor get, with deaf, blind, bald, or to say that someone behaves in a mad or excited way; and also\nwith go bad/off/mouldy/rotten (about old food), go bust (= a company closes because it has run\nout of money), go dead (= when a telephone stops working), go missing, and go wrong:\n\n© The children went completely crazy at the party. | © My computer’s gone wrong again.\nNotice, however, some common exceptions: get ill, get old, get tired.\n\nAfter the verbs come, get, and grow (but not after become) we can use a to-infinitive. Come and\ngrow are often used to talk about gradual change:\nOC Leventually came/grew to appreciate his work. (not ...became to appreciate his work.)\nDI soon got to know their names. (not ...became to know their names.)\n\nExercises Unit 21\n\n2a\n\n21.2\n\n21.3\n\nPut brackets around to be in these sentences if it can be left out. By 8.30 the\ntaxi hadn’t arrived and I was starting (8) to become worried. It was (9) getting obvious that we\nwere going to miss our plane if we didn’t leave soon. These verbs have two corresponding passives:\nactive pattern: V+IO+DO/ V+DO+prep+I0 Passive —\n\n| Alice gave us that vase. / We were given that vase (by Alice). /\nAlice gave that vase to us. / That vase was given (to) us (by Alice). /\n\nThe passive form you choose depends on which is more appropriate in a particular context. If\nwe specify an agent (see Appendix 1), this follows by at the end of the clause. Note that in\ninformal contexts ‘to’ can be left out in the second passive pattern. Many of these verbs are to\ndo with ‘giving’, e.g. award, hand, lend, offer, send, throw, and ‘telling’ e.g. ask, read, teach.\n\nVerbs that can’t be followed by! 10+ DO ‘in the active have only one. of these passive forms:\n\nactive pattern: V + DO + prep + 10 | passive\nHeexplained-methe-problem-X Tt was explained the- problem: x\n\nHe explained the problem to me. / | The problem was explained to me. 7\n\nMany of these verbs are to do with reporting what was said or thought, including announce,\ndemonstrate, describe, introduce, mention, propose, report, suggest.\n\nVerbs followed by object + complement (see Glossary) in the active have one passive form:\n\nactive pattern: V + object + complement | Passive\n\nThey elected her president _ She was elected president.\n\nOther verbs like this are to do wii h giving someone a precnine position, e.g. appoint, declare,\nmake, nominate, vote, and ‘naming’, ¢.g. call, name, title.\n\nSome verbs that are followed by object + bare infinitive (= an infinitive without ‘to’) in the\nactive are followed by a to-infinitive in the passive.\n\n| active pattern: V + object + bare infinitive passive\n\n| They have made him return the money. He has been made to return the money.\n\nOther verbs like this include feel, hear, help (‘help’ can also be followed by V + object +\nto-infinitive in the active), observe, see (see also Unit 23).\n\nWe can make a passive form of many transitive two- and three-word verbs (see also Unit 94),\n\nactive passive\n\nKathy looks after him He is looked after (by Kathy)\nThey put the accident down to bad luck. (ot A problem was come up against)\nor only used in the passive in certain senses when it may not be important to mention the\nsubject (e.g. call (someone) up (= ordered to join the army, etc., passive possible; = telephone,\nno passive), call (someone) back (= ask to return, passive possible; = telephone, no passive), let\nin (= allow into a place, passive possible; = allow rain, etc. in, no passive), let out (= allow to\nleave, passive possible; = let out a sound, no passive), put out (= put out a statemenvlight/fire,\npassive possible; = put out a hand/arm/foovtongue, no passive):\n\nOI put out a hand to steady myself. (= passive)\n\nOther verbs in this pattern include bring, catch, hear, find, keep, notice, observe, send, show:\nEveryone was brought running into the room by her screams.\nin the security video the burglars are seen entering the bank through a window.\n\nSome verbs that can be followed by an -ing form can be used with a passive form being + past\nparticiple:\n\nO I really love being given presents.\n\nO The children enjoyed being taken to the zoo.\n\nOther verbs like this include avoid, deny, describe, dislike, face, hate, (not) imagine, like,\nremember, report, resent.\nVerbs which in the active are followed by an object consisting of a noun phrase and -ing clause\n\nusually have no passive:\n© I dread him (or his) finding out. (but not He is dreaded finding out)\n\nOther verbs like this include anticipate, appreciate, dislike, forget, hate, imagine, like, (not)\nmind, recall, remember.\n\nActive patterns with verb + to-infinitive\n\nThe active pattern verb + object + to-infinitive is made passive with ‘be’ + past participle + to-\ninfinitive. Compare:\n\nO Mr Price has taught Peter to sing for many years. and\n\nCO Peter has been taught to sing (by Mr Price) for many years.\n\nOther verbs in this pattern include advise, allow, ask, believe, consider, expect, feel, instruct,\nmean, order, require, tell, understand.\n\nNotice that in some contexts it is possible to make both verbs passive:\nChanges to the taxation system are expected to be proposed. (compare the active We\nexpect the government to propose changes to the taxation system.)\n\nSome verbs followed by an object + to-infinitive in the active have no passive:\n© Susan liked Tom to be there. (but not Tom was liked to be there.)\n\nVerbs like this are to do with ‘liking’ and ‘wanting’, and include (can’t) bear, hate, love, need,\nprefer, want, wish.\n\nThe active pattern verb + to-infinitive + object is made passive with verb + to be + past\nparticiple. Compare:\n\n© Supermarkets started to sell fresh pasta only in the 1990s. and\n\nO Fresh pasta started to be sold by supermarkets only in the 1990s.\n\nOther verbs in this pattern include appear, begin, come, continue, seem, tend; also agree, aim,\narrange, attempt, hope, refuse, want. The verbs in the first group (and start) have corresponding\nmeanings in active and passive sentences, but the verbs in the second group do not. Compare:\n\nOC People have come to regard her as the leading violinist of her generation, (active)\n\ncorresponds to\n© She has come to be regarded as the leading violinist of her generation. (passive)\n>) Mr Smith wanted to help me. (active) does not correspond to\nO I wanted to be helped by Mr Smith. (passive)\n\nExercises Unit 23\n\n23.1 Complete these sentences using one of these pairs of verbs. Use either was/were + past\nparticiple + -ing or past simple + being + past participle. Consequently, we prefer\npassives when the agent:\n- is not known: © My office was broken into when I was on holiday.\n- is ‘people in general’: © An order form can be found on page 2.\n~ is unimportant: © He is thought to be somewhere in Russia.\n= is obvious: © She is being treated in hospital. (the agent is clearly ‘medical staff’)\n\n¥ In factual writing, particularly in describing procedures or processes, we often wish to omit\nthe agent, and use passives:\n© Nuclear waste will still be radioactive even after 20,000 years, so it must be disposed of\nvery carefully. It can be stored as a liquid in stainless-steel containers which are encased\nin concrete. The most dangerous nuclear waste can be turned into glass. It is planned to\nstore this glass in deep underground mines.\n\n* In informal contexts, particularly in conversation, we often use active sentences with a\nsubject such as people, somebody/someone, something, they, we, or you even when we do\nnot know who the agent is. In more formal contexts, we often prefer to use a passive so\nthat we can avoid any mention of an agent. Compare:\n\nC Somebody will give you the questions a week before the exam. and\n\n© You will be given the questions a week before the exam. (or The questions will be given\nto you...) (both more formal)\n\n© They’re installing the new computer system next month. and\n\n© The new computer system is being installed next month. (more formal)\n\nNotice also that some verbs have related nouns which express the same meaning. These nouns\ncan be used as the subject of passive sentences, with a new passive verb introduced. Compare\nthe example above and:\n\nO The installation of the new computer system will be completed by next month.\n\nIn English we usually prefer to put the topic (what is already being talked about) at the\nbeginning of a sentence (or clause) and a comment on that topic at the end. Choosing the\npassive often allows us to do this. Compare these two texts and notice where the topic (in\nitalics) is placed in the second sentence of each. The second text uses a passive where the\nemphasis is on the equipment:\nG The three machines tested for the report contained different types of safety valve. The\nBoron Group in Germany manufactured all the equipment.\nO The three machines tested for the report contained different types of safety valve. All the\nequipment was manufactured by the Boron Group in Germany.\n\nIt is often more natural to put long subjects at the end of a sentence. Using the passive allows us\nto do this. So, for example:\n\n© I was surprised by Don's decision to give up bis job and move to Sydney.\nis more natural than ‘Don's decision to give up his job and move to Sydney surprised me’,\nalthough the choice can depend on considerations of style and context.\n\nInstead of making a that-clause the subject of a passive sentence, it is normal to use an it-clause\n(see also Unit 25):\n\n© Everybody believed (that) the plan would fail. (active)\n\nCG It was believed that the plan would fail. (passive) is more natural than\n\n© That the plan would fail was believed by everybody. (passive)\n\nGrammar review: —> Section D & Appendix 1\n\nExercises Unit 24\n\n24.1\n\n24.2\n\nRewrite these sentences. Using this pattern can allow\nto put important information at the end of the sentence (see Unit 24C):\n0 It is reported that the damage is extensive. Compare:\nO It is thought (that) there are too many obstacles to peace. and\nO There are thought to be too many obstacles to peace.\nOC In 1981 it was believed (that) there were only two experts on the disease in the country.\nand\nO In 1981 there were believed to be only two experts on the disease in the country.\n\nWe can use the same verbs in this pattern as with subject + passive verb + to-infinitive (see C).\n\nExercises Unit 25\n\n25.1\n\n25.2\n\n25.3\n\nWhich of the verbs can complete the sentence? CO Who was her father?\n\nWhom is used as a formal alternative to who as object, and also directly after prepositions:\n© Whom did you meet? © To whom were you talking?\n\nWhich is used to refer to people when we want to identify somebody in a group (for example,\nin a crowded room or on a photograph):\n\nG ‘Which is your brother?’ ‘The one next to Ken.’\nand we can use which instead of who to talk about particular classes of people:\n\n© Which do you think earns more, a teacher or a police officer? (or Who do you think...?)\n\nWe usually use which, rather than who or what, in questions before one(s) and of, as which is\ncommonly used to ask or talk about a choice between one or more things:\n\n2 I’ve decided to buy one of these sweaters. Which one do you think I should choose?\n\n© Which of you would like to go first? (rather than Who of...?)\n\nWhen we use who or what as subjects, the verb that follows is usually singular, even if a plural\nanswer is expected:\n2 Who wants a cup of coffee? (said to a number of people; not Who want a cup of coffee?)\n2 Whar is there to do in Birmingham at Christmas? (expects an answer giving a number of\nthings to do; not What are there to do in Birmingham at Christmas?)\n\nHowever, the verb can be plural in echo questions (see Unit 27E) after a plural subject or a\nsubject consisting of two or more noun phrases joined by and:\n\nCo ‘Mr Smith and his family are here to see you.’ ‘Who are here?’ (or Who's here?)\nand when who and what function as complements:\n\n0 Who are those people over there? ) What are the consequences of the decision?\n\nWe use how, not what, to ask —\n% a general opinion on something: © How was the journey?\n% about general health: (0 How is your brother?\n+r about preferences relating to food and drink: | © How do you like your coffee?\n\nWe use what, not how, to ask -—\n\nx a general opinion on something with What...like? © What was the journey like?\n\nvx for details with What... like/hate (etc.) about...? © What do you like about the job?\n¥ about the consequences of something with What if...? © What if your plan doesn’t work?\n% about the naming of something in the question © What's it called?\n\nWe use either what or how —\nx to make a suggestion with What/How about...? ) How/What about having a swim?\n+ to ask for more information in the question How/What do you mean?\n© ‘There’s something wrong with the car.’ ‘Something wrong? How/What do you mean?’\n\nWe can use whose to ask about the person that owns or is responsible for something. Whose\ncan be used either before a verb (as a pronoun):\n© Whose are these boots?\nor before a noun or noun phrase (as a determiner) introducing direct or indirect questions:\n© Whose boots are these? G She asked me whose coat I was wearing.\nIn formal contexts we can use a preposition before whose (see also Unit 55B):\n2 In whose desk was it found? (less formally Whose desk was it found in?)\nHowever, in questions without a verb a preposition comes before whose:\n(‘We're meeting at nine.’ ‘In whose house?’ (not Whose house in?)\n\nGrammar review: questions —> Section E & Appendix 2\n\nExercises Unit 26\n\n26.1 Underline the correct word. This happens particularly in yes/no rather than wh-questions:\n0 Did she not realise that she'd broken it? (less emphatically Didn’t she realise that...?)\n© Can you not get there a bit earlier? (less emphatically Can't you...?)\n\nWe sometimes use negative words other than -n't such as never, no, nobody, nothing, nowhere:\nCO Why do you never help? © Have you nowhere to go? (or Do you have nowhere to go?)\nor less emphatically or more informally:\nU Why don’t you ever help? 0 Haven't you got anywhere to go? (or Don't you have anywhere...\n\nWe can make a suggestion with Why not + verb or Why don't/doesn'’t... (but not Why do\nnot/does not...):\n\nO Why not decorate the house yourself? (or Why don’t you decorate...?)\n\n© Why not give her what she wants?’ (or Why don't we give her ...?)\n\nWhy didn’t... isn’t used to make a suggestion, but can show that we think an action was wrong.\nFor example, depending on intonation and context, it can be used to criticise someone:\n\nCO Why didn’t you decorate the house yourself? (I think you should have done)\n\n0 Why didn’t you tell me that in the first place? (I’m annoyed that you didn’t)\n\nNegative question forms are used in exclamations giving opinions:\n\n) Haven't you growa! © Doesn't she look Tovely! © Didn't it snow wat!\n\nExclamations like this are usually said with a falling intonation.\n\nEcho questions\n\nEcho questions are used when we haven't understood what has been said or to check that we\nheard correctly, perhaps because we found it very surprising. We might repeat, usually with a\nrising intonation, the whole of what was said:\n(1) ‘Jane’s lost her job.’ ‘Jane’s lost her job?”\nor focus on part of what was said using a stressed wh-word or a phrase with how:\n1 ‘Tom's arriving at 6.30.’ ‘When's Tom aseiviig?/Tom' 's arriving\n1 ‘We paid £3,000 for the painting.’ ‘How much ch did you-pay?/You rie how much?\n\nWe can use what or ‘do’ what to focus on the verb or part of the sentence beginning with the verb:\n© ‘We paid £3,000 for the painting.’ ‘You. it?’ (or ‘You did what?’)\nCO ‘I think she’s having a sleep.’ ‘She’s t?” (or ‘She’s doing what?”\n\nQuestions with that-clauses\n\nA wh-question can refer to a following that-clause, particularly after verbs such as expect, hope,\nreckon, say, suggest, suppose, and think. We can leave out that in these questions:\n\nCO When do you reckon (that) you'll finish the job?\n\nCO Why did they suggest (that) we should buy new computers for the library?\nHowever, when the wh-word is the subject, object or complement of the verb in the subordinate\nclause, we do not use that:\n\n© What did you think was in the box? (not What did you think that was in the box?)\n\n© Who do you suppose did it? (not Who do you suppose that did it?)\n\nGrammar review: questions — Section E & Appendix 2\n\nExercises Unit 27\n\n27.1\n\n27.2\n\n273\n\n27.4\n\nWrite an appropriate negative question for each situation, using -n't in your answer. Compare:\n\n2D She closed the door. (transitive) and The door closed. (intransitive)\n\n0 I’ve ripped my shirt. (transitive) and My shirt has ripped. (intransitive)\n\nVerbs like this are often used to talk about some kind of change. Other examples are begin,\nbend, break, burn, change, decrease, drop, finish, increase, move, open, shut, start, vary, wake.\n\nSome transitive verbs don’t need an object when the meaning is clear from the context:\n© He has smoked (cigarettes) since he was 10.\n© She plays (the saxophone) beautifully.\n\nOther verbs like this include answer, ask, change, cook, dance, drink, drive, eat, fail, park,\nphone, read, sing, study, wash, wash up, wave, win, write.\n\nAfter some verbs we usually add a complement - a phrase which completes the meaning of a\nverb, noun or adjective - which is an adverb or prepositional phrase:\n© The disease originated in Britain. (not The disease originated. We need to add something\nabout where or how it originated.)\n\nOther verbs commonly have a complement but may not. Compare:\n0D He paused for a few moments. and He paused. (no complement needed)\n\nSome verbs are commonly followed by a particular preposition or prepositions and then an\nobject (see also Unit 94):\n\n(2 We had to deal with hundreds of complaints. (not We had to deal.)\n\n© I'm sure that blue car belongs to Matthew. (not I'm sure that blue car belongs.)\n\nHere are some more verbs with the prepositions which usually follow them: adhere to, aspire\nto, culminate in/with, detract from, differentiate between, incline to/towards, specialise in.\n\nSome verbs are usually followed by an object + prepositional phrase complement:\n© Lalways associate pizza with Italy. _ (not | always associate pizza.)\n© She put the report on the floor. (not She put the report.)\n\nHere are some more verbs with the prepositions which usually begin the complement: attribute\n«--to, base...on/upon, equate...with, inflict...on, mistake...for, regard...as/with, remind...of.\n\nSome verbs are often followed by an object + adjective (or adjective phrase) complement:\nG The people of this country will hold the government responsible.\n© Beckman pronounced himself fit for the match.\n\nOther verbs that can be followed by an object + adjective complement include assume, believe,\nconsider, declare, find, judge, prove, report, think. The object after declare, find, pronounce and\nprove is often a reflexive pronoun (himself, etc.).\n\nNotice that when these verbs are used with an object + adjective complement the sentence is\nusually rather formal. Less formal alternatives can be made by adding to be after the object or\nby using a that-clause:\n0 Dr Adams argues that house prices will fall, but other economists believe the opposite true.\n(or less formally ...believe the opposite to be true. or ...believe that the opposite is true.)\n\nNote that many of the verbs in this unit can be followed by a number of patterns, sometimes\nassociated with different meanings. Compare, for example:\n© She found her ring. and ( She found herself in an embarrassing situation.\n\nGrammar review: transitive and intransitive verbs —* F1-F3\n\nExercises Unit 28\n\n28.1 If it is possible to leave out the object (in italics) after the verbs (underlined), put brackets\naround it, as in 1. Usually the first object (= the Indirect Object (10)\nis a person or group of people and the second object (= the Direct Object (DO)) is a thing:\n© Can you bring me (= 1O) some milk (= DO) from the shops?\n1 Tread Suzanne (= IO) a story (= DO).\n) He made himself (= 10) a cup of coffee. (= DO).\n\nMany verbs that can have two objects may also be used with a DO only (e.g. I read a story).\n\nWith many verbs that can have two objects, it is possible to reverse the order of the objects if\nwe put for or to before the IO (this is then called a prepositional object). Compare:\n\n© I built my daughter a doll’s house. and © I built a doll’s house for my daughter.\n\n© Can you pass me that bandage? and © Can you pass that bandage to me?\n\nWe often use this pattern if we want to focus particular attention on the object after for/to.\nalso use it if the IO is a lot longer than the DO:\n) Jasmin taught music to a large number of children at the school. (not Jasmin taught a\nlarge number of children at the school music.)\n\nWe use for + object with verbs such as book, build, buy, catch, choose, cook, fetch, find, get,\nmake, order, pour, save. For suggests that the IO receives and benefits from goods or services.\nWe use to + object with verbs such as award, give, hand, lend, offer, owe, pass, show, teach,\ntell, throw. To suggests a transfer of the DO to the IO.\n\nIf the DO is a pronoun, a pattern with DO + preposition + IO is usual. Patterns without a\npreposition are avoided because they are considered to be bad style:\n\n© I gave them to Tim, (rather than | gave Tim them./ gave them Tim.)\n\nUO We bought it for them. (rather than We bought them it./We bought it them.)\n\nThe verbs bring, leave, pay, play, post, read, sell, send, sing, take, and write can be used with\neither for or to. Often there is a difference in meaning: to suggests that there is a transfer of\nsomething to someone, and for suggests that someone benefits from something. Compare:\n\nO Thadn’t got time to visit Ann, so I wrote a letter to her. and\n\n© Ann had broken her wrist and couldn’t hold a pen, so I wrote a letter for her.\nSometimes, however, the meaning is very similar:\n\n© He played the piece to (or for) me. © Can you sing that song again to (or for)\n\nNotice that when object + object is used after these verbs it usually has a similar meaning to\nverb with object + to + object. For example:\nG I sold him the car. (means I sold the car to him, not I sold the car for him.)\n\nSome verbs that are followed by two objects cannot have their objects reversed with for/to:\n© We all envied him his lifestyle. (but not We all envied his lifestyle for/to him.)\nOther verbs like this include allow, ask, cost, deny, forgive, guarantee, permit, refuse.\n\nSome verbs can only have a second object if this is a prepositional object with to (see also Uni\n22A), Compare:\n\nO She described the situation. or\n\nO She described the situation to me. (but not She described me the situation.) and\n\nO She told this joke. or She told this joke to me. or She told me this joke.\nOther verbs like describe include admit, announce, demonstrate, explain, introduce, mention,\npoint out, prove, report, say, suggest.\n\nSome verbs can only have a second object if this is a prepositional object with for. Compare:\n© He fixed the tap. or O He fixed the tap for me. (but not He fixed me the tap.) and\n© I booked a room. or © I booked a room for her. or © I booked her a room.\n\nOther verbs like fix include collect, mend, and repair.\n\nExercises Unit 29\n\n29.1\n\n29.2\n\n29.3\n\nComplete these sentences with a suitable form of one of the following verbs and cither to or\nfor. (but not ...the horse's winning...)\n\nSome verbs can be followed by to + -ing where to is a preposition:\n\n© She confessed to stealing the money. © You don't object to working late tonight, do you?\nOther verbs like this include adapt, adjust, admit, look forward, own up, resort. Note that these\nverbs can also be followed by to + noun phrase:\n\n© She confessed to the crime. G You don’t object to the work, do you?\n\nOther verbs can be followed by different prepositions + -ing. For example:\n\nwr by + -ing (begin, close, end, finish (off/up), open, start (off/out))\n© Can you begin by cleaning the floors, and then do the windows?\n\nx on + -ing or on + object+ -ing (concentrate, count, depend, focus, insist, rely)\nO Clare insisted on (Jack) wearing a suit to the party.\n\n+ of + -ing or of + object + -ing (approve, hear, know, speak, talk, tell)\nO I don’t approve of (them/ their) bunting animals for sport.\n\n+r object + from + -ing (deter, discourage, keep, prevent, prohibit, stop)\n0 The noise from next door prevented me from sleeping.\n\nSome verbs (feel, hear, notice, observe, overhear, see, watch) can be followed by an object and\nthen either by an -ing form or a bare infinitive, but their meanings may be slightly different. An\n-ing form suggests that an action is in progress, while a bare infinitive suggests a completed\naction. Compare:\n\nO I saw them playing football from my window. and 0 I saw him smash the bottle.\n\nAlso, an -ing form can suggest that we watch, hear, etc. some of an action, but not from start to\nfinish, while a bare infinitive suggests that we watch, hear, etc. the whole action from start to\nfinish. Compare:\n\nO I was able to watch them building the new car park from my office window. and\n\n© I watched him climb through the window, and then I called the police.\n\nAfter the verbs dare and help we can use either a bare infinitive or to-infinitive:\n© 1 was angry with him, but I didn’t dare (to) say anything.\n1 We hope the poster campaign will help (to) raise awareness of the problem.\nWhen dare has an object, we can only use a to-infinitive. Compare:\n© I dared him to cross the river. (not | dared him cross...) and © [helped them (to) pack.\n\nAfter have, let and make we can use an object + bare infinitive but not to-infinitive:\nD His exam results might make bin work harder.
