(not We've decided us to leave early.)\n\nAfter some verbs, including apply, arrange, ask, campaign, plan, and wait, we have to put a\npreposition, usually for, immediately after the verb before an object + to-infinitive (see also\nUnit 29):\n\n© We waited for the taxi to come before saying goodbye. (not We waited the taxi\n\nto come...) .\n\nO They arranged for Jane to stay in London. (not They arranged Jane to stay...)\nAfter apply, ask and campaign, the to-infinitive is often passive:\n©) They applied for the court appearance to be postponed.\n\nOther verbs can be followed by different prepositions + object + to-infinitive. For example:\n\n* at + object + to-infinitive (go on (= to criticise continually), keep on (= to talk about\nsomething many times), scream, yell)\n© I shouted at the man to open the door.\n\n* on + object + to-infinitive (call (= to officially ask someone to do something), count, prevail, rely)\nO We're depending on you to find a solution soon.\n\n* to + object + to-infinitive (appeal, gesture, motion)\n© He closed the door and signalled to the pilot to take off.\n\nA number of other to-infinitive and -ing forms can also follow verbs -\nw verb + negative to-infinitive and negative -ing forms:\n© We decided not to go to Paris after all. (compare The people didn’t decide to go to war, it\nwas their political leaders.)\n© Some of my friends have considered not going to college because of the cost. (compare I\nhaven’t considered going to college - | don’t want to go on studying after school.)\n\nx verb + to have + past participle. Compare:\n© Can you hear that strange noise? It seems to happen every time I turn on the tap. and\n(0 The accident seems to have happened at around 1.00 p.m. yesterday.\nThis form is often used to give an opinion (with verbs like seem and appear) about a past\nevent, or to report what is or was said (with passive verbs like is/was alleged, believed, said,\nthought) about past events:\n(© Simons is alleged to have assaulted a police officer.\n\n% verb + having + past participle. The verb + -ing and verb + having + past participle forms\nhave a similar meaning with these verbs. Compare:\n\n© I now regret buying the car. and Cc I now regret having bought the car.\nThis form is most often used with the verbs admit, deny, forget, recall, regret and\nremember.\n\nGrammar review: — F4-F13\n\n-XErcises Unit\n\n31.1 Choose one of the verbs in brackets to complete cach sentence. When we do this\nwe can use sentences that have a reporting clause and a reported clause (see also Units 33 to 39):\n\nreporting clause reported clause\n\nShe explained (that) she couldn’t take the job until January.\nHe didn’t ask me where to put the boxes.\nIf their exact words are important or if we want to create some dramatic effect, we might report\ntheir actual words. In writing this is done in a quotation (see also Appendix 3):\n‘I suppose you've heard the latest news,’ she said.\n© ‘Of course,’ Carter replied, ‘you'll have to pay him to do the job.’\nThe reporting clause can come before, within, or at the end of the quotation.\n\nIn the English used in stories and novels, the reporting verb (e.g. say, reply, think) is often\nplaced before the subject when the reporting clause comes after the quotation:\n(0 ‘When will you be back?’ asked Arnold. (or ...Arnold asked.)\nHowever, we don’t use this order when the subject is a pronoun (except in a literary style):\nQ ‘And after that I moved to Italy,’ she continued. (not ...continued she.)\n\nNegatives in reporting\n\nTo report what somebody didn’t say or think, we make the reporting verb negative:\n© He didn’t tell me how he would get to London.\n\nIf we want to report a negative sentence, then we usually report this in the reported clause:\n© ‘You're right, it isn’t a good idea.’ + He agreed that it wasn’t a good idea.\nalthough it may be reported in the reporting clause, depending on meaning:\n© ‘I disagree. It’s not a good idea at all.’ - He didn’t agree that it was a good idea.\nHowever, with some verbs, to report a negative sentence we usually make the verb in the\nreporting clause negative:\n© ‘Lexpect he won't come.’/ ‘I don’t expect he will come.’ - She didn’t expect him to coi\n(rather than She expected he wouldn't come.)\nOther verbs like this include believe, feel, intend, plan, propose, suppose, think, want.\n\nReporting questions\n\nTo report a wh-question we use a reporting clause and a clause with a wh-word:\nC She asked me what the problem was. ) Lasked him where to go next.\n\nWhen we report a yes/no question we use a reporting clause followed by a clause beginning\nwith either if or whether (but note that we can’t use if + to-infinitive; see Unit 34):\n© Liz wanted to know if/whether we had any photos of our holiday.\n\nThe usual word order in a wh-, if-, or whether-clause is the one we would use in a statement:\nG ‘Have you seen Paul recently?’ > She wanted to know if I had seen Paul recently.\nHowever, if the original question begins what, which, or who followed by be + complement\ncan put the complement before or after be in the report:\n0 ‘Who was the winner?’ — I asked who the winner was. (or ...who was the winner.)\n\nNotice that we don’t use a form of do in the wh-, if-, or whether-clause:\n\nO She asked me where I found it. (not ...where did I find it/...where I did find it.)\nHowever, if we are reporting a negative question, we can use a negative form of do:\n\n2 He asked (me) why I didn’t want anything to eat.\n\nGrammar review — G1-G7 & Appendix 3\n\nises Unit 32\n\nReport what was said, quoting the speaker's exact words with one of the following reporting\nverbs, as in 1. Less formally we can use a to-infinitive\nclause after ask and require (e.g. The club asks its members to pay their fees by 31\"\nDecember). However, we can’t use a fo-infinitive clause after demand (not The company\ndemands its staff to...}.\n\nGrammar review —> G1-G7\n\nercises Unit 33\n\n1 Underline the correct verb. The verbs ask and show often\n\nhave an object before a wh-clause, but not always:\n© Lasked (him) how I could get to the station, and he told me.\n\nThese verbs can also be followed by object + wh-word + to-infinitive:\n© She taught me how to play chess. © I showed her where to put her coat.\n\nWe can often use the way instead of how referring to either the route or the means:\n© Go back the way (thavby which) you came. (or informally Go back how you came.)\n© Have you noticed the way (that/in which) he spins the ball? (or ...noticed how he spins...?)\n\nNotice that we don’t use ‘the way how’. (e.g. not ‘Go back the way how you came’.)\n\nWhether\n\nWe can use whether as the wh-word in a wh-clause when we want to show possible choices.\nWhether has a similar meaning to ‘if? (see Unit 86);\n© He couldn't remember whether/if he had turned the computer off.\n\nWhether is commonly followed by a to-infinitive to talk about the choice between two or more\npossibilities. Notice that ‘if is never used before a to-infinitive:\n© You have 14 days to decide whether to keep it or not. (not ...to decide if to keep it...)\n\nVerbs that are often followed by whether + to-infinitive are concerned with talking or thinking\nabout choices, and include choose, consider, debate, decide, determine, discuss, know (in\n\nquestions and negatives), wonder. Some other verbs to do with talking or thinking are not used\nwith whether + to-infinitive, including ask, conclude, explain, imagine, realise, speculate, think.\n\nNotice the difference between these pairs of sentences. The first in each pair has a wh-clause\nwith whether and the second has a that-clause (see Unit 33):\n© I didn’t know whether the shop was shut. (the wh-clause says what\nthe choice is)\n(1 They decided that the airport should be closed. (the that-clause says what was decided)\n\nIn rather formal contexts, particularly in writing, we can use as to with a meaning similar to\n‘about’ or ‘concerning’ before a wh-clause. This is most common before whether:\n© Opinion was divided as to whether the findings from the study were representative of the\npopulation as a whole. I did it well before the wedding.’ + She reassured me that\nshe sent out the invitations well before the wedding. (or...had sent...)\n\nWhen the situation described in the reported clause is a permanent/habitual situation, or still\nexists or is relevant at the time we are reporting it, then we use a present tense (or present\nperfect) if we also use a present tense for the verb in the reporting clause:\n© Dr Weir thinks that he spends about 5 minutes on a typical appointment with a patient.\n© US scientists claim that they have developed a new vaccine against malaria.\nNote that the present perfect focuses attention on the result of the action, not the action itself.\n\nHowever, when we use a past tense in the reporting clause we can use either a present or past\ntense (or present perfect or past perfect) in the reported clause:\n\n© She argued that Carl is/was the best person for the job.\n\n© They noted that the rate of inflation has/had slowed down.\nChoosing a present tense (or present perfect) in the reported clause emphasises that the situation\nbeing reported still exists or is still relevant when we report it.\n\nIf we want to show we are not sure that what we are reporting is necessarily true, or that\na situation may not still exist now, we prefer a past rather than a present tense. Compare:\n© Sarah told me that she has two houses. (= might suggest that this is true) and\nO Sarah told me that she had two houses. (= might suggest either that this is perhaps not\ntrue, or that she once had two houses but doesn’t have two houses now)\n\nVerb tense in the reporting clause\n\nWhen we report something that was said or thought in the past, the verb in the reporting clause\nis often in a past tense:\n© Just before her wedding, she revealed that she had been married before.\n\nWhen we report current news, opinions, etc. we can use a present tense for the verb in the\nreporting clause. In some cases, either a present or past tense is possible, although we prefer a\npresent tense to emphasise that what was said is true or still relevant when we report it:\n\n© She says that she’ll have to close the shop unless business improves. (or ...said...)\n\nWe often prefer a present rather than a past tense —\n+ to report information that we have been told or heard, but don’t know whether it is true:\n) [hear you’re unhappy with your job.\n* to report what is said by some authority:\n© The law says that no-one under the age of 16 can buy a lottery ticket.\n® to report what many people say:\nC Every teacher I’ve spoken to tells me that standards of spelling are in decline.\n\nGrammar review —> G1-G7\n\nUnit 35\n\n1 Change the sentences into reported speech. The changes are summarised here:\n\n| modal verb in original\n\nca\n\ncould, would, should, might,\nneedn't, ought to, used to,\ncould have, should have, etc\n\nwill, can, may\n\nmodal verb in report\n\n\" could, would, should, might,\n| needn't, ought to, used to,\n\n(i.e. no change)\ncould have, should have, etc.\n\nwould, could, might\n\nwill, can, may (existing or future situations and\npresent tense verb in reporting clause)\n\nwill or would, can or could, may or might\n(existing or future situations and past tense verb in\nreporting clause)\n\n| shall | would, should (offers, suggestions, etc.)\n\nmust (= necessity) | mrustor had:to\nmust (= conclude; see Unit 18B) | must\n| mustn’t mustn't\n\nWe sometimes use a modal verb in a report when there is no modal verb in the original:\n) *You’re not allowed to smoke here.’ — She told me that I mustn’t smoke there.\n© ‘My advice is to look for a new job now.’ -> She said that I should look for a new job now.\n\nThe verbs could, would, should, might, needn't, ought to, used to, and could have, should have,\netc. don’t change in the report:\n\n5 ‘LT could meet you at the airport.’ -* He said that he could meet us at the airport.\n\n© ‘You should have contacted me earlier.’ —+ She said I should have contacted her earlier.\n\nWill usually changes to would, can to could, and may to might. However, if the situation we\nreporting still exists or is still in the future and the verb in the reporting clause has a present\ntense, we use will, can, and may in the reported clause (see Unit 32). Compare:\n\n‘I'll be in Paris at Christmas.’ -» She tells me she’ll be in Paris at Christmas. and\n\nCD ‘Careful! You'll fall through the ice!” + 1 warned him he would fall through the ice.\n\nIf the situation we are reporting still exists or is still in the future and the verb in the reporting\nclause has a past tense, then we can use either would or will, can or could, or may or might in\nthe reported clause:\n\n0 ‘The problem can be solved.’ -> They said the problem can/could be solved.\n\nWhen shall is used in the original to talk about the future, we use would in the report:\n\nO ‘I shall (I'll) call you on Monday.’ -* She told me she would call me on Monday.\nHowever, when shall is used in offers, requests for advice and confirmation, etc. then we can\nshould in the report, but not shall or would:\n\n© ‘Where shall [ put this box?’ + He asked where he should put the box.\n\nWhen must is used in the original to say that it is necessary to do something, we can usually\neither must or had to in the report, although had to is more natural in speech:\n\n© ‘You must be home by 9 o'clock.’ =» She said I must/had to be home by 9 o'clock.\nHowever, when must is used in the original to conclude that something (has) happened or that\nsomething is true, then we use must, not had to, in the report:\n\nOD ‘Lkeep forgetting things. Most of these nouns are\nrelated to reporting verbs (acknowledgement — acknowledge, statement — state, etc.). Notice that\nwhen we report using nouns and adjectives (see C) the exact words that were said are not\nnecessarily reported. Instead we might report what was said in our own words, or report that\nsomething was said without reporting what was said.\nwx Nouns followed by a that-clause include acknowledgement, advice, allegation,\nannouncement, answer, argument, claim, comment, conclusion, decision, explanation,\nforecast, guarantee, indication, observation, promise, recommendation, reply, speculation,\nstatement, suggestion, threat, warning:\n© The claim is often made that smoking causes heart disease.\n© The jury came to the conclusion that the woman was guilty.\nNotice that we don’t usually leave out that in sentences like this (see Unit 33).\n\nNouns followed by a to-infinitive clause include decision, encouragement, instruction,\n\ninvitation, order, promise, recommendation, refusal, threat, warning:\n\n© L accepted Louisa’s invitation fo visit her in Rome.\n\nOG He was delighted with his portrait and gave me every encouragement to take up\npainting again.\n\nNotice that some of these nouns can also be followed by a that-clause:\n\n0 They carried out their threat to dismiss workers on strike. (or ...their threat that they\nwould dismiss workers on strike.)\n\n% Nouns followed by a wh-clause include explanation, discussion, issue, problem, question.\nWe usually use of after these nouns in reporting:\n© John raised the question of when the money would be collected.\n© Our previous meeting looked at the issue of how to increase income.\n\nAfter many of the nouns listed in A we can use as to + wh-clause or as to + wh-word +\nto-infinitive to introduce the subject of a question or topic discussed or thought about (see also\nUnit 34F). Notice that an alternative preposition can usually be used instead of as to:\nO She asked my advice as to what subject she should study at university. (or ...advice on...)\n© There was some discussion as to whether the price included tax or not. (or ...discussion of...)\n© Before we left we gave them strict instructions as to how to cook it. (or about how to...)\n\nReporting using adjectives\n\nA number of adjectives can be used to report a speaker's feelings or opinion about a situation.\nSome are followed by a that-clause. These include adamant, agreed, angry, annoyed, certain,\ngrateful, insistent, sure:\n\nO The builders are certain that they'll be finished by the end of the week.\n\nAdjectives expressing uncertainty are usually followed by a wh-clause. These include doubtful\n(usually + whether), uncertain, not certain, unsure, not sure:\n© Scientists aren’t sure where the remains of the spacecraft will come to land.\n\nSome are usually followed by a preposition (followed by a noun phrase). Here are some\nexamples together with the most common preposition(s) following them: apologetic,\ncomplimentary, insulting, tactful (+ about something); critical, dismissive, scornful (+ of\nsomebody/something); abusive, sympathetic (+ to/towards somebody):\n© Today’s newspapers are very critical of the President's decision to appoint Mr Walters.\n© When I asked him what he thought of my new suit, he was quite insulting about it.\n\nercises Unit 38\n\n1 Complete the sentences with one of these nouns and then expand the notes in brackets. This form is ¢\npresent subjunctive (see Unit 85A for the past subjunctive) and is used when we talk about\nbringing about the situation expressed in the that-clause. Note that although they are called\n‘present’ and ‘past’ subjunctive, they do not refer to present and past time:\nThey have proposed that Jim move to their London office.\n© They directed that the building be pulled down.\n\nTo make a negative form, we use not (not ‘do not’) before the verb:\n© We advised that the company not raise its prices.\n\nIn less formal contexts we can use ordinary forms of the verb instead of the subjunctive. Com\nsuggested that he should give up golf. (negative: ...that he shouldn't give up.\nO I suggested that he give up golf. (more formal) (negative: ...that he not give up...)\n2 LI suggested that he gives up golf. (less formal) (negative: ...that he doesn’t give up...)\n\nOther verbs that can be used in a reporting clause before a that-clause with either should or\nsubjunctive include advise, ask, beg, command, demand, direct, insist, instruct, intend, order,\nprefer, propose, recommend, request, require, stipulate, suggest, urge, warn. (or ...that he recetves...)\n\nWe can use should in a that-clause when we talk about our own reaction to something we are\nreporting, particularly after be + adjective (e.g. amazed, amused, anxious, astounded,\nconcerned, disappointed, shocked, surprised, upset). Compare:\n\n© Iam concerned that she should think I stole the money and\n\n©) Lam concerned that she thinks | stole the money. (not ...that she think I stole...)\n\nNotice that when we leave out should in sentences like this we use an ordinary tense, not a\nsubjunctive. There is usually very little difference in meaning between sentences like this with\nand without should. We leave out should in less formal contexts.\n\nWe can use should in a that-clause to talk about both a situation that exists now:\nCQ It’s not surprising that they should be seen together — they're brothers.\n\nor one that may exist in the future:\nO We believe it is important that she should take the exam next year.\n\nIf we are talking about an intention or plan, we can often use a subjunctive rather than sh\nO I've arranged that she come to the first part of the meeting. These include belongings, clothes,\ncongratulations, earnings, goods, outskirts, overheads, particulars (= information), premises\n(= building), riches, savings, stairs, surroundings, thanks. The noun whereabouts can be used\nwith either a singular or a plural verb. The nouns police and people always take a plural verb,\nand the noun staff usually does:\n\n© The company’s earnings have increased for the last five years.\n\n© Police believe that Thomas is in Brazil, although his exact whereabouts are/is unknown.\n\n© Staff say that the new computer system has led to greater levels of stress in their work.\n\nSome nouns always end in -s and look as if they are plural, but when we use them as the subject\nthey have a singular verb:\n2 The news from the Middle East seems very encouraging.\n\nOther words like this include means (= ‘method’ or ‘money’); some academic disciplines, e.g.\neconomics, linguistics, mathematics, phonetics, physics, politics, statistics; some sports, ¢.g.\nathletics, gymnastics; and some diseases, e.g. diabetes, measles, rabies. However, compare:\n\nacademic subject general use\n\n© Politics ts popular at this university. © Her politics are bordering on the fascist\n(= political belief)\n\n© Statistics was always my worst subject. ) Statistics are able to prove anything you\n\nwant them to. (= numerical information)\n\n© Economics has only recently been The economics behind their policies are\nrecognised as a scientific study. unreasonable. (= the financial system)\n\nAlthough the words data and media (= newspaper, television, etc.) are plural (with singular\nforms datum and medium), they are commonly used with a singular verb, However, in formal\ncontexts such as academic writing a plural verb is preferred. Notice that other similar plurals\nsuch as criteria and phenomena (with singular forms criterion and phenomenon) are always\nused with plural verbs. Compare:\n\nCD All the data is available for public inspection. (or ...are available...) and\n\n© [agree that the criteria are not of equal importance. (rot ...the criteria is not...)\n\nWith a phrase referring to a measurement, amount or quantity we usually prefer a singular verb:\nCD Only three metres separates the runners in first and second places. (rather than\n... Separate...)\n© The fifty pounds he gave me was soon spent. (rather than ...were...)\nand a singular verb must be used when the complement is a singular noun phrase (e.g. a\nlong time):\n3 Three hours seems a long time to take on the homework, (not Three hours seem...)\n\nAfter per cent (also percent or %) (of) we use a singular verb if the per cent phrase refers to a\nsingular or uncountable noun and a plural verb if it refers to a plural noun. Compare:\n\n© An inflation rate of only 2 per cent makes a big difference to exports. and\n\n© I would say that about 50 per cent of the houses need major repairs.\n\nHowever, where we use a singular noun that can be thought of as either a whole unit or a\ncollection of individuals, we can use either a singular or plural verb:\nU0 Some 80 per cent of the electorate is expected to vote. (or ...are expected...)\n\nExercises Unit 42\n\n42.1 Make any necessary corrections to the underlined verbs. These include -\nvw when the first noun only has a plural form:\n© a savings account a customs officer a clothes shop (compare a shoe shop)\nthe arms trade (arms = weapons) a glasses case (glasses = spectacles. Compare ‘a glass\ncase’ = a case made of glass) an arts festival (arts = music, drama, film, dance,\npainting, etc. Compare ‘an art festival’; art = painting, drawing and sculpture)\nw when we refer to an institution (an industry, department, etc.), such as\nO the building materials industry _ the publications department\nwhich deals with more than one kind of item or activity (different types of building\nmaterial, different forms of publication).\n\nNotice that to make a compound noun plural we usually make the second noun plural:\n© coal mine(s) office-worker(s) tea leaf/tea leaves\n\nSometimes a noun + noun is not appropriate and instead we use noun + -’s + noun (possessive\nform) or noun + preposition + noun. (or ...a year.)\nO Wait here for one minute, and I'll be with you. (or ...a minute...)\nUsing one in sentences like these gives a little more emphasis to the length of time, quantity,\namount, etc.:\nG He weighs one hundred and twenty kilos! Would you believe it! (using one emphasises\nthe weight more than using a)\n\nHowever, we use one rather than a/an if we want to emphasise that we are talking about only\none thing or person rather than two or more:\n\n0 Do you want one sandwich or two?\n\nO Are you staying only one night?\n\n© I just took one look at her and she started crying.\nWe use one, not a/an, in the pattern one...other/another:\n\nO Close one eye, and then the other.\nC Bees carry pollen from one plant to another.\n\nWe also use one in phrases such as one day, one evening, one spring, etc. to mean a particular,\nbut unspecified day, evening, spring, etc.:\n\n© Hope to see you again one day.\n\n© One evening, while he was working late at the office...\n\nGrammar review: articles —> 16-17\n\nExercises Unit 44\n\n44.1\n\nWrite a or an in the spaces. The is used when we assume the listener or reader\nwill understand who or what we are referring to, or when other words in the noun phrase\nthe reference specific. Compare:\n\nO The government has promised not to tax books. (= books generally) and\n\nO The books have arrived. (= the books you ordered)\n\nU Music played an important part in his life. (= music generally) and\n\nO I thought the music used in the film was the best part. (= this particular music)\n\nWe often use zero article with the names of holidays, special times of the year, months, and da:\nof the week including Easter, Ramadan, New Year’s Day. But compare:\nCP see you on Saturday. (= next Saturday) © We met on Saturday. (= last Saturday)\nO They arrived on a Saturday as far as I can remember. (we are only interested in the day\nof the week, not which particular Saturday)\n© They arrived on the Saturday after my birthday. (a particular Saturday, specifying\nwhich one)\n\nWith winter, summer, spring, autumn, and New Year (meaning the holiday period), we can use\neither zero article or the:\n© In (the) summer I try to spend as much time as I can in the garden.\nWe use the when it is understood or we go on to specify which summer, spring, etc. we mean:\nI'd like to go skiing in the autumn, (= this year) ( I first went skiing in the spring of 1992.\nWe say ‘in the New Year’ to mean near the beginning of next year:\n© T’ll see you again in the New Year.\n\nWhen we want to describe the features of a particular holiday, season, or other period of time\nand say that it was somehow special when compared with others, we can use It/That was... +\na/an + noun + modifying phrase. Compare:\n© That was a winter I'll never forget. (= compared to other winters it was unforgettable) and\n© That was the winter we went to Norway. (= a statement about a particular winter)\n\nWe use zero article with times of the day and night such as midnight, midday, and noon:\nIf possible, I’d like it finished by midday. © Midnight couldn’t come quickly enough.\nBut notice that we can say either the dawn or dawn:\n© He got back into bed and waited for (the) dawn.\n\nWe use the + morning/afternoon/evening for a day which is understood or already specified:\n© L enjoyed the morning, but in the afternoon the course was boring. But compare:\n© Morning is the time I work best. (= mornings in general; The morning... is also possible}\n2 Pll be there by (the) morning/evening. (but ...by the afternoon, not ...by afternoon)\n1 waited all morning. (more usual than all the morning/afternoon, etc.)\n© *You look upset.’ ‘Yes, I’ve had a terrible morning.’ (= compared to other mornings)\n\nWe use by + zero article to talk about means of transport and communication, including\ngo/travel by car/taxi/bus/plane/train/air/sea; contact/communicate by post/email/phone.\nCompare:\n\n© I generally go by bus to work. and © I generally take the bus to work.\n\nWe often use zero article in patterns where repeated or related words are joined by a\npreposition and used with a general meaning:\n\n© The government makes grants according to criteria that differ from region to region.\nOther examples include person to person, back to back, end to end, face to face, side by side,\nstart to finish, day by day, put pen to paper.\n\nGrammar review: articles — 11-19\n\nExercises Unit 47\n\n47\n\n47.2\n\nComplete these sentences using one of these words. (= a specific criticism)\n\nWe often use any in clauses that begin with before, and with comparisons:\nG I cleared up the mess before anyone saw it. However, there are a number of exceptions —\n\n% In formal contexts, such as academic writing, much (of) and many (of) are often preferred.\nWe can also use phrases such as a large/considerable/substantial amount of (with\nuncountable nouns), or a large/considerable/great/substantial number of (with plural nouns):\n© Much debate has been heard about Thornton’s new book.\n© There could be many explanations for this.\n© Much of her fiction describes women in unhappy marriages.\n\nCG A large amount of the food was inedible. (or Much of...)\n© The book contains a large number of pictures, many in colour. (or ...many...)\n% In formal contexts we can use much and many as pronouns:\n) There is no guarantee of a full recovery. Much depends on how well she responds to\ntreatment.\nCG The government's policies have done much to reduce unemployment.\n© Many (= many people) have argued that she is the finest poet of our generation.\nO Not once did I see a tiger in the jungle, although I heard many. (referring back to ‘tiger(s)’)\n\n% We usually use many rather than a lot of or lots of with time expressions (days, minutes,\nmonths, weeks, years) and number + of (c.g. thousands of voters, millions of pounds):\n© We used to spend many hours driving to Melbourne and back.\n© He was the founder of a company now worth many millions of pounds.\n\nWe can use many following the, my, its, his, her, etc. and plural countable nouns:\nO Among the many unknowns after the earthquake is the extent of damage to the\nfoundations of buildings.\nO The gallery is exhibiting some of his many famous paintings of ships.\n\nWe can use the phrase many a with a singular noun to talk about a repeated event or a large\nnumber of people or things:\nO The manager must have spent many a sleepless night worrying about his team selection.\n© Many a pupil at the school will be pleased that Latin is no longer compulsory.\n\nTo emphasise that we are talking about a large number we can use a good/great many with a\nplural noun:\n© She has a good/great many friends in New Zealand.\n\nTo emphasise that we are talking about a large amount we can use a good/great deal of with a\nsingular or uncountable noun:\n© A good/great deal of the exhibition was devoted to her recent work.\n\nWe use far (not ‘much’ or ‘many’) before too many + a plural countable noun or too much + an\nuncountable noun:\n© Far too many students failed the end-of-year maths exam. (not Much/Many too many...)\n© Far too much time is wasted filling in forms. (ot Much/Many too much time...)\n\nWe often use plenty of instead of a lot of or lots of with uncountable and plural countable\nnouns. However, plenty of means ‘enough, or more than enough’ and is therefore not likely in\ncertain contexts. Compare:\n© We took lots of food and drink on our walk through the hills. (or ...plenty of...) and\nG Jim doesn’t look well. He’s lost a lot of weight. (‘plenty of’ is unlikely here)\n\nGrammar review: much (of), many (of), a lot of, lots (of) —* 138-142\n\nExercises\n\n50.1\n\nMake corrections or improvements to these extracts from conversations (1-3) and from\nacademic writing (4-6). (or All (of) his songs sound...)\n© We all think Ann’s working too hard. (or All of us think...)\n\nNotice that we usually put all after the verb be and after the first auxiliary verb if there is one:\n© They are all going to Athens during the vacation. (not They all are going...)\nG You should all have three question papers. (not You all should have...; however, note that\nwe can say ‘You all should have...’ for particular emphasis in spoken English)\n\nTo make negative sentences with all (of) we usually use not all (of) rather than all...not\n(although all...not is sometimes used in informal spoken English):\n© Not all (of) the seats were taken. or The seats were not all taken.\n\nNote that not all and none of have a different meaning. Compare:\nO Not all my cousins were at the wedding. (= some of them were there) and\n© None of my cousins were at the wedding. (= not one of them was there)\n\nAll and whole\n\nBefore singular countable nouns we usually use the whole rather than all the:\n© They weren't able to stay for the whole concert. (rather than...for all the concert.)\n\nHowever, we can also say all + day/week/night/month/winter, etc. (but not usually all October/\n2001/ 21\" May, etc.; all Monday/Tuesday, etc. are only usually used in informal contexts); all\nthe time, all the way; and in informal speech we can use all the with things that we see as being\nmade up of parts (all the world/house/city/country/department, etc.):\n\n© She spent the whole winter in the south of Spain. (or ...all winter...)\n\n© After the fire the whole city was covered in dust. (or ...all the city... in informal speech)\nNotice that we can use entire instead of whole immediately before a noun:\n\nO The whole/entire building has recently been renovated.\n\nBefore plural nouns we can use all (of) or whole, but they have different meanings. Compare:\n© All (of) the towns had their electricity cut off. (= every town in an area) and\n© After the storm, whole towns were left without electricity. (= some towns were\ncompletely affected; note that we don’t say ‘...whole the towns...’)\n\nEvery and each\n\nOften we can use every or each with little difference in meaning. However, we use every —\n* with almost, nearly, virtually, etc. to emphasise we are talking about a group as a whole:\n© Almost every visitor stopped and stared. (not Almost each visitor...)\n¥% with a plural noun when every is followed by a number:\nG I go to the dentist every six months. (rather than ...each six months.)\nw in phrases referring to regular or repeated events such as: every other (kilometre), every\nsingle (day), every so often, every few (months), and every now and again (= occasionally)\n% with abstract uncountable nouns such as chance, confidence, hope, reason, and sympathy to\nshow a positive attitude to what we are saying. Here every means ‘complete’ or ‘total’:\nO She has every chance of success in her application for the job.\nWe use each -\nvx when we are talking about both people or things in a pair:\n© Lonly had two suitcases, but each one weighed over 20 kilos.\n® as a pronoun:\nOG Lasked many people and each gave the same answer. (or ...each/every one gave...)\n\nGrammar review: all (of) —> 143-144; each/every > 145-146\n\nExercises Unit 51\n\n51.1 Put all in the more appropriate space in each sentence. However, we can also use them as pronouns:\nO Little is known about the painter’s early life.\nO Iris a part of the world visited by few. (= few people)\n© Do you want a chocolate? (or The few visitors...)\nNotice that we can also say ‘She gave whav'the little she had...’ and ‘What/The few we have...’\nwhen it is clear from the context what is being referred to.\n\nWe can use few (but rarely little) after personal pronouns (my, her, etc.) and these and those:\nO I learned to play golf during my few days off during the summer.\n© She put her few clothes into a bag, and walked out of the house for ever.\n© These few miles of motorway have taken over ten years to build.\n\nIn speech and informal writing, it is more usual to use not many/much or only/just... a few/little\ninstead of few and little to talk about a small amount or number, and we often use a bit (of) in\ninformal speech instead of a little:\n\n2 Sorry I haven’t finished, I haven’t had much time today. (rather than ...a little chocolate?)\n\nIn more formal contexts, such as academic writing, we generally prefer few and little:\n© The results take little account of personal preference. (rather than ...don’t take much...)\n\nless (than) and fewer (than)\nWe use less with uncountable nouns and fewer with plural countable nouns:\n\n© You should eat less pasta. © There are fewer cars on the road today.\nLess is sometimes used with a plural countable noun (e.g. ...less cars...), particularly in\nconversation. However, this is grammatically incorrect.\n\nWe use less than with a noun phrase indicating an amount:\n\n© Lused to earn less than a pound a week when I first started work. ]\nLess than is sometimes also used with a noun phrase referring to a group of things or people, ]\nparticularly in conversation. However, some people think this is incorrect, particularly in formal\ncontexts, and that fewer than should be used instead:\n\n© There were fewer than twenty students present. (or mformally ...less than...)\nWhen we talk about a distance or a sum of money we use less than, not fewer than:\n\n© The beach is less than a mile away.\nTo emphasise that a number is surprisingly large we can use no less than or no fewer than:\n\n© The team has had no fewer than ten managers in just five years. (or ...no less than...)\nNotice that we prefer no less than with percentages, periods of time and quantities: |\n\n) Profits have increased by no less than 95% in the last year. (rather than ...no fewer than...)\n\nGrammar review: few, little, less — 147-152\n\nUnit 52\n\nComplete the sentences with (a) few, (a) little, the few, the little, what few or what little,\ngiving alternatives where possible. (more\nnatural than ‘| received a letter, whose poor spelling made me think...’)\n\nHowever, we sometimes use whose when we talk about towns, countries, or organisations:\nO The film was made in Botswana, whose wildlife parks are larger than those in Kenya.\n© We need to learn from companies whose trading is more healthy than our own,\n\nIn academic writing whose is used to talk about a wide variety of ‘belonging to’ relationships:\n© Students are encouraged to use an appropriate theory in order to solve problems whose\ngeographical limits are clear.\n\nClauses with when, whereby, where and why\nWe can begin relative and other clauses with when (referring to time), whereby (method or\nmeans; used mainly in formal contexts), and where (location). In formal English in particular, a\nphrase with preposition + which can often be used instead of these:\n© He wasn’t looking forward to the time when he would have to leave. (or ...the time at\nwhich ...}\n© Do you know the date when we have to hand in the essay? (or ...the date on/by which...)\nD The government is to end the system whereby (= ‘by which means’) farmers make more\nmoney from leaving land unplanted than from growing wheat. (or ...the system in/by\nwhich farmers...)\n© This was the place where we first met. (or ...the place at/in which we...)\n\nIn academic English, we can also use where to refer to relationships other than location,\nparticularly after words such as case, condition, example, situation, system:\n© Later in this chapter we will introduce cases where consumer complaints have resulted in\nchanges in the law. (or more formally ...cases in which...)\n\nWe can also use a/the reason why or a/the reason that or just a/the reason:\n© I didn’t get a pay rise, but this wasn’t the reason why I left. (or ...the reason (that) I left.)\n\nClauses with who and what; whatever, whoever and whichever\nSome clauses beginning with a wh-word are used like a noun phrase in a sentence. These are\nsometimes called nominal relative clauses:\nOC Can you give me a list of who's been invited? (= the people who have been invited) |\n© I didn’t know what I should do next. (= the thing that I should do next) |\nNotice that we can’t use what in this way after a noun: ]\nC I managed to get all the books that you asked for. (not ...all the books what you asked for)\n\nWe use clauses beginning with whatever (= anything or it doesn’t matter what), whoever (= the\nperson/group who or any person/group who), or whichever (= one thing or person from a\nlimited number, to talk about things or people that are indefinite or unknown:\n\nO I'm sure I'll enjoy eating whatever you cook.\n\n© Whoever wins will go on to play Barcelona in the final.\n\nOC Whichever one of you broke the window will have to pay for it.\nGrammar review: relative clauses —* J1-J3\n\nxercises Unit 54\n\n$4.1\n\n$4.2\n\nCombine a sentence from i with a sentence from ii to make new sentences with whose, as in 1. (or more formally The school of which\nshe...)\n© The book that/which he’s most proud of... (or more formally The book of which he...)\n\nIn formal, mainly written, English whose can come after a preposition in a relative clause.\nHowever, it is more natural to put the preposition at the end of the clause in less formal\ncontexts and in spoken English:\n© The council is in discussion with Lord Thomas, on whose land most of the village is\nbuilt. (or less formally ...Lord Thomas, whose land most of the village is built on.)\nO I now turn to Freud, from whose work the following quotation is taken. (or less formally\n...Freud, whose work the following quotation is taken from.)\n\nWhen a preposition is needed with the relative pronouns which and whom we usually put it\nbefore the relative pronoun in formal styles:\n\nO The rate at which a material heats up depends on its chemical composition.\n\n© Her many friends, among whom | like to be considered, gave her encouragement.\n\nAfter a preposition we usually use whom rather than who in formal styles:\n© Is it right that politicians should make important decisions without consulting the public\nto whom they are accountable? (rather than ...the public to who they are accountable.)\nand we don’t use that or no relative pronoun:\nO The valley in which the town lies is heavily polluted. (not The valley in that the town lies\nis heavily polluted.; not The valley in the town lies is heavily polluted.)\n\nIn less formal English we usually put the preposition later in the relative clause rather than at\nthe beginning:\n© The office that Graham took us to was filled with books.\nand we prefer who (or that) rather than whom (see also Unit 26A):\nThe playground wasn’t used by the children who it was built for.\n\nIf the verb in the relative clause is a two-word verb (e.g. come across, fill in, look after, take on)\nwe don’t usually put the preposition before the relative pronoun:\n© The Roman coins, which a local farmer came across in a field, are now on display in the\nNational Museum. (of ...coins, across which the local farmer came, are...)\n\nWith three-word verbs, we only put the preposition before the relative pronoun in a very formal\nor literary style, and many people avoid this pattern:\n© She is one of the few people to whom I look up. (or less formally ...who I look up to.)\n\nGrammar review: relative clauses — J1-J3\n\nExercises Unit 55\n\n55.1\n\n55.2\n\n§5.3\n\n55.4\n\nRewrite these sentences so that they are more appropriate for formal written English. However, when the second item acts like a defining\nrelative clause, when it is usually a name, there is usually no punctuation in writing or\nintonation break in speech:\n0 My friend Jim has moved to Sweden. (rather than My friend, Jim, ...)\n0 The current champion is expected to survive her first-round match with the Italian Silvia\nFarina. (rather than ...the Italian, Silvia Farina.)\n\nWe can add information to a noun phrase with a conjunction such as and or or:\n© Kurt Svensson, her teacher and well-known concert pianist, thinks that she has great\ntalent. (= her teacher is also a well-known concert pianist)\n0 My business partner and great friend Tom Edwards is getting married today.\n© Phonetics or the study of speech sounds is a common component on courses in teaching\nEnglish as a foreign language.\n\nThe adverb namely and the phrase that is are used to add details about a noun phrase:\nO This side-effect of the treatment, namely weight gain, is counteracted with other drugs.\nCO The main cause of global warming, that is the burning of fossil fuels, is to be the focus of\nnegotiations at the international conference.\n\nWe can also add information to a noun phrase using a participle clause beginning with an -ing,\n-ed or being + -ed verb form. These are often similar to defining relative clauses (see GR: J1-2):\n© Any passengers travelling to Cambridge should sit in the first two carriages of the train.\n(or Any passengers who are travelling...)\n© The people living next door come from Italy. (or The people who are living next door...)\n© The weapon used in the murder has now been found. (or The weapon that was used..)\n© The book published last week is his first novel. (or The book that was published last week...)\n© The prisoners being released are all women. (or The prisoners who are being released...)\n( The boys being chosen for the team are under 9. (or The boys who are being chosen...)\n\nNotice that -ing participle clauses correspond to defining relative clauses with an active verb,\nwhile -ed and being + -ed clauses correspond to defining relative clauses with a passive verb.\n\nWe can also use a to-infinitive clause, as in:\n© Have you brought a book to read? (= you bring it and you read it)\nHave you brought a book for Kevin to read? (= you bring it and Kevin reads it)\nMy decision to resign from the company was made after a great deal of thought.\nI thought that the decision of the committee, to increase staff holidays, was a good one.\n\nooo\n\nIn written English, particularly in newspapers, -ing and -ed clauses are also used instead of non-\ndefining relative clauses. These are usually written between commas or dashes (-):\n© The men, wearing anoraks and hats, made off in a stolen Volvo estate.\n© The proposals - expected to be agreed by ministers - are less radical than many\nemployers had feared.\n\nExercises Unit 56\n\n56.1\n\n56.2\n\n$6.3\n\nAdd the information given below to the sentences and rewrite them in an appropriate way,\nusing the examples in A and B as models.\nKlaus Schmidt is the current European champion\nAndy Todd is head of Downlands Hospital\nBeluga caviar is among the most expensive food in the world\nTonya’s father has also been her trainer for the last 10 years\nPaul Jennings is Australian My colleague is Paul\nGofast Fechnology +s part of the Maddison Enterprises Group:\nAnother name for rubella is German measles\nThe German 10,000 metres record holder is also the current European champion\n1 Gofast Technology has launched its new generation of high-speed trains.\nGofast Technology, part of the Maddison Enterprises Group, has launched its new _\ngeneration of high-speed trains.\n2 I went on an IT training course with my colleague.\n3 Rubella is still a common childhood disease in many countries.\n4 Four kilos of Beluga caviar has been ordered for James and Stephanie’s wedding party.\nS$ One of the most popular modern writers for children is Paul Jennings.\n6 Tonya’s father was in the crowd to watch her victory.\n7 Dr Andy Todd has criticised government plans to cut health funding.\n8 Klaus Schmidt is running in the Stockholm Marathon.\n\nMatch the sentence beginnings (in i) and endings (in ii) and add appropriate information\n(from iii) after namely or that is. For example:\n\n© The protesters were demonstrating against the mistreatment of animals on farms.\ncould mean either that the place the protesters were demonstrating was ‘on farms’ or that the\nanimals were ‘on farms’. (C)\n1 A man was talking with a grey suit.\n\n2 A lorry was stopped by a police officer carrying thousands of stolen cigarettes.\n3 I discussed my plan to decorate the room with my parents.\n\n115\n\nWe can use present participle (-ing) and past participle (-ed) clauses with an adverbial meaning. —\n(See also Unit 59.) Clauses like these often give information about the timing, causes, and\nresults of the events described:\nOC Opening her eyes, the baby began to cry. (= When she opened her eyes...)\nO Faced with a bill for £10,000, John has taken an extra job. (= Because he is faced...)\n© Looked after carefully, the plant can live through the winter. (= If it is looked after.\n0 Having completed the book, he had a holiday. (perfect; = When/Because he had\ncompleted...)\nO The fruit was expensive, being imported. (simple passive; = ...because it was imported)\nO Having been hunted close to extinction, the rhino is once again common in this area.\n(perfect passive; = Although it had been hunted close to extinction...)\n\nThe implied subject of a participle clause (that is, a subject known but not directly mentioned)\nis usually the same as the subject of the main clause:\n\n© Arriving at the party, we saw Ruth standing alone. (= When we arrived...we saw...)\nHowever, sometimes the implied subject is not referred to in the main clause:\n\n© Having wanted to drive a train all his life, this was an opportunity not to be missed.\n\nIn careful speech and writing we avoid different subjects for the participle and main clause:\nG Turning round quickly, the door hit me in the face. (first implied subject = ‘I’; second\nsubject = ‘the door’)\n\nIn formal English, the participle clause sometimes has its own subject, which is often a pronoun\nor includes one:\n\n© The collection of vases is priceless, some being over two thousand years old.\n\n© Her voice breaking with emotion, Jean spoke about her father’s illness.\n\nWe use a present participle (-ing) clause to talk about something happening at the same time as\nan event in the main clause, or to give information about the facts given in the main clause.\n\nWhen we use not in a participle clause it usually comes before the participle. However, it can\nfollow the participle, depending on the part of the sentences affected by not. Compare:\nCO Wishing not to go out that night, | made an excuse. (‘not’ relates to ‘to go out that night’;\nthe sentence means ‘I didn’t want to go out on that particular night’) and\n© Not wishing to go out that night, I made an excuse. (‘not’ relates to ‘wish to go out that\nnight’; the sentence could mean ‘going out on that particular night wasn’t my wish’)\n\nWe use a clause beginning with having + past participle rather than a present participle if the\naction in the main clause is the consequence of the event in the participle clause:\n© Having won every major judo title, Mark retired from international competition. (or\nAfter winning...; not Winning every major judo title...)\nCO Having broken her leg the last time she went, Brenda decided not to go on the school\nskiing trip this year. (or After breaking her leg...; not Breaking her leg...)\n\nWe can use either a present participle (-ing) clause or a having + past participle clause with a\nsimilar meaning when the action in the participle clause is complete before the action in the\nmain clause begins. Compare:\nO Taking off his shoes, Ray walked into the house. (Having taken off...has a similar\nmeaning) and\n© Running across the field, I fell and hurt my ankle. (= While I was running...; ‘Having\nrun...’ would suggest that I fell after | had run across the field)\n\nGrammar review: -ing clauses and -ed clauses — J4-)8\n\nExercises Unit 58\n\n$8.1\n\n58.3\n\nRewrite the sentences beginning with one of the clause forms shown in A and D.\n\nWhen she saw the dog coming towards her, she quickly crossed the road.\nSeeing the dog coming towards her, she quickly crossed the road.\n\n2 As she was dressed all in black, she couldn't be seen in the starless night.\n\n3 As I don’t have a credit card, I found it difficult to book an airline ticket over the phone.\n\n4 Keith spent a lot of time filling in job application forms because he was unemployed.\n\n5 Because I was walking quickly, I soon caught up with her.\n\n6 The house was built of wood, so it was clearly a fire risk.\n\n7 I was eager to catch the bus in good time because I had been told off the day before for\narriving late.\n\n8 She didn’t know where the theatre was, so she asked for directions at the hotel reception.\n\n9 As she was a nurse, she knew what to do after the accident.\n\n10 He had spent his childhood in Oslo so he knew the city well.\n\nWhere the implied subject of the two clauses is the same write S and where it is different write\nD. We also use reflexive\npronouns to emphasise that the subject caused a certain action:\nC1 When Tom and Jack saw the robbery they called the police, but then they got arrested.\n© When Tom and Jack saw the robbery they called the police, but gor themselves arrested.\n(emphasises that Tom and Jack did something to make the police arrest them)\n© When Tom and Jack saw the robbery they called the police, but they got arrested\nthemselves. (emphasises that Tom and Jack were arrested, not the robbers)\n\nIf the object of a transitive verb refers to the same person or thing as the subject, then that\nobject must be a reflexive pronoun. Compare:\n\n© He walked around the golf course to familiarise himself with it. and\n\n©) We walked around to familiarise the children with their new surroundings.\nOther verbs commonly used in this way include absent...from, avail...of, busy...with,\nconcern...with, occupy...with, pride...on, tear...away from, trouble...about/with.\n\nWith some verbs we can use a reflexive pronoun or leave it out with little difference in meaning:\n© We are confident that both sets of fans will behave (themselves) at the match.\nOther verbs like this include acclimatise, adapt, (un)dress, hide, move, prepare, shave, wash, We\ninclude the reflexive pronoun if we want to emphasise particularly that the person or thing\nreferred to in the subject is affected by the action:\n© Although he helped other athletes in their preparations for competing at high altitudes,\nhe found it difficult to acclimatise himself.\n\nWhen the subject and object (after a preposition) refer to the same person or thing we use a\nreflexive pronoun after the preposition:\n© He was pleased with himself. (not ...pleased with him.)\n\nIf the verb has a direct object we use a personal pronoun, not a reflexive pronoun:\n\n0 I remember closing the door behind me. (not ...closing the door behind myself.)\nHowever, if the clause has a direct object and we need to make it clear that the subject and\nprepositional phrase refer to the same person or thing, we use a reflexive pronoun:\n\n© She bought the bracelet for herself. (*...for her’ suggests it was bought for someone else)\n\nMyself is sometimes used after and and or rather than ‘I’ or ‘me’, although some people\nconsider this use incorrect and avoid it:\n\nOC I believe that Tony and myself have done a pretty good job.\n\nOG When you've finished the job can you send the bill either to Mrs Smith or myself?\nUsing myself reduces focus on the speaker or writer and so sounds less forceful or more polite.\n\nWhen we want to contrast someone’s characteristics with how they were or usually are we can\nuse a possessive pronoun with self or selves:\n© Colin was very cheerful this morning. He didn’t seem at ail like his usual miserable self.\nC The image that people have of famous actors may not coincide with their real selves.\n\nGrammar review: reflexive pronouns —* K1-K4\n\nercises Unit 60\n\n60.1 Complete the sentences with one of these verbs in an appropriate form followed by a reflexive\npronoun and, if necessary, a preposition. (not ...I'll bring you one/ones.)\n© asked him to get apple juice, but he got orange. (not ...but he got orange one/ones.)\n\nWe can’t use ones without defining precisely which group of things we are talking about.\n\nInstead, we use some. Compare:\n\n<) ‘We need new curtains.’ ‘Okay, let’s buy green ones this time./...ones with flowers\non/...those ones.’ and\n\n1D ‘We need new curtains.” ‘Okay, let’s buy some.’ (not Okay, let’s buy ones.)\n\nWe don’t use one/ones after nouns used as adjectives:\n© I thought my key was in my trouser pocket, but it was in my coat pocket.\n(not ...my coat one.)\nInstead of using onc/ones after possessive determiners (my, your, her, etc.) we prefer mine,\nyours, hers, etc. However, a possessive determiner + one/ones is often heard in informal speech:\n) I'd really like a watch like yours. (or ‘...like your one’ in informal speech)\n\nWe usually use ones to refer to things rather than people:\n© We need two people to help. We could ask those men over there. (or ...than younger students.)\nNotice also that we use ones to refer to people in the little ones (= small children), (your) loved\nones (= usually close family), (one of) the lucky ones.\n\nWe can leave out one/ones —\nve after which:\n© When we buy medicines, we have no way of knowing which (ones) contain sugar.\n* after superlatives:\n© Look at that pumpkin! Their chances of\ndoing so (= reaching the summit of the mountain) are better than they were last week. (In\nvery formal English we can also use so doing.)\n© When he was asked to check the figures, he claimed that he had already done so.\n(= checked the figures)\n\nDo so is most often used in formal spoken and written English. In informal English we can use\ndo it or do that instead:\n\n© Mrs Bakewell waved as she walked past. She does so/it/that every morning.\n\n© Ricardo told me to put in a new battery. I did so/fit/that, but the radio still doesn’t work.\n\nWe can also use do alone rather than do so in less formal English, especially after modals or\nperfect tenses (see also B):\n\n© ‘Will this programme work on your computer?’ ‘It should do.’\n\n© Itold you that I'd finish the work by today, and I have done. (‘have’ is stressed here)\n\nWe can use do so instead of verbs that describe actions, but we avoid do so with verbs that\ndescribe states and habitual actions. Compare:\n\n©) 65% of the members voted for Ken Brown this time, whereas 84% did so last year.\n\nC Kenyon confessed to the murder, although he only did so after a number of witnesses had\n\nidentified him as the killer.\n\n© I gave her the medicine, and I take full responsibility for doing so. and\n\n© Stefan doesn’t like Porter's films but Bridget does.\n\nG He earned a lot more than I did.\n\n(I don’t have time to go swimming every day, but I usually do.\n\nSuch\nWe can use such + (a/an) + noun to refer back to something mentioned before, with the\nmeaning ‘of this/that kind’. We use such + noun when the noun is uncountable or plural, and\nsuch + a/an + noun when the noun is countable and singular. Such is used in this way mainly e\nformal speech and writing:\n© The students refer to teachers by their first names and will often criticise them for badly-\nprepared lessons. Such behaviour is unacceptable in most schools. (more informally\nBehaviour like this...)\nOG When asked about rumours that the company is preparing to lose more than 200 jobs, a\nspokeswoman said: ‘I know of no such plans.’ (more informally ...no plans of this kind.\n© They needed someone who was both an excellent administrator and manager. Such a\nperson was not easy to find. (more informally A person like this...)\nOC We allow both men and women to have time off work to bring up children. We were\nfirst department to introduce such a scheme. (more informally ...a scheme like this.)\n\n“xercises Unit 63\n\n3.1 Make the two sentences into one, joining them with either and or but as appropriate. (or like that >)\n\n127\n\nTo avoid repeating words from a previous clause or sentence we use an auxiliary verb (be, have,\ncan, will, would, etc.) instead of a whole verb group (e.g. ‘has finished’) or instead of a verb and\nwhat follows it (e.g. ‘like to go to Paris’):\n\n© She says she’s finished, but I don’t think she has. (instead of ...has finished.)\n\n© ‘Would any of you like to go to Paris?’ ‘I would.’ (instead of | would like to go to Paris.)\n\nIf there is more than one auxiliary verb in the previous clause or sentence, we leave out all the\nauxiliary verbs except the first instead of repeating the main verb. Alternatively, we can use two\n(or more) auxiliary verbs:\n\n© Alex hadn't been invited to the meal, although his wife had. (or ...had been.)\n\n© ‘They could have been delayed by the snow.’ ‘Yes, they could.’ (or ...could have (been).)\n\nIf there is no auxiliary verb in the previous clause or sentence, or if the auxiliary is a form of\ndo, we can use a form of do instead of repeating the main verb. We use do when the main verb\nis a present simple form and did when it is a past simple form:\n© Monica plays golf on Saturdays, and I do too. (instead of ...and I play golf on Saturdays\ntoo.; *...and so do I’ is also possible)\n1 ‘I didn’t steal the money.’ ‘No-one thinks that you did.’ (instead of ...thinks that you stole\nit.; ‘No-one thinks so’ is also possible.)\n\nIf be is the main verb in the previous clause or sentence, we repeat a form of the verb be:\n© ‘The children are noisy again.’ ‘They always are.’\n\nIf have or have got is the main verb in the previous clause or sentence, we can usually use a\nform of either do or have:\n\n3 ‘Do you think I have a chance of winning?’ ‘Yes, I think you have.’ (or ...you do.; ‘Yes, I\n\nthink so’ 1s also possible.)\n\n© Even if he hasn’t got a map himself, he may know someone who has. (or ...who does.)\nHowever, if we use have + noun in the previous clause or sentence to talk about actions (have a\nshower, have a shave, have a good time, etc.) we prefer do:\n\n© I wasn’t expecting to have a good time at the party, but I did.\n\nNotice that sometimes we can use either do, be or have with a similar meaning (see also C):\n© Lasked Suzie to tidy her room, and she has/did. (‘has’ replaces ‘has tidied her room’; ‘did’\nreplaces ‘tidied her room’.)\n\nIf we use have as an auxiliary verb, we can often follow it with done instead of repeating the\nmain verb. This happens particularly in spoken English:\n) ‘She's never made a mistake before.’ ‘Well, she has (done) this time.”\nHowever, this is usually not possible when the verb being substituted is intransitive:\n© ‘They've already gone.’ ‘I don’t think Bob has.’ (rot ...Bob has done.)\n\nSimilarly, after a modal auxiliary verb (can, could, may, might, must, ought to, shall, should,\nwill, would) we can use do, particularly in spoken English:\nU ‘Will you be seeing Tony today?’ ‘I might (do).’\nSometimes we can use be instead of do with a similar meaning (see also D):\n2 ‘Will you be seeing Tony today?’ ‘I might (do/be).’ (‘do’ replaces ‘see Tony today’; ‘be’\nreplaces ‘be seeing Tony today’.)\n\nIf we use be as an awxtliary verb in the previous clause or sentence, we can use be after a modal:\nO ‘Is Ella staying for lunch?’ *Yes, I think she will (be).’ (or ...she will do.)\n\nHowever, if be is used as a main verb in the previous clause or sentence, or as an auxiliary verb\n\nwithin a passive, we can usually leave out be after a modal in informal contexts only. Compare:\n0 The high price surprised him, and © The price seemed high.\n\nSome adjectives are seldom or never used before the noun they describe. These include -\n\n% some adjectives formed by adding a prefix ‘a-’, often to a noun or verb: afraid, alike, alive,\nalone, ashamed, asleep, awake, aware:\n© The horse was alone in the field. (but not The alone horse...)\nSome of these adjectives with an ‘a-’ prefix have related adjectives that can be used either\nbefore a noun or after a linking verb. (for emphasis)\nSome emphasising adjectives (such as complete, and also absolute, entire, mere, sheer, total,\nutter) are seldom or never used after a linking verb:\n\n© It was a total failure. For example, we\ncan talk about ‘democratic decisions’, where ‘democratic’ distinguishes them from other types of\ndecision. Other classifying adjectives include atomic, chemical, cubic, digital, environmental,\nmedical, phonetic; annual, general, occasional, northern (etc.), maximum, minimum,\nunderlying. Classifying adjectives are seldom or never used after a linking verb:\n© a nuclear explosion (but not usually ‘The explosion was nuclear’, unless we particularly\nwant to emphasise a contrast with other kinds of explosion)\n\nQualitative adjectives are used to give the quality that a thing or person has. We use them either\ndirectly before a noun or after a linking verb. Compare:\nOC a beautiful sunset and G The sunset was beautiful.\n\nNote that some classifying adjectives can also be used with different meanings as qualitative\nadjectives and placed after a linking verb. Compare:\n© All the people present (= who were there) approved of the decision. and\n© I was asked for my present address. Here are some examples of adjectives\nused as gradable in their most common meanings:\n\nGrading , 4 bit, dreadfully, extremely,\n\nadverbs | hugely, immensely, intensely, +\nrather, reasonably, slightly, very\n\nangry, big, busy, clever,\n\ncommon, deep, fast, friendly, Gradable\nhappy, important, low, popular, adjectives\nquiet, rich, strong, weak, young |\n\n) She was extremely rich. OG The people there are reasonably friendly.\n\nNon-gradable adjectives are not used with adverbs such as very or extremely because we don’t\nusually imagine degrees of the quality referred to. With non-gradable adjectives we can use adverbs\nwhich emphasise their extreme or absolute nature, such as absolutely, completely, etc. Many\nclassifying adjectives (see Unit 66) are usually non-gradable. Adverbs such as almost, exclusively,\netc., which indicate the extent of the quality, are commonly used with classifying adjectives. Here\nare some examples of adjectives used as non-gradable in their most common meanings:\n\nr\nabsolutely, completely, entirely, | awful, excellent, huge,\nNon- | perfectly, practically, simply, | impossible, superb, terrible, | Non-\ngrading | totally, utterly, virtually; almost, | + | unique, unknown, whole; gradable\nadverbs exclusively, fully, largely, domestic, environmental adjectives\nmainly, nearly, primarily (see Unit 66C)\n© She gave us a completely © It was absolutely superb.\n\nimpossible problem to solve.\n\nGradable adjectives are sometimes used with non-grading adverbs such as absolutely and\ntotally, and non-gradable adjectives are sometimes used with grading adverbs such as extremely,\nrather and very, particularly when we want to give special emphasis or when we are being\nhumorous (see also Unit 68):\n© What you're asking isn’t just difficult — it’s extremely impossible! (grading adverb + non-\ngradable adjective)\n© You’ve won a hundred pounds? Wow, you're virtually rich! (non-grading adverb +\ngradable adjective)\n\nNote that not all the adverbs can go with all the adjectives given in each of the tables above.\nFor example, we can say ‘absolutely huge’, but we wouldn't usually say ‘completely huge’ unless\nit was for particular emphasis or for humour.\n\nThe adverbs fairly (= to quite a large degree, but usually less than ‘very’), really (= ‘very\n(much)’) and pretty (= similar to ‘fairly’; used in informal contexts) are commonly used with\nboth gradable and non-gradable adjectives:\n\n© She’s fairly popular at school. © It was a fairly awful film.\n© I'm really busy at the moment. O The flooding was really terrible.\n© It’s a pretty important exam. O The bill was pretty huge.\n\nHowever, notice that we don’t generally use fairly (or very) with gradable adjectives such as\nessential, invaluable, perfect, superb, tremendous and wonderful which indicate that something\nis very good or necessary:\n1 Some experience is really/pretty essential for the job. (not ...fairly essential...)\n© The weather that day was really/pretty perfect. Most of these participle adjectives can be used before the noun they describe or\nfollowing linking verbs (see Unit 21):\n\nO The hotel had a welcoming atmosphere.\n\nGI found this broken plate in the kitchen cupboard.\n\n© The students’ tests results were pleasing.\n\nG My mother seemed delighted with the present.\n\nWe can use many participle adjectives immediately after nouns when they identify or define the |\nnoun, This use is similar to defining relative clauses (see GR: J1-J2) and they are often called ]\n‘reduced relatives’: |\n© I counted the number of people waiting. (or ...people who were waiting.)\n© We had to pay for the rooms used. (or ...the rooms that were used.)\n\nSome of these are rarely used before the noun:\nCO None of the candidates applying was accepted. (but not ...the applying candidates...)\n© My watch was among the things taken. (but not ...the taken things.)\n\nParticiple adjectives like this include caused, found, included, provided.\n\nOthers can be used before or immediately after nouns. For example, we can say:\n© Rub the area infected with this cream. or —_O Rub the infected area with this cream,\n© The crowd watching grew restless. or —_() The watching crowd grew restless.\nParticiple adjectives like this include affected, alleged, allocated, broken, chosen, identified,\ninterested, remaining, resulting, stolen.\n\nIn formal English, that and those can be used as pronouns before a participle adjective:\nO The office temperature is lower than that required by law. (= the temperature which is\nrequired)\nG The quality of the motors is lower than those manufactured elsewhere. (= the motors\nwhich are manufactured elsewhere)\n\nNotice that those can mean ‘people’:\n} Here is some advice for those (= people) preparing to go on holiday.\n\nMany compound adjectives include a participle adjective. If possible, use a -ly\n\n143\n\nComparatives: -er vs more/less...than\n\nUM We usually add -er to one-syllable adjectives and adverbs to make their comparative form.\nHowever, we use more + adjective -\nvw with one-syllable past participle adjectives (see Unit 69) such as bored, creased, pleased, worn:\n© After I'd ironed my shirt it looked more creased than before. (not ...creaseder...)\n* with fun, real, right and wrong:\n© Lexpected the film to be rather dull, but I couldn't have been more wrong, (not ...wronger.)\n% when we are comparing two qualities:\n© ‘Don’t you think Carl was brave to go bungee jumping?’ ‘Personally, | thought he was\nmore mad than brave.”\n© Although the paint was called ‘Sky blue’, | thought it was more green than blue. {\nWe can also use *...he wasn’t so much brave as mad’ and ‘...it was blue rather than green’. |\n\nWe can sometimes use more as an alternative to the -er form to emphasise the comparison:\n© You might think it’s dark here but it’s more dark in the cellar. (or ...darker...)\nOther adjectives used like this include clear, cold, fair, rough, soft, true.\n\nSome adjectives with two syllables are most commonly used with more/less, particularly |\nparticiple adjectives (e.g. worried, boring); adjectives ending in -ful and -less (e.g. careful,\ncareless); afraid, alert, alike, alone, ashamed, aware; and some other adjectives, including active,\ncautious, certain, complex, direct, eager, exact, formal, frequent, modern, special, recent. Most\ntwo-syllable adjectives ending -y, -ow, -er and -ure can take either an -er or the more + adjective\nform, although the -er form is more frequently used.\n\nSome adjectives (such as complete, equal, favourite, ideal, perfect, unique) have a comparative\nor superlative meaning so they are not often used with -er/-est or more/less/most/least.\nHowever, we can use comparative or superlative forms for special emphasis or for a particular\ncommunicative purpose:\n\n© The weather today was good, but less perfect than yesterday.\n\nSuperlatives\n\nWe usually use the, a possessive form (with -’s), or a possessive pronoun before a superlative\nadjective or adverb. In informal contexts we sometimes leave out the before an -est or most +\nadjective superlative after a linking verb, particularly when the superlative is at the end of a\nsentence:\n© ‘Why did you go by bus?’ ‘It was (the) cheapest.’ © Which was (the) most expensive?\nHowever, we can’t leave out the when we go on to say what group of things is being compared:\nO ‘Why did you buy these oranges?’ “They were the cheapest ones I could find.’ (not They\nwere cheapest ones...)\n\nWhen most + adjective/adverb is used without the, most means something like ‘very’:\n© Did you see how she looked at you? It was most peculiar. (= very peculiar)\nI checked the form most carefully (= very carefully) but didn’t notice the mistake.\n\nAfter a superlative we use of + a plural noun phrase to name the objects being compared:\n2 John’s the oldest of my three brothers.\n\nNotice that we can put the of-phrase at the beginning to emphasise it:\n3 Of my three brothers, John’s the oldest.\n\nWhen we give the location or context within which the comparison is made we usually use in +\n\na singular noun phrase:\n\n(© It was the tallest tree in the forest. (not ...the tallest tree of the forest.)\n\nGrammar review: — (7-18\n\nExercises Unit 72\n\n72.1\n\nUnderline the correct or more likely alternative. In formal speech and\nwriting it is more common to use less + adjective + than:\n© The gap between the sides is not as/so wide as it was. (or ...is less wide than it was.)\n\nIf we put a singular countable noun between an adjective and the second as, we use a/an in\nfront of the noun:\n©) Despite his disability, he tried to lead as normal a life as possible. (not ...as normal life as...)\n0 She was as patient a teacher as anyone could have had. (not ...as patient teacher as...)\n\nThe negative form of sentences like this can use either not as or sometimes not such:\nO It’s not as quiet a place (or ...not such a quiet place ...) as it used to be.\nNotice that we use not as + adjective + a/an + noun but not such a/an + adjective + noun.\n\nWe can use how, so and too followed by an adjective in a similar way:\n© How significant a role did he play in your life?\n0 It’s not quite so straightforward a problem as it might at first seem.\n© ‘Conspiracy’ is perhaps too strong a word. <) How big a piece do you want?\n\nWe also use as much/many as or as little/few as to say that a quantity or amount is larger or\nsmaller than expected. Many and few are preferred before numbers; much and little are\npreferred with amounts (e.g. $5, 20%) and distances (e.g. 3 metres):\n\n© There are a small number of people involved, possibly as few as twenty.\n\nO Prices have increased by as much as 300 per cent.\n\nWe can use not + adjective/adverb + enough + to-infinitive to mean that there isn’t as much as is\nnecessary to do something:\n© T'm not tall enough to reach. ) He didn’t speak loudly enough to be heard.\n\nWe can use sufficiently before adjectives to express a similar meaning to enough. Sufficiently is\noften preferred in more formal contexts:\n© She didn’t play sufficiently well to qualify. (or ...well enough to qualify.)\n\nWe can use too + adjective/adverb + to-infinitive to mean ‘more than necessary, possible, etc.’ to\ndo something:\n\n© They arrived too late to get seats. © It moved too fast fo see it clearly.\nIf we need to mention the things or people involved in the action, we do this with for...:\n\n© The suitcase was too small (for him) to get all his clothes in.\n\nIn rather formal English we can use too + adjective + a/an + noun:\n© LT hope you haven't had too tiring a day. (nor ...a too tiring day.)\n(In a less formal style we might say ‘I hope your day hasn't been too tiring.’)\n\nWe can use so + adjective/adverb + that-clause to say that something existed or happened to\nsuch a degree that a specified result occurred (see also Unit 81):\n© It’s so simple that even I can do it. © He came in so quietly that I didn’t hear him.\nLess often we use so + adjective/adverb + as + to-infinitive with a similar meaning. Compare:\n\n0 The difference was so small that it wasn’t worth arguing about. and\n© The difference was so small as to not be worth arguing about. (= Because the difference\nwas so small, it wasn't worth arguing about)\n\nWe can use go so/as far as + to-infinitive to talk about actions that are surprising or extreme:\n© One furious woman went so/as far as to throw tomatoes at the minister.\n\nExercises Unit 73\n\n73.1\n\nComplete these sentences with as...as or not as/such...as. For example:\n3 He turned round slowly. (end) © Slowly he turned round. (front)\n2 He slowly turned round. (mid)\n\nEnd position\n\nIn end position, we usually put an adverb after an object if there is one rather than immediately\nafter the verb:\n\n© We considered the problem briefly. (not We considered briefly the problem.)\nHowever, if an object is very long other positions are possible:\n\n© We considered briefly the long-term solution to the problem. (or We briefly considered...)\n\nWe avoid putting an adverb between a main verb and a following -ing form or to-infinitive:\n© He began running quickly. or He quickly began running. (mot He began quickly running.)\n) She tried to leave quietly. or She quietly tried to leave. (not She tried quietly to leave.)\nThe position of the adverb can change the meaning of the sentence (see Unit 75A). Compare:\nO I recall telling him clearly that he had won. (= I told him clearly; ‘clearly’ modifies ‘telling\nhim’) and\nO I clearly recall telling him that he had won. (= I clearly recall it; ‘clearly’ modifies ‘recall’.\n\n‘T recall clearly telling him that he had won’ is also possible, but is ambiguous; it can have either of\nthe two meanings given above. In speech, the meaning intended is usually signalled by intonation.\n\nWhen there is more than one adverbial (see Glossary) in end position, the usual order in written |\nEnglish is adverbial of manner (= saying how something is done), place, and then time:\n\nOC In the accident she was thrown violently forwards. (= manner + place)\n\nCO We arrived here on Saturday. (= place + time)\nFor special emphasis we can move an adverbial to the end:\n\nCO In the accident she was thrown forwards, violently.\n\nIf one adverbial is much longer than another then it is usually placed last:\n\nO They left at 3.00 with a great deal of noise. (= time + manner)\nAn adverb usually comes before a prepositional phrase when these have the same function (i.e.\nwhen they both describe manner, or place, or time):\n\nO She went downstairs to the cellar. (= place + place)\n\nEnd position is usual for many adverbials of place, definite frequency, and definite time\n(including adverbial prepositional phrases):\n1 They live upstairs. (not They upstairs live.) <2 She goes weekly. (not She weekly goes.)\nC0 Have you heard the good news? Jane had a baby in May. (not Jane in May had a baby.)\nHowever, adverbs of indefinite time usually go in mid position (see Unit 75).\n\nNotice that in journalism, other adverbs of time are often used in mid position, where we would\nnormally place them in end (or front) position:\n2 The government yesterday announced an increase in education spending.\n\nExercises Unit 74\n\n74.1 Write the adverb in brackets in the sentence in an appropriate position.
