As a result, Japan faces a crisis.\n\nwe time and place adverbs (e.g. tomorrow, nearby) which give more information about a\nprevious reference to a time or place, or show a contrast:\n© The last few days have been hot. Tomorrow the weather will be much cooler.\n\n% comment and viewpoint adverbs (c.g. presumably, financially) which highlight the speaker’s\nattitude to what they are about to say (see Unit 78):\n© She has just heard that her sister is ill. Presumably, she will want to go home.\n\nHowever, other positions are possible for these adverbs.\n\nSome words can be used both as comment adverbs or adverbs of manner. As comment adverbs\nthey usually go in front position (but can go in other positions) and relate to the whole of the\nclause; as adverbs of manner they usually go in end position and modify the verb. Compare:\n\n© Naturally, P'll do all Tcan to help. and © The gas occurs naturally in this area.\n\nMost types of adverb commonly go in front position in a clause (see Unit 74A). In particular -\nOther adverbs like this include clearly, curiously, frankly, honestly, oddly, plainly, seriously.\n\nNote that for special emphasis or focus, adverbs that usually go in mid position (see B) and end\nposition (see also Units 74 and 76) can sometimes be put in front position:\n0 In May, Jane had a baby. © Regularly, Kim works on several paintings at once.\nMid position\nThe following types of adverb usually go in mid position (see Unit 74A) -\nwr degree adverbs (c.g. almost, hardly, nearly, quite, rather, scarcely):\nO The street lighting was so bad that we almost missed the turning.\nalthough some (e.g. completely, enormously, entirely, greatly, slightly) can go in end position;\nO I greatly admire your work. (or I admire your work greatly.)\n\nNotice, however, that some degree adverbs are not usually used in mid position with some\nverbs. For example, enormously is not usually used in mid position with develop, differ, go\nup or vary; greatly is not normally used in mid position with care or suffer.\n\nvw adverbs which indicate the order of events, such as first, last and next. These can also go in\nend position, but if there is a phrase giving the time of an event they usually go before this:\nOI first met her in 1987. (or I met her first in 1987.)\nWe don’t usually put these in front position, except when we use them to list actions (when\nwe usually follow them with a comma in writing; see also Unit 76B):\nO Next, add three teaspoons of sugar.\n\nwx adverbs of frequency which say in an indefinite way how often something happens,\nincluding hardly ever, often, rarely, regularly, seldom (see also Unit 76B); and also the\nfrequency adverbs always and never:\nU We hardly ever see Derek nowadays, he’s so busy at the office.\n\nNotice, however, that adverbial phrases of indefinite frequency (e.g. as a rule, on many\noccasions, from time to time, every so often) usually go in front or end position:\n0 As a rule, I go every six months. (or every six months, as a rule; not ‘I as a rule go...’)\n\nWe rarely put long adverbials (including clauses (see Units 58 and 59), and prepositional and\nnoun phrases) in mid position. Usually they go in end position or front position for emphasis:\nG She phoned home, anxious for news. (or Anxious for news, she phoned home.)\nG He picked up the vase with great care. (or With great care he picked up the vase.)\nG I'd seen Jack the day before. (or The day before I'd seen Jack.)\n\nExercises Unit 75\n\n(75.1\n\n75.3\n\nUse one of these adverbs in each pair of sentences. The effect may\nalso be to highlight what comes at the end (e.g. ‘a body’ in the example below). This order is\nfound mainly in formal descriptive writing and reports. Compare:\n\nO The money was eventually found under the floorboards. (= end) and\n\nU The police searched the house. Under the floorboards they found a body. (= front)\n\nIf we put an adverb of place in front position we put the subject after the verb be (sce also Unit\n99A):\nO Next to the bookshelf was a fireplace. (or less formally Next to the bookshelf there was a\nfireplace; not Next to the bookshelf a fireplace was.)\nNote that this doesn’t apply when the subject is a pronoun. For example, we can’t say ‘Next to\nthe bookshelf was it.’\n\nWe can also put the subject after the verb with intransitive verbs (except with a pronoun\nsubject) used to indicate being in a position or movement to a position, including hang, lie, live,\nsit, stand; come, fly, go, march, roll, run, swim, walk:\n\n© Beyond the houses lay open fields. (but compare Beyond the houses they lay.)\n\n© Through the town marched the band. (but compare Through the town it marched.)\nNote that ‘...open fields lay’, ‘...the band marched’ (etc.) might be used in a literary style.\n\nHowever, we don’t usually put the subject after the verb when we talk about actions: if one of\nthese intransitive verbs is followed by an adverb of manner; with other intransitive verbs; or\nwith transitive verbs:\n\n© Through the waves the boy swam powerfully. (rather than ...swam the boy powerfully.)\nOutside the church the choir sang. (rather than ...sang the choir.)\nIn the garden John built a play house for the children. (not In the garden built John...)\n\noa\n\nWhen we put certain adverbs of time in front position the subject must come after an auxiliary\nverb or a main verb be (see also Unit 100):\n© At no time would he admit that his team played badly. (not At no time he would admit...)\n© Not once was she at home when I phoned. (not Not once she was...)\n\nIf the main verb is not be and there is no auxiliary, we use do, although inversion is not\nnecessary in this case:\n©) Only later did she realise how much damage had been caused. (or Only later she realised...)\n\nAdverbs like this include negative time adverbials such as at no time, hardly ever, not once, only\nlater, rarely, and seldom. Notice also that we can put first, next, now and then in front position —\nwith the verb come to introduce a new event, when the subject follows the verb. But if a comma\n(or an intonation break in speech) is used after first (etc.) the verb follows the subject. Compare:\n© At first there was silence. Then came a voice that I knew. (not Then a voice came...) and\n© At first there was silence. Then, a voice came that I knew.\n\nAdverbs of time which indicate a definite point or period in time or a definite frequency, usually\ngo in end position, or front position for emphasis, but not in mid position. Note that when\nthese adverbs are in front position there is no inversion of subject and verb:\n\n© L went to Paris yesterday. (or Yesterday I went to Paris.)\n\n© We meet for lunch once a week. (or Once a week we meet for lunch.)\n\nNote that the adverbs daily, hourly, monthly, weekly, annually, quarterly (= four times a year),\netc. only go in end position:\n\n© The train leaves hourly. (not Hourly the train leaves; not The train hourly leaves.)\n\n© Lpay my subscription annually, (not Annually I pay...; ot I annually pay...)\n\nExercises Unit 76\n\n76.1\n\nRewrite the sentences with the underlined adverbs of place or direction at the front of the\nclause. O I really hate coffee. © He almost always arrived late.\nSome degree adverbs, such as almost, largely, really and virtually, are usually used before the\nmain verb, and others, such as altogether, enormously, somewhat, and tremendously, are usually\nused after the main verb. Degree adverbs are rarely used in front position (see Unit 75B).\n\nFocus adverbs draw attention to the most important part of what we are talking about. Some\n(e.g. especially, even, mainly, mostly, particularly, specifically) make what we say more specific:\n© There is likely to be snow today, particularly in the north.\nand others (e.g. alone, just, only, simply, solely) limit what we say to one thing or person:\n© Many people offered to help me invest the money, but I only trusted Peter.\n\nMuch and very much\n\nIn affirmative sentences in formal contexts, much can be used as a degree adverb before the\n\nverbs admire, appreciate, enjoy, prefer and regret to emphasise how we feel about things:\n© I much enjoyed having you stay with us. © Their music is much admired.\n\nMuch is used in this way particularly after | and we and (with admire and appreciate) in passives.\nNote that we don't usually use this pattern in questions (e.g. not ‘Did you much enjoy...?’).\n\nWe can use very much in a similar way before these verbs and also before agree, doubt, fear, hope,\n\nlike and want. Notice, however, that we don’t use much before this last group of verbs. Compare:\n© I much prefer seeing films at the cinema than on television. (or I very much prefer...) and\n©) We very much agree with the decision. (or We agree very much...; but not ...much agree...)\n\nWe can also use much or very much before a past participle which is part of a passive:\n\n© The new by-pass was (very) much needed.\n\nWe don’t use much but can use very much before past participle adjectives (see Unit 69A):\nShe was (very) interested in the news. (or ... very much interested...; but not much\ninterested...)\n\nand we don’t use either much or very much before present participle adjectives:\n\n© The hotel was (very) welcoming. (but not The hotel was (very) much welcoming.)\n\nIn negative sentences in informal contexts we can use (very) much before verbs such as\nappreciate, enjoy, like, and look forward to to emphasise a negative feeling about something:\n© I didn’t (very) much enjoy the film.\n\nVery and too\nBefore an adjective or another adverb we use very when we mean ‘to a high degree’, and too\nwhen we mean ‘more than enough’ or ‘more than is wanted or needed’. Compare:\n\n© The weather was very hot in Majorca. Perfect for swimming. (not...too hot...) and\n\n© It’s too hot to stay in this room — let’s find somewhere cooler. (not ...very hot...)\n\nIn negative sentences in informal spoken English we can use not too to mean ‘not very’:\n© I'm not too bothered about who wins. (or I’m not very bothered...)\n\nEven, only and alone\nEven and only usually go in mid position (see Unit 75), but if they refer to the subject they\nusually come before it. Compare:\n© My mother has only brought some food. (= She hasn’t brought anything else) and\n1) Only my mother has brought some food. (= My mother and nobody else)\n) Sue can even speak French. (= in addition to everything else she can do) and\n© Even Sue can speak French. (= you might not expect her to) (rather than Sue even...)\n\nWhen alone means that only one thing or person is involved, it comes after a noun:\n5 You alone should decide what is right for you.\n\nExercises Unit 77\n\n771\n\n77.2\n\n77.3\n\nWhich of these can go in the spaces: very, much, very much? At the beginning and end of sentences we usually separate them from the rest of the\nsentence by a comma in writing or by intonation in speech:\n\n© Presumably, he didn’t hear me when I called.\n\n© The book was based on his experience in China, apparently.\n\n© Jackson believes that child development can be slowed down by poor nutrition. This is\n\nundoubtedly the case.\n\nComment adverbs which show judgement usually follow the subject, although they can be put\nin front position for emphasis:\n© He kindly offered to take me to the station. (or Kindly, he offered... to emphasise\n‘Kindly’)\nIf comment adverbs apply to only part of the sentence they can be used in other positions.\nCompare:\n© Astonishingly, she did well in the exam. (= I was surprised that she did well)\n© She did astonishingly well in the exam. (= she did very well)\n© You've had a major operation. Obviously, it will be very painful for a while. (= I expect\nyou to know this already)\n© When he stood up it was obviously very painful. (= the pain was clear to see)\n\nSome adverbs are used to make clear what viewpoint we are speaking from; that is, identifying\nwhat features of something are being talked about:\n\n© Financially, the accident has been a disaster for the owners of the tunnel.\n\n© The brothers may be alike physically, but they have very different personalities.\nOther examples include biologically, environmentally, financially, ideologically, industrially,\nlogically, medically, morally, outwardly, politically, technically, visually.\n\nA number of phrases are used in a similar way. For example:\n\npolitically speaking in political terms in terms of politics\nfrom a political point of view _ as far as politics are concerned\n\n© Politically/In political terms, this summer is a crucial time for the government.\nO Financially/From a financial point of view, it is a good investment.\n\nSome adverbs or phrases are used to say whose viewpoint we are expressing:\n© The head of National North Bank is to receive, according to newspaper reports, a 50%\nsalary increase.\nO In my view, the Foreign Minister should resign immediately.\nOther examples include to my/his/her (etc.) knowledge, from my/his/her (etc.) perspective,\npersonally, in my/his/her (etc.) opinion.\n\nExercises Unit 78\n\n78.1\n\n78.2\n\n78.3\n\nChoose a comment adverb to replace the underlined part. In the second sentence ‘as’ or ‘while’\nwould be very unlikely because lights usually go out instantaneously.\n\nWe prefer as to say that when one thing changes, another thing changes at the same time:\nO As the cheese matures, its flavour improves. (rather than When the cheese...)\nWe can also use ‘While...’, particularly with a continuous tense: ‘While the cheese is maturing...’.\n\nWe prefer while or as (rather than when) to talk about two longer actions that go on at the\nsame time, although while is more common than as in informal speech:\n© I went shopping while Linda cleaned the house. (or ...as Linda cleaned...)\n\nWe use while or when (rather than as) to avoid ambiguity where ‘as’ could mean ‘because’:\n© While you were playing golf, I went to the cinema. (‘As you were playing golf...’ could\nmean ‘Because you were playing golf...\")\n\nBefore, after and until\nWe use before or after to talk about an event happening earlier or later than another event:\n© [put on my coat before I went out. © The message arrived after I'd left.\n\nWe can often use either until or before when a situation continues to happen up to a time\nindicated in the adverbial clause:\n\n© Thad to wait six weeks until/before the parcel arrived.\nWe use until to talk about an action that continues to a particular time and then stops:\n\n© They sat on the beach until the sun sank below the horizon, and then they went home.\nand when the adverbial clause describes the result of an action in the main clause:\n\n© He cleaned his shoes until they shone. (‘shining’ is the result of ‘cleaning’.)\n\nHardly, no sooner, scarcely\nWhen we say that one event happened immediately after another we can use sentences with\nhardly, no sooner, and scarcely (see also Unit 100). After hardly and scarcely the second clause\nbegins with when or before; after no sooner it begins with than or when:\n\n© The concert had hardly begun before all the lights went out.\n\n0 Thad no sooner lit the barbecue than/when it started to rain.\nWe often use a past perfect in the clause with hardly, no sooner or scarcely and a past simple in\nthe other.\nGrammar review — M1-M8\n\nxercises Unit 79\n\nBy\n\n79.2\n\nChoose as, when or while, whichever is correct or more likely, to complete these sentences. (not\n«Vague not to commit himself...)\nThe land was bought quickly so as not to delay the building work. (not ...quickly not to\ndelay...)\nHowever, in contrastive sentences we can use not + to-infinitive, but + to-infinitive as in:\n© I came to see you not (in order/so as) to complain, but (in order/so as) to apologise.\nNotice that we can put in order/so as before the to-infinitives in sentences like this.\n\nIn order that and so that\n‘We also use in order that and so that to talk about a purpose. Compare:\n) She stayed at work late in order/so as to complete the report. and\n) She stayed at work late in order that/so that she could complete the report. (not ...in\norder that/so that to complete the report.)\nSo that is more common than in order that, and is used in less formal situations. Note that\ninformally we can leave out that after so, but we always include it after in order.\n\nA present tense verb in the main clause is usually followed by a present tense verb (or a modal\nwith present or future reference - can, will, etc.) in the clause beginning in order that/so that. A\npast tense verb in the main clause is usually followed by a past tense verb (or a modal with past\nreference — could, would, etc.) in the clause beginning in order that/so that. Modal verbs are\nvery often used after in order that/so that:\n\n© Regular checks are made in order that safety standards are maintained.\n\nAdvice is given in order that students can choose the best course.\n) Did you give up you job so that you could take care of your mother?\n© I hid the presents so that Jackie wouldn't find them.\n\nSuch that and in such a way that; such...that\nIn formal contexts, such as academic writing, we can use such that to introduce a result:\n© The model was designed such that the value of x could be calculated. (= ‘in a way that\nhas the result that...’; or ...in order that...; or ...so that...)\nLess formally we can also use in such a way that or in such a way as + to-infinitive with a\nsimilar meaning:\n} The advertisement is printed in such a way that two very different pictures can be seen\ndepending on how you look at it.\nC1 In fact, the tax cuts have been designed in such a way as to leave out the very people it is\nsupposed to help.\n\nWe can also use such + noun phrase + that to introduce a result:\n© Iris such a popular play that the performance was sold out after the first day.\n(For so + adjective/adverb + that, see Unit 73.)\n\nExercises Unit 81\n\n81.1\n\n| 81.2\n\n81.3\n\n81.4\n\nCombine the two sentences in the most appropriate way using in order (not) + to-infinitive or\nso as (not) + to-infinitive. I’m not keen on yogurt, though.\n\nWe can give special emphasis to an adjective by putting it before though in the pattern adjective\n+ though + noun/pronoun + verb (usually a linking verb such as appear, be, become, feel, look,\nseem, sound, prove, etc.). As can be used instead of though, but notice that you can’t use\nalthough. Compare:\n\n© Hot though (or as) the night air was, they slept soundly. and\n\nO Although/Though the night air was hot, they slept soundly.\n\nEven though and even if\nWe can use even though (but not ‘even although’) to mean ‘despite the fact that’ and even if to\nmean ‘whether or not’. Spanish\n\n© Even if Tom doesn’t speak = Whether or not he Le. the speaker doesn’t\nSpanish, I think he should still speaks Spanish know definitely whether\nvisit Madrid. Tom speaks Spanish or not\n\nWhile, whilst and whereas\nIn formal contexts we can use while or whilst with a meaning similar to ‘although’ to introduce\nsomething that qualifies what is said in the main clause or something that may seem to conflict\nwith it. In this case, the while/whilst clause comes before or within the main clause, but not after it:\n© While/Whilst there is no evidence that Peter cheated, we were all astonished that he\npassed the exam. (not We were all astonished that he passed the exam, while...)\n(0 The diesel model of the car, while/whilst more expensive, is better value for money.\nNote that whilst is a rather literary word and some people avoid using it.\n\nWe can use while or whereas (or less often whilst) to say that something contrasts with something\nin the main clause. The while/whereas clause may come before or after the main clause:\n\n© Dave gets lots of homework from school, while/whereas Sue gets very little.\n© While/Whereas | always felt | would pass the exam, I never thought I would get an A grade.\n\nWe don’t use whereas where what is said in the subordinate clause makes what is said in the\nmain clause unexpected:\nAlthough/While Sophie’s father is from Spain, she doesn’t speak Spanish. (not Whereas...)\n\nWe can use -ing and past participle (-ed) clauses after although, though, while and whilst, and\nalso clauses with the subject and verb left out (see Unit 59D): |\n© (Aljthough not huge, the garden needs constant attention. (or If you’re leaving now...)\nHowever, in conversation we can use be going to instead of a present tense verb:\nIf I’m going to catch the train, Ill have to leave now,\nWe'll need more chairs if we’re going to invite so many people to the performance.\n\nWhen we make offers, and give instructions or advice we can use an imperative in the main clause:\n‘Take another sandwich if you’re hungry.\n© If you have a mobile phone, check that it is turned off.\n\nWe can use if-clauses with a present tense verb to introduce certain conditions under which\nsomething is true:\n\n© The video pauses if you press this button.\n\n© If age-related changes are taken into account, the conclusion remains the same.\n(Here ‘if? has a meaning similar to ‘when’.)\n\nWe can talk about possible future events with a present perfect verb in the #f-clause and a future\nform (will, present continuous, or be going to) in the main clause. Sometimes present perfect or\npresent simple can be used with a similar meaning:\n© Vlllend you War and Peace if I've finished it before you go on holiday. (or ...if I finish...)\n© If you haven't paid the bill by Friday, we're taking the carpets back. (or If you don't pay...)\nHowever, to focus on the future consequences of a past event, we use the present perfect. Compare:\n© If I’ve failed my maths exam again, I’m going to give up the course. (suggests I have\nalready taken the exam; I don’t know the result) and\nIf I fail my maths exam again, I’m going to give up the course. (I may or may not have\ntaken the exam)\n\nUnreal conditionals\n\nIn unreal conditionals (see GR note above M9) we can use if...were + to-infinitive rather than if\n+ past simple to talk about imaginary future situations, particularly to suggest it is unlikely that\nthe situation in the if-clause will happen (see also Unit 14):\n\n© If the technology were to become available, we would be able to expand the business.\nHowever, notice that we don’t usually this pattern with verbs such as belong, doubt, enjoy,\nknow, like, remember, and understand when they describe a state:\n\n© If I knew they were honest, I'd gladly lend them the money. (not If I were to know...)\n\nWe sometimes use this pattern to make a suggestion sound more polite:\n© Would it be too early for you if we were to meet at 5.30?\n\nWe use if it was not for + noun phrase (or more formally if it were not for + noun phrase) to\nsay that one situation is dependent on another situation or on a person (see also Unit 85A).\nWhen we talk about the past we can also use if it had not been for + noun phrase: |\n> If it wasn’t/weren’t for Vivian, the conference wouldn’t be going ahead.\n© If it wasn’t/weren’t for the fact that Chloe would be offended, I wouldn’t go to the party.\n© If it hadn't been for Dad, I wouldn’t have gone to college. (or If it wasn’t/weren't for...)\nIn formal language we can also use Were it not for... and Had it not been for... (see Unit 84B):\nWere it not for Vivian... © Had it not been for Dad...\n\nWe can use but for + noun with a similar meaning, particularly in formal contexts:\nThe village school would have been closed years ago but for the determination of\nteachers and parents to keep it open. (= ...if it hadn’t been for the determination...)\nGrammar review: real and unreal conditionals —* M9-M17\n\nExercises Unit 83\n\n83.1\n\n83.2\n\n83.3\n\n83.4\n\n3 If you see John today,\n\nCUYUDAHAWN\n\noy\n\nSuggest completions for these sentences. (= I want to speak) I think it’s important to...\nO If we could get back to the subject of homework. Why haven't you done any for the last\nweek? (= Let’s go back to talking about homework)\nand to qualify what someone else has said. In this case if is usually stressed:\nOC A: Bill won’t be happy if he finds out you've taken his bike. B: If he finds out.\n(= shhe doesn’t necessarily agree that Bill will find out, or s/he will make sure that he doest\n\nWhen the first verb in a conditional if-clause is should, were, or had we can leave out if and put\nthe verb at the start of the clause (see Units 99 and 100 for more on inversion). We do this\nparticularly in formal or literary English, and only in hypothetical conditionals (a type of unreal\nconditional which answers the question ‘What would happen if...?’):\n\nCO Should any of this cost you anything, send me the bill. (= If any of this should cost...)\n\n© It would be embarrassing, were she to find out the truth. (= ...if she were to find out..)\n\n© Had they not rushed Jo to hospital, he would have died. (= If they hadn’t rushed Jo...)\n\nWe don’t usually use if...will in conditional clauses. However, we can use if...will -\n¥ when we talk about a result of something in the main clause. Compare:\nOG Open a window if it will help you to sleep. (or ...if it helps you to sleep; ‘Helping you to\nsleep’ is the result of opening the window) and\nOC IT will be angry if it turns out that you are wrong. (mot ...if it will turn out...; ‘Turning out\nthat you are wrong’ is not the result of being angry)\nvr in requests or with the meaning ‘if you are willing to’ (or if...would to be more polite):\n0D If you will/would take your seats, ladies and gentlemen, we can begin the meeting.\n% in real conditionals when we want to show that we disapprove of something. In this case,\nwill is stressed in speech (see also Unit 16B):\nGa: P'mtired. 8: Well, if you will go to bed so late, I’m not surprised.\n\nNotice that we can use if...won’t when we talk about a refusal to do something:\nU There’s no point in trying to teach the class if they won't pay attention.\n\nIn a real conditional sentence (see GR note above M9), we use if...happen to, if...should, or\nif...should happen to to talk about something which may be possible, but is not very likely.\nIf...khappen to is most common in spoken English:\n\n© If you happen to be in our area, drop in and see us. (or If you should (happen to) be..)\nNotice that we don’t usually use this pattern in unreal conditionals talking about states or\nevents in the if-clause which the speaker perceives as highly unlikely or impossible:\n\nO If the North Sea froze in winter, you could walk from London to Oslo, (but probably\n\nnot If the North Sea happened to freeze/should (happen to) freeze in winter...)\n\nIn comparison clauses we can use as if followed by a noun phrase, -ing clause, past participle (-ed)\nclause, or to-infinitive to introduce a comparison with a situation described in the main clause. We\ndo this to give an explanation or to say that something appears to be the case but is not:\n\nO Richard walked in as if nothing had happened.\n\nO His hands made a circular motion, as if steering a bus through a sharp bend.\n\n0 After scoring, Fowler fell to the floor as if bit by a bullet.\n\n0 As if to convince herself that Rob was really there, she gently touched his cheek.\nNotice that we can use as though instead of as if, and in informal speech some people use like\nwith the same meaning:\n\nQ The crowd reacted as though they were watching a boxing match. (or ...as if...)\n\nO He walked into the room like nothing had happened. (or ...as if...)\n\nGrammar review: real and unreal conditionals —- M9-M11, M17\n\nExercises Unit 84\n\n84.1\n\n84.2\n\n84.3\n\n84.4\n\nWrite a new sentence with a similar meaning to the sentence given. This use of\nwere is sometimes called the past subjunctive, and is generally preferred only in formal contexts.\nNote that although the verb has a past form, reference is to the imagined present or future:\n\n© If your mother were here, I’m sure she wouldn’t let you eat all those chocolates.\n\n1 We would not be able to provide after-school sports if it were not for dedicated teachers\n\nlike Mr Morgan.\n\nWas can be used instead of were with the same meaning (‘If your mother was here.\nHowever, we prefer were rather than was when we give advice with If I were you...:\n\n© If I were you, I'd take it back to the shop. It’s got a hole in it. (rather than If | was you...)\n\n, ete.).\n\nWere is used in this way in other patterns when we talk about imaginary situations -\n(i) when we use were + subject + to-infinitive or were + subject as a more formal alternative to\nif + subject + was/were (see also Unit 84B):\n© Were the election to be held today, the Liberals would win easily. (or If the election was/\nwere held today...)\n© Were I not in my 70s and rather unfit, I might consider taking up squash. (or If I wasn’t/\nweren't in my 70s and rather unfit...)\n\n(ii) after wish:\n© Lenjoy my job enormously, but I wish it were closer to home. (or ...1 wish it was...)\n© Of course I’m pleased that James has been given the award. I only wish he weren’t so\nboastful about it. (or ...] only wish he wasn’t...)\n\n(iti) after if only when we express our regret that a situation isn’t different: -\nC1 ‘If your job is so bad, why don’t you leave?’ ‘If only it were that simple.’ (or If only it was...)\nOM d really like to do accounting. If only I weren’t so poor at maths. (or If only I wasn’t...)\n\n(iv) after would (’d) rather and would (’d) sooner when we talk about preferences:\nOI feel embarrassed about what happened and would rather the event were forgotten. (or\n«..Was forgotten.)\n© ‘T’ve arranged a meeting for the end of July.’ ‘I’d sooner it were earlier, if possible. (or ...it\nwas earlier.)\n\n(v) in sentences or clauses beginning with suppose, supposing and imagine:\n© Suppose | were to lower the price by £100. Would you consider buying the car then?\n(or Suppose | was to lower...)\n© I know it looks rather dirty now, but imagine the house were (to be) repainted. It would\nlook a lot more attractive. (or ...imagine the house was (to be) repainted.)\n\nAnd in comparisons we can use were -\n(vi) after as if and as though (see Unit 84E) and even if:\n© I remember stepping off the boat in New York as if it were yesterday.\n© Despite losing the election, she continues to act as though she were prime minister.\n© It would be too late to start work on the building this year even if it were possible to find\nthe money for it.\n\nWe can use the phrase as it were to show that we realise that something we have said may not\nprecisely express what we mean. Often (but not always) these go at the beginning of the sentence:\n© There was no heating in the building. As a result, the workers had to be sent home. (or\nThe workers had to be sent home as a result.)\nOther words and phrases (conjunctions) are used to connect clauses within a single sentence:\nG While I was waiting, I read a magazine.\nOC I'll be wearing a red jumper so that you can see me easily.\n\nWe can’t use a comma to connect ideas joined by a sentence connector. However, these words and\nphrases can be used to connect two clauses in one sentence if the clauses are joined with and, but,\nor, so, or a semi-colon (;), colon (:), or dash (-):\n\n© The building was extremely well constructed and, as a result, difficult to demolish.\n‘ou could fly via Singapore; however, this isn’t the only way.\n\ntype of connection example sentence connectors example conjunctions\n\ncomparing, contrasting, | after all, all the same, alternatively, anyway, although, even\n\nand indicating that a by contrast, even so, however (but see D), in| though, though,\n\nsituation (in the main | any case, in contrast, instead, nevertheless, whereas, while, yet\n\nclause) is unexpected on the contrary, on the other hand\n\nreasons and results as a consequence, as a result, consequently, as, because, for, in\nfor one thing, so; hence, in consequence, that, since, insofar as,\n\ntherefore, thus (the last four are rather formal) | so, so that\n— t\n\nadding information above all, after all, also, besides, furthermore,\nin addition, likewise, moreover, similarly,\nwhat's more; as well, too (the last two are\nnot used at the beginning of a sentence)\n\ncondition if not, if so, otherwise as long as, assuming\n(that), if, on condition\nthat, provided (that),\nso long as, supposing\n\n= (that), unless ]\ntime: one event at the | at that time, at the same time, meanwhile as, when, whenever,\nsame time as another while 1\ntime: one event before | after, after that, afterwards, before, before after, as soon as,\nor after another that, earlier, later, previously, soon, before, since, until\n\nsubsequently, then\n\nNote that after, before and so can be both connectors and conjunctions.\n\nEven though is a conjunction used to say that a fact doesn’t make the rest of the sentence\nuntrue (see also Unit 82B). It connects ideas within a sentence:\n\n© Even though it was midday, I put on the light.\nEven so is a sentence connector used to introduce a fact that is surprising in the context of wha\nwas just said. It connects ideas between sentences:\n\n0 It was midday. Even so, I put on the light.\n\nHowever is often used as a sentence connector, but it can also be used -—\nvw as an adverb when it is followed by an adjective, adverb, or much/many:\n© We just don’t have the money to do the work, however necessary you think it is.\n* as a conjunction when it means ‘in whatever way’:\n© However she held the mirror, she couldn’t see the back of her neck.\n\nExercises Unit 87\n\n87.1\n\n«87.2\n\n87.3\n\nChoose items from (i) and from (ii) to complete these sentences in an appropriate way. Begin however + adjective/adverb/many/much.\n\n(0)\n\n1 She is determined to be a successful artist, however difficult it might be to achieve\n\n2 » it is difficult to lose weight without cutting down on\nthe amount you eat.\n\n3 , it is important to spend some time apart.\n\n4 Professor Malcolm is always happy to spend time with his students,\n\n175\n\nAcross, over\nWe can use across or over to talk about a position on the other side of, or moving to the other\nside of a road, bridge, border, river, etc.:\n\n© Mike lives in the house across/over the road from ours.\n\n© The truck came towards them across/over the bridge.\n\n© Once she was across/over the border, she knew she would be safe.\n\nWe use over rather than across when we talk about reaching the other side of something that is\nhigh, or higher than it is wide, Compare:\n© He jumped over the fence into the garden. and © He jumped across the stream.\n\nWhen we are talking about something we think of as a flat surface, or an area such as a country\nor sea, we prefer across rather than over:\n© He suddenly saw Sue across the room. ) The figures moved rapidly across the screen.\nG The programme was breadcast across Australia,\n\nWe prefer all over rather than all across to mean ‘to or in many different parts of an area’.\nHowever, we commonly use across, or right across for emphasis:\n© The disease has now spread all over the world. (or ...(right) across the world.)\n\nAlong, through\nWhen we talk about following a line of some kind (a road, a river, etc.), we use along:\n© They walked along the footpath until they came to a small bridge.\n\nWe use through to emphasise that we are talking about movement in a three dimensional space,\nwith things all around, rather than a two dimensional space, a flat surface or area:\n\n© He pushed his way through the crowd of people to get to her.\nThrough often suggests movement from one side or end of the space to the other. Compare:\n\n© She walked through the forest to get to her grandmother’s house. and\n\n© She spent a lot of her free time walking in the forest.\n\nAbove, over; below, under; beneath, underneath; throughout\n\nWe can use either above or over when we say that one thing is at a higher level than another:\n© Above/Over the door was a sign saying, ‘Mind your head’.\n\nHowever, we prefer above, when one thing is not directly over the other. Compare:\n© They lived in a village in the mountains above the lake. (not directly over) and\n© The bird hovered just a few metres above/over the lake. (directly over)\n\nWe use over, not above, when something covers something else and touches it:\nO She put a quilt over the bed.\n\nand usually when we are talking about horizontal movement at a higher level than something:\n© I saw the helicopter fly out over the water, near the fishing boat.\n\nBelow is the opposite of above; under is the opposite of over. The differences in the uses of\nbelow and under are similar to those between above and over (see above):\n© It’s hard to believe that there is a railway line below/under the building. (at a lower level)\n© Her head was below the level of the table so nobody noticed her. (not directly under)\nCO She hid the presents under a blanket. (the blanket covers and touches the presents)\n© Esther ran under the bridge. (horizontal movement at a lower level)\nWe can use underneath as an alternative to under as a preposition of place. Beneath is\nsometimes used as a more formal alternative to under or below.\n\nWe can use throughout to emphasise that something is in every part of a place (see also Unit 90A};\n© The flower is found throughout the island. © The same laws apply throughout Europe,\n\n.1 Complete the sentences with across or over, whichever is correct or more likely. Among is only used with three or more people or things:\n\n0 She held the diamond between her thumb and forefinger.\n\n0 Zimbabwe is situated between Zambia to the north, Mozambique to the east, Botswana\n\nto the west, and South Africa to the south.\n© He stood among all his friends in the room and felt very happy.\n( She eventually found her passport among the clothes in the drawer.\n\nNote that amongst is sometimes used instead of among, but in more literary contexts.\n\nBetween and among are not only used as prepositions of place. For example, we can use either\nto talk about something divided or shared between people:\n\n© The money is to be divided between/among the towns in the area.\n\n© The prize will be shared between/among the first six finishers in the race.\n\nWe also use between -\n\nto talk about comparisons and relationships (e.g. with the words association, balance,\ncomparison, connection, contrast, correlation, difference, distinction, link, relationship):\n© There should be a better balance on the committee between the various ethnic groups.\nO They are wrong to claim that there is a link between unemployment and crime.\n\n% to talk about choices:\nO Ihave to choose between the universities of Leeds, York and Manchester.\nO He felt torn between his family and his friends.\n\n* to talk about discussions or the results of discussions when we specify the two or more\npeople or groups involved:\n\n) There was a disagreement between Neil, John and Margaret.\n\nO The treaty was signed between Great Britain and France,\n\n* to say that people or things share an amount of something:\nC Between them, Ray and Ingrid must earn about £100,000 a year.\nO Last year the three companies built 30,000 houses between them.\n\nWe also use among -\nvw when we mean ‘existing or happening in a particular group’:\nO The disease has now broken out among the hill tribes.\nO Their music is still very popular among young teenagers.\n% when we mean ‘included in a particular group’:\n© They are among the best hockey players in the world.\n© Among the capital cities of South America, Quito is the second highest.\n\nThere are a number of common expressions using between and among, including among others\n(= what is said applies to other people or things, too), among other things (= including other\nthings), between ourselves (= this should be kept secret), and between you and me (= this should\nbe kept secret):\n0 The concert features, among others, Karl Frisk and the Johnsons. (= other singers/groups\nare featured, too)\n© [later found out that he had been a carpenter and a dustman, among other things. (= he\nhad had other jobs, too)\n© Between ourselves (= keep this a secret), | don’t think Tom is as honest as he should be.\nCO I’ve got a maths exam next week, but between you and me (= but keep this a secret) I\nhaven't done any revision yet.\n\nercises Unit 89\n\n89.1 Underline the correct answer. (or ...in the summer.)\n\nO The population of the city has actually fallen during the last decade. (or ...in the last...)\nor the event may happen at some time, or be repeated a number of times, in the period of time:\n\nOC We went to France during the summer. (or ...in the summer.)\n\n© He suffered a number of injuries during his career as a footballer. (or ...in his career...)\n\nWe use during, rather than in, to mean ‘at some time in the period of’ before nouns such as\nillness, holiday, meal, stay, treatment, and visit, when we refer to an event which lasts some\n© The President made the speech during a visit to Madrid.\nand also with the phrase the whole (of), emphasising duration of an entire period:\n© No-one was allowed to leave the ship during the whole of its time in port.\n\nWe can use throughout to emphasise that something happens over the whole of a period of time:\nO We had enough firewood to keep us warm throughout the winter. (or ...warm during/\nthrough the winter.)\n\nWe can use over or during when we talk about something that goes on for a length of time\nwithin a period of time, cither for some of that period or for the whole of it:\n\n© Weather conditions have been improving over/ during the past few days.\n\nOI fell, banged my head, and can't remember anything about what happened over/ during\nthe next hour or so.\n\nHowever, if we talk about a short event that happens within a period of time, we prefer during:\n© She sneezed during the performance. (= on one or a few occasions; not ...over the\nperformance.)\n© During a pause in the conversation, she left the room. (not Over a pause...)\n\nNote that we can use throughout to mean that short events happen continuously within a\nperiod of time:\n© She sneezed throughout the performance. |\n\nUntil, by, up to\nThere are a number of ways of saying when something that has continued for some time stops -\n\ns+ We use until to say that something continued or will continue to a particular time, and\nby to say that something happened or will happen either before a particular time or at that\ntime at the latest. Compare:\nOC We have to be at home until 2.30. (We must not leave home before 2.30) and\n© We have to be at home by 2.30. (We must arrive home either before or at 2.30)\nNote that till can be used instead of until. It is particularly common in conversation, but\nrarely found in formal writing.\n\n* In informal contexts we can use up to or up till instead of until. We commonly use up to/till\nwith now and with then:\nG I’ve just bought a computer. I’ve always used a typewriter up to now. (or ...up till now...)\nO The roadworks are likely to go on up to the end of May. (or ...up till the end...)\n\nx We can use until now to talk about a situation that will not continue beyond now:\n© Supermarkets say that until now there has been little consumer interest in buying organic\n\nproduce. (The situation has changed or is going to change)\n\nNotice, however, that we don’t use until now for a situation that will or may continue into\nthe future. Instead we can use so far or, in formal contexts, to date:\nCO It was certainly the best match of the football season so far.\n© When the contract is signed it will be the building company’s biggest order to date.\n\na]\n\nercises Unit 90\n\nIn which of the sentences can the word in brackets replace during? However, informally except for is sometimes also used,\nalthough this is grammatically incorrect:\n\n© There is likely to be rain everywhere today except in Wales.\n\nO There is nothing more the doctor can do except keep an eye on him.\n\n© They look just like the real thing, except (that) they’te made of plastic.\n\nWe can use except for, but not except, with the meaning ‘but for’ (see C below).\n\nWe use except (for) to mean that something is not included in a particular statement, but we use\nbesides to mean ‘as well as’ or ‘in addition to’. Compare:\n\nO I don’t enjoy watching any sports except (for) cricket. (= | enjoy only cricket) and\n\nO Besides cricket, I enjoy watching football and basketball. (= I enjoy three sports)\n\n© Thaven’t read anything by her, except (for) one of her short stories. and\n\nOC Besides her novels and poems, she published a number of short stories.\n\nApart from can be used with the same meanings as both except (for) and besides:\n0 I don’t enjoy watching any sports apart from cricket. (= except for)\n© Apart from cricket, I enjoy watching football and basketball. (= besides; as well as)\n\nWe can use but with a similar meaning to except (for), particularly after negative words such as\nno, nobody, and nothing:\nO Immediately after the operation he could see nothing but/except (for)/apart from vague\nshadows.\nO There was no way out but/excepv/apart from upwards, towards the light.\n\nBut for has a different meaning from except for. We use but for to say what would or might\nhave happened if the thing introduced by but for had not happened:\n© The country would now be self-sufficient in food but for the drought last year. (= if there\nhadn’t been the drought...)\n© But for the leg injury he suffered last year, he would probably have been picked for the\nnational team by now. (= if he hadn’t injured his leg...)\nHowever, some people use except for in the same way as but for, particularly in informal\nspoken English:\nOd have got there on time except for the taxi being late. (or ...but for the taxi being late.)\n0 Except for the problems with my computer, I would have got the book finished weeks\nago. (or But for the problems with my computer...)\n\nWe can use excepted, apart or aside after mentioning a person or thing to say that they are not\nincluded in a statement that we make:\nO It has been, 1984 excepted/aparv/aside, the hottest July for the last 100 years.\n0 Tom had difficulties with question two. This excepted/apart/aside, he did very well in the\nexam.\n\nExercises\n\n91.1\n\nUnit 91\n\nComplete the sentences with except or except for. Indicate where both are possible. In these cases, there is not stressed:\n\n© There was a loud bang from upstairs. (not A loud bang was from upstairs.)\n\n© There’s nothing to eat. (not Nothing is to eat.)\nWe invert this pattern in questions to ask about the existence etc. of people and things:\n\nO Is there anybody in here?\n\nWe can also use there with auxiliary and modal verbs with be (e.g. has been, can be):\n© There must be some way of contacting her.\nwith verbs and to be (e.g. used to be, is supposed to be, tends to be, appears to be, seems to be):\n© There appears to be a major disagreement between the two presidents.\nand some other verbs that indicate existence (c.g. arise, emerge, exist, remain):\n) During the 1990s there arose a demand for organic food.\n\nBecause we use there in this way to introduce topics, the noun after there + be often has an\nindefinite or non-specific meaning. So we often use a/an, zero article, any(one) (+ noun), or\nsome(thing), no(body), etc. rather than the, this, my, your (+ noun), or a name, which give the\nnoun a more definite or specific meanitig. Compare:\n\n© There’s nobody here. © There was something strange about her. and\n\n© The cat was in the kitchen. (more usual than There was the cat in the kitchen.; but\n\ncompare There was a cat in the kitchen.)\n) Sam is waiting for me outside. (more usual than There is Sam waiting for me outside.)\n\nWhen we use there + be + the, this is often done to show a change of topic. Choosing the, that, —\netc, + noun indicates that we think the topic is already known to the listener or reader: |\n© ...And then there is the question of who is going to pay. |\n\nIf the noun after be is singular or uncountable, the verb is singular; if the noun is plural, the\nverb is plural:\n© There is a very good reason tor my decision. |\n© There were too many people trying to get into the football stadium. :\nHowever, in informal speech we sometimes use there’s before a plural noun:\n© ‘Anything to eat?’ ‘Well, there’s some apples on the table.’\n\nIf the noun phrase consists of two or more nouns in a list, we use a singular verb if the first\nnoun is singular or uncountable, and a plural verb if the first noun is plural:\n© When I opened the fridge there was only a bottle of milk, some eggs, and butter.\nWhen I opened the fridge there were only some eggs, a bottle of milk, and butter.\n\nThere + be is also used with nouns followed by a that-, wh-, to-infinitive or -ing clause:\no Is there a chance (that) Kim could arrive this afternoon?\nO There is no reason (why) | can’t see you tomorrow.\n© There is a small stream which/that runs at the bottom of the garden.\n© There was an attempt to resolve the dispute at the factory.\n© There was a taxi waiting outside the hotel.\n\nWe don’t usually leave out a relative pronoun when it is a subject of the following finite verb\n(e.g. There is a small stream which/that runs...) except in very informal speech (e.g. There was\nman (who) phoned about half eleven.), but we can leave out a relative pronoun when it is the\nobject of the following finite verb (e.g. There is a small stream (that) the children play in.).\n\nIn formal English we can use a clause with there being to introduce a reason for something:\n© There being no evidence against him, he was released. (= Because there was no evidence...)\n© There being no reports of adverse reactions, the drug is to be sold more widely.\n\nExercises Unit 95\n\n95.1\n\n95.2\n\n95.4\n\nRewrite these sentences using there only if the answer is likely. Compare:\nTo drive without a licence is illegal. and © It is illegal to drive without a licence.\n% to focus attention on a sentence element by placing this element at the end (the usual place\nfor new or important information in English). Compare:\nO That she wasn’t burt isa miracle. and © It’s a miracle that she wasn't hurt.\n\nIntroductory it is commonly used when the subject is a to-infinitive or that-clause (as in the\nexamples above), and also when the subject is a wh- or -ing clause (see also Unit 25):\nOC Itis clear why Don decided to leave Spain. OC Itis useless asking Sue to help.\n\nWe often use introductory it with be + adjective/noun (as in the examples above), but other\npatterns with an introductory it are possible. Here are some common examples -\n+: it + verb + to-infinitive clause\n© ‘I’ve got a terrible headache.’ ‘It helps to lie down.’\no If you want someone to help you, it doesn’t do to annoy them just before you ask. (‘(not)\ndo’ = (not) advisable, acceptable or enough)\nOther verbs used in this pattern are hurt and pay (= give an advantage or benefit).\n\n% it + verb + object + to-infinitive clause\n© It shocked him to see her looking so ill. (4 It annoys me to bear James swear like that.\n1 It costs a fortune to go to the opera. © It means a lot to get a place at university.\n\nMost of the verbs used in this pattern are to do with feelings, and include:\namaze, annoy, astonish, concern, frighten, hurt, scare, shock, surprise, upset, worry.\nAfter these verbs the object usually refers to a person.\n\nWe can also use it + take + object + to-infinitive clause when we say what is or was needed\nin a particular activity; for example, the time, resources or characteristics needed. Compare:\nCO It took (them) a week to mend our roof. and = To mend our roof took (them) a week.\nCG It takes a lot of effort to play the flute. and To play the flute takes a lot of effort.\n\nv it + verb + that-clause\n© It seems that she has lost her memory. (nor ...that\nJacks had been convicted of burglary before emerged.)\nOther verbs used in this pattern include appear, come about, follow, happen, transpire.\nNote that alternatives with the that-clause in initial position are not possible.\n\n* it + verb + object + that-clause\n© It suddenly hit me that Jane wanted to borrow money. (less likely is ...that Jane wanted\nto borrow money suddenly hit me.)\n© When Bond saw Vanya taking photographs of the plane, it dawned on hin that she was\na spy. (less likely is...that she was a spy dawned on him.)\nThe object usually refers to a person. Other verbs used in this pattern include those in the\nbox above and also strike (= occur to) and turn out.\n\nWe don’t usually use an it... pattern as an alternative to a noun as subject:\nTheir success was unexpected. (not It was unexpected their success.)\nHowever, in informal contexts, particularly in speech, this is quite common in order to give\nspecial emphasis to the information immediately following it...:\n© It tastes really good, this new ice cream,\nand also to place a longer noun phrase at the end in order to focus attention on it:\nG It’s ridiculous, all the bureaucracy involved in running a school these days.\n\nExercises Unit 96\n\n96.1\n\n96.2\n\n96.3\n\nRewrite these sentences beginning It... . A sentence in which emphasis is given using this pattern is sometimes called a cleft\nsentence. The clause after the it-clause (usually a that-clause) contains information that is\nalready known or considered to be less important:\n\n© ‘Helen bought the car from Tom.’ ‘No, it was Tom that bought the car from Helen.”\n\n© He already plays for the national side, and it was only last year that he turned professional.\n\n© I don’t mind her criticising me, but it’s how she does it that I object to.\n\n© It was to show her how much I cared for her that | bought her the necklace.\n\nWe sometimes use which or who instead of that; when and where can also be used, but usually\nonly in informal English; and note that how or why can’t replace that:\n© Karl was always there to help her, and it was to him thativho she now turned for support.\n© ‘Carol’s seriously ill in hospital.’ ‘But it was only last Sunday when/that I was playing\ntennis with her.’\nOG It was in Bristol where/that the film was made.\n© ‘Was it by cutting staff that he managed to save the firm?’ ‘No, it was by improving\ndistribution that he made it profitable.’ (not ...how he made it profitable.)\n\nFocusing with what-clauses $s\n\nIf we want to focus particular attention on certain information in a sentence, we sometimes use\na what-clause followed by be. Sentences with this pattern are another form of cleft sentence.\nThis pattern is particularly common in conversation. The information we want to focus\nattention on is outside the what-clause. Compare:\n\nQ We gave them some home-made cake. and\n\nCO What we gave them was some home-made cake.\n\nWe often do this if we want to introduce a new topic; to give a reason, instruction or\nexplanation; or to correct something that has been said or done. In the following examples, the\ninformation in focus is in italics:\n© What I'd like you to work on is exercise two on page 38.\n© Tim arrived two hours late: what had happened was that his bicycle chain had broken.\nO ‘We've only got this small bookcase — will that do?’ ‘No, what I was looking for was\nsomething much bigger and stronger.’\n\nWe can often put the what-clause either at the beginning or the end of the sentence:\n(© What upset me most was his rudeness. or His rudeness was what upset me most.\n\nTo focus attention on an action performed by someone, we use a sentence with what + subject +\ndo + be + to-infinitive clause. We can’t use an it-clause to do this (see A):\n© Dave lost his job and was short of money, so what he did was (to) sell his flat and move\nin with his brother. (rot ...s0 it was (to) sell his flat that he did.)\nNotice that the ‘to’ in the infinitive can be omitted.\n\nThe pattern in B is only usually used with what-clauses. Instead of placing other wh-clauses\n(beginning how, when, where, who, why) at the beginning of the sentence we prefer to use a\nnoun which has a meaning related to the wh-word (e.g. reason rather than why; place rather\nthan where) followed by a that- or wh-clause. Here are some examples:\nCO The only reason (why/that) I left the party early was that I was feeling unwell. (rather\nthan Why | left the party early was...)\n> The place (where/that) you should play football is the playground, not the classroom.\n(rather than Where you should play football is...)\n© Somebody (who/that) I enjoy reading is Peter Carey. (not Who I enjoy reading 1s...)\n© The time (when/that) 1 work best is carly morning. (rather than When | work best is...)\n\nExercises Unit 98\n\n98.1\n\n98.2\n\nRewrite the sentences to focus attention on the underlined information. We can refer to this as inversion. There are two main types of inversion: when the\nverb comes before the subject (inversion is often optional), and when the auxiliary comes before\nthe subject and the rest of the verb phrase follows the subject (inversion is usually necessary):\n© Her father stood in the doorway. — In the doorway stood her father. (or ... her father\n\nstood.)\n\nHe had rarely seen such a sunset. —-* Rarely had he seen such a sunset. (not Rarely he had\n\nseen...)\nInversion brings about fronting, the re-ordering of information in a sentence to give emphasis in\na particular place. Often this causes an element to be postponed until later in the sentence,\nfocusing attention on it.\n\nIn conversation we use Here comes + noun and There goes + noun, with inversion of verb and\nsubject, to talk about things and people moving towards or away from the speaker:\n) Here comes the bus. © There goes Nigel Slater, the footballer.\n\nHere comes... is also used to say that something is going to happen soon, and There goes... is\nused to talk about things (particularly money) being lost and to say that something (such as a\nphone or door bell) is ringing:\n\n© Here comes lunch. 0 My bike’s been stolen! There goes £100!\n\n© There goes the phone. Can you answer it? :\n\nWe also put the verb before the subject when we use adverbs expressing direction of movement,\nsuch as along, away, back, down, in, off, out, up with verbs such as come, fly, go. This pattern\nis found particularly in narrative, to mark a change in events:\n\n© The door opened and in came the doctor. (less formally ... and the doctor came in.)\n\n© As soon as I let go of the string, up went the balloon, high into the sky. (less formally\n..the balloon went up...)\n\n© Just when I thought I'd have to walk home, along came Miguel and he gave me a lift.\n\n(less formally ... Miguel came along and gave me...)\n\nFor more on inversion after adverbs of place and direction, see Unit 76A.\n\nWe can use clauses with inversion instead of certain kinds of if-clause (see Unit 83). Compare:\n\n© It would be a serious setback, if the © It would be a serious setback, |\ntalks were to fail. were the talks to fail.\n\n© If you should need more information, | © Should you need more information, fJ\nplease telephone our main office. please telephone our main office. |\n\n© If Alex had asked, | would have been © Had Alex asked, | would have been\nable to help. able to help. a\n\nThe sentences with inversion are rather more formal than those with ‘if’. Notice that in negative\nclauses with inversion, we don’t use contracted forms:\n© Had the plane not been diverted, they would have arrived early. (ot Hadn’t the plane...)\n\nIn formal written language we commonly use inversion after as and than in comparisons:\n4) The cake was excellent, as was the coffee. (or ...as the coffee was.)\nI believed, as did my colleagues, that the plan would work. (or ...as my colleagues did...)\nResearch shows that parents watch more television than do their children. (or ...than\ntheir children do.)\n\nNotice that we don’t invert subject and verb after as or than when the subject is a pronoun:\n© We now know a lot more about the Universe than we did ten years ago. {not ...than did\nwe ten years ago.)\n\nExercises Unit 99\n\n99.1\n\n99.2\n\n99.3\n\nComplete the sentences with an appropriate adverb and a form of either come or go. The subject and first auxiliary are inverted, and do is used with a simple tense verb after -\nve the time adverbials never (before), rarely, seldom; barely/hardly/scarcely...when/before; no\nsooner,..than:\n© Seldom do we have goods returned to us because they are faulty.\n0 Hardly had everybody taken their seats when Dr Smith began his lecture.\nonly + a time expression, as in only after, only later, only if, only once, only then, only when:\nO She bought a newspaper and some sweets at the shop on the corner. Only later did she\nrealise that she'd been given the wrong change.\nG Only once did J go to the opera the whole time I was in Italy.\n© Only when she apologises will | speak to her again.\nx only + other prepositional phrases beginning only by..., only in..., only with..., etc.:\n© Only by chance had Jameson discovered where the birds were nesting.\n© Mary had to work in the evenings and at weekends. Only in this way was she able to\ncomplete the report by the deadline.\n+ expressions such as at no time, in no way, on no account, under/in no circumstances:\nOC At no time did they actually break the rules of the game.\n© Under no circumstances are passengers permitted to open the doors themselves.\ntr expressions with not..., such as not only, not until, not since, not for one moment and also\nnot a + noun: .\n) Not until August did the government order an inquiry into the accident.\n© Not a word had she written since the exam had started.\nvw little with a negative meaning:\n© Little do they know how lucky they are to live in such a wonderful house. (= ‘they don’t\nknow’ or ‘they don’t know sufficiently’)\n© Little did J realise that one day Michael would become famous. (= ‘I didn’t realise’ or ‘I\ndidn’t realise sufficiently’)\n\nNotice that inversion can occur after a clause beginning only after/if/when or not until:\n0 Only when the famine gets worse will world governments begin to act.\n© Not until the train pulled into Euston Station did Jim find that his coat had gone.\n\nInversion after so + adjective... that; such + be...that; neither.../nor...\nWe can use so + adjective at the beginning of a clause to give special emphasis to the adjective.\nWhen we do this, the subject and first auxiliary are inverted, and do is used with a simple tense\nverb. Compare:\n\n© Her business was so successful that Marie was able to retire at the age of SO. and\n\nO So successful was her business, that Marie was able to retire at the age of 50.\n\nO The weather conditions became so dangerous that all mountain roads were closed. and\n\n© So dangerous did weather conditions become, that all mountain roads were closed.\n\nWe can use such + be at the beginning of a clause to emphasise the extent or degree of\nsomething. Compare:\n3 Such is the popularity of the play that the theatre is likely to be full every night. and\n© The play is so popular that the theatre is likely to be full every night.\n\nWe use inversion after neither and nor when these words begin a clause to introduce a negative\naddition to a previous negative clause or sentence:\n\n© For some time after the explosion Jack couldn’t hear, and neither could he see.\n\n© The council never wanted the new supermarket to be built, nor did local residents.\n\nNotice that we also use inversion in Neither/Nor do I, Neither/Nor does Kate (etc.) and in So\ndo I, So does Becky (etc.).\n\nExercises\n\nonly if barely only with rarely.\n\nUnit 100\n\n100.1 Write new sentences with a similar meaning beginning with one of these words and phrases. The event or activity is in progress\nat the present time, but not necessarily at the moment of speaking.\n\nA2 OO She’s doimg voluntary work with young children until she starts her university course.\nWe use the present continuous to suggest that an event or activity is or may be 4\ntemporary.\n\n(For the present continuous for the future, see B7 & Unit 10.)\nPresent simple (—> Units 1, 2 & 8)\n\nA3 OO Trees grow more quickly in summer than in winter.\nWe use the present simple with verbs describing states or situations that are always\ntrue or continue indefinitely.\n\nA4 OO This cake tastes wonderful, Where did you buy it?\nWe use the present simple with states or situations (thoughts, feelings) that exist at the\npresent moment.\nVerbs generally used to talk about states include *agree, appreciate, attract, “desire,\n*doubt, expect, hate, hope, like, love, *prefer, *regret (to do with emotions, attitudes,\nand preferences); anticipate, assume, *belicve, consider, expect, feel, find, imagine,\n*know, realise, think, understand (mental states); ache, hear, *notice, see, *smell,\nsound, “taste (senses and perception); *belong to, *consist of, *constitute, *contain,\ncost, “differ from, have, look, *mean, measure, “own, *possess, *resemble, “seem,\nweigh (‘being’, ‘having’, etc.).\nThe verbs marked * are rarely used with continuous tenses (but can be if we mean\nactions rather than states).\n\nAS C1 Do you go to Turkey every year for your holidays?\nWe use the present simple to talk about habits or regular events or actions.\n(For the present simple for the future, see B6 & Unit 10.)\n\nPast simple (—> Units 3, 4, 5 & 8)\n\nAGC Kathy left a few minutes ago.\n\nAT —_G Jim continued the course even though it was proving very difficult.\n\n‘We use the past simple to refer to a completed action or event in the past or to talk\nabout situations that existed over a period of time in the past, but not now. We can\neither say when something happened, using a time adverbial (e.g. a few minutes ago:\nA6), or assume that the listener or reader already knows when it happened or can\nunderstand this from the context (A7).\n1 I saw my grandparents every week as a child.\n\nWe use the past simple to talk about repeated past actions.\n\n(For the past simple in conditionals, see M12 & M13 and Unit 83.)\n\nPresent perfect (— Units 3, 6 & 8)\n\nAQ sO We can’t have a meeting, because so few people have shown any interest\n\nAlO © My ceiling has fallen in and the kitchen is flooded. Come quickly!\n\nAll 0 We have belonged to the tennis club since we moved here.\n\nA1l2 Lee has represented his country on many occasions, and hopes to go on to compete in\n\nA8\n\nthe next Olympics.\nWe use the present perfect to talk about a past action, event or state, when there is\nsome kind of connection between what happened in the past, and the present time.\n\nGrammar review\n\nOften we are interested in the way something that happened in the past affects or is\nrelevant to the situation that exists now (A9). However, the connection with the present\nmay also be that the action happened recently with a consequence for the present (A10),\nthat it continues until the present time (A11), or that a repeated event in the past may (or\nmay not) happen again (A12).\n\nPast continuous (—> Units 4, 7 & 8)\n\nA130 When he realised I was looking at him, he turned away.\nWe use the past continuous to talk about a situation (...I was looking at him...) that\nstarted before a particular point in the past (...be turned away) and was still in progress\nat that point.\n\nPast perfect (— Units 5, 7 & 8)\n\nA14 © When I went into the bathroom, I found that the bath had overflowed.\n\nA1S © By 10 o'clock most people had gone home.\nWe use the past perfect to talk about a past event that took place before another past\nevent (A14), or before or up to a particular time in the past (A15).\n\n(For the past perfect in conditionals, see M14 & Unit 83.)\n\nPresent perfect continuous (— Units 6 & 8)\n\nA16 CO Since the operation two months ago, Joe has been learning to walk again. He can already\ntake one or two steps unaided.\n\nAl7_ © Your eyes are red - have you been crying?\nWe use the present perfect continuous to talk about an activity in progress in the past for\na period until now, which is still in progress (A16) or has recently finished (A17).\n\nPast perfect continuous (—» Units 7 & 8)\n\nA18 © When I saw the vase in the shop window, I knew it was exactly what I had been looking for.\nWe use the past perfect continuous to talk about a situation or activity that was in\nprogress over a period up to a particular past point in time.\n\nTHE FUTURE (—> Units 9-14)\n\nWill + infinitive (— Unit 9)\n\nB1 > It’s late. I think I'll go to bed now.\n\nB2 I think you'll enjoy the film\nWe use will when we state a decision made at the moment of speaking (B1) and when we\nsay that we think something is likely to happen in the future (B2).\n\n83 OT'lll make one of my special desserts for dinner, if you like.\n\nB4 1 I've asked her to join us this evening, but she won't.\nWe use will (or Il) when we talk about willingness to do something in the future (e.g. in\noffers (B3), invitations, requests, and orders) and will not (or won’t) when we talk about\nunwillingness to do something in the future (e.g. reluctance, refusal (B4)).\n\nBe going to + infinitive (— Unit 9)\n\nBS —_O ‘Has anybody offered to look after the children?’ ‘Jo’s going to do it.”\nWe use be going to when we state a decision made some time before we report it. Going\nto is often preferred in informal spoken English (where it is often pronounced /gana/) and\nwill is preferred in more formal contexts.\n\nPresent simple for the future (—> Unit 10)\n\nB6 sO The next train to Newcastle leaves at 3.45. [station announcement]\nWe use the present simple to talk about future events that are part of some official\narrangement such as a timetable or programme. A time expression is usually used with\nthe present simple for the future (...@¢ 3.45) unless the time referred to is already clear\nfrom the context.\n\n203\n\nGrammar review\n\n204\n\nPresent continuous for the future (—> Unit 10)\n\nB7 —-O We're having a party next Saturday. Can you come?\nWe use the present continuous to talk about future activities and events that are\nintended or have already been arranged. Usually a personal pronoun is used (We...)\nand a future time is mentioned (...next Saturday) or already understood.\n\nFuture continuous (— Unit 11)\n\nB8 OO After the operation you won't be doing any sport for a while.\nWe use the future continuous to talk about an activity or event happening at a\nparticular time or over a particular period in the future. We usually mention the fu\ntime (After the operation...).\n\nMODALS & SEMI-MODALS (— Units 15-20)\n\nThe modal verbs are: will, would, can, could, may, might, shall, should, must. Modal verbs\nhave meanings relating to ideas such as possibility, likelihood, prediction, necessity, permission.\nand obligation. They do not have to-infinitive, -s, -ing or past participle forms. They are often\nfollowed by the bare infinitive of another verb (e.g. She might go) but can also be used on their\nown (e.g. Yes, I can). They cannot be followed directly by a to-infinitive, an -ing form, a past\nparticiple, or another modal verb. In questions they come before the subject (e.g. Could you\nhelp?) and before not in negatives (e.g. He won't (= will not) help). The semi-modals are: ought\nto, used to, need, dare, had better, have (got) to, be able to. These have meanings like modal\nverbs but not the same formal features: for example, some can be marked for tense (e.g. have/\nhad (got) to); some have non-modal uses (e.g. She needs a rest).\n\nCan, could and be able to (— Unit 15)\n\nC1 GA polyglot is someone who can speak several different languages.\n\nC2 CG Anita could speak three languages before she was six.\n\nC3. Martha couldn't swim until she was ten.\nWhen we say that someone or something has or doesn’t have the ability to do\nsomething, we use can('t) (for the present; C1) or could(n’t) (for the past; C2, C3).\n\nC4 0 “Why isn’t Tim here yet?’ ‘It could be because his mother’s ill again.’\nWe use could, not can, to say there is a possibility of something happening or being true.\n\ncs 1 Despite yesterday’s snowfalls, we were able to drive home in less than an hour.\nWe can use be able to instead of can or could to talk about ability. We prefer be able\nto when we talk about a specific achievement (particularly if it is difficult, requiring\nsome effort; CS) rather than a general ability. Where there is a choice, in speech we\ngenerally prefer can or could rather than be able to.\n\nC6 After the trees have been cut back, we will be able to see more of the garden from the\nsitting room.\nWe use will be able to, not can, to say that something is possible in the future on\ncondition that something is done first.\n\nC7) We can/are allowed to stay up late on Fridays and Saturdays because we don’t have to\ngo to school the next day.\nWe use can for the present or the future and could for the past to report permission.\nWe can also use be allowed to.\n\nWill, would and used to (—> Unit 16)\n\nC8 =O Will/Won't you have another biscuit? (‘Won't you...?’ is a very polite and rather formal\noffer)\n\nC90 ‘John wants to borrow the car.’ ‘He will not.’ (a firm refusal)\n\nC10 «6 You will now put your pens down and pay attention. (a firm instruction)\nWe use will and will not (won't) to talk about (un)willingness (see B34) and also to\nmake offers (C8), requests, refusals (C9), and to give instructions (C10).\n\ncn\n\nC12\n\nC13\nC14\n\nGrammar review\n\n© You should apply for the job. You would have a good chance of getting it.\n\nWe can use would to make a prediction about an imaginary situation; that is, about\nsomething that may or may not happen (see also M13).\n\n© Would you like me to get you some water?\n\nWe can use Would you like... when we make an offer, but not ‘Will you like...’ In\nrequests, too, we can say I would like..., but not ‘I will like...’. We can use should (with I\nor we) instead of would in requests like this, but this is formal.\n\nO We would/used to lend him money when he was unemployed.\nO | used to live in a flat in Paris.\n\nTo talk about things that happened repeatedly in the past, but don’t happen now, we can\nuse would or used to + infinitive (C13). Used to is more common in informal English. We\ncan use used to but not would to talk about permanent past states (C14). Notice how we\nnormally make questions and negatives with use to in spoken English: ‘Did your children\nuse to sleep well when they were babies?’; ‘I didn’t use to like visiting the dentist when I\nwas young.’ Many people avoid using used to in questions and negatives without do\n(‘Used you to...?’, ‘I usedn’t to...\") and in question tags (..., usedn’t you?) because it\nsounds very formal and old-fashioned.\n\nmay, might, can and could (—> Units 15 & 17)\n\nC15\n\n© If the drought goes on much longer, there may/might/could be water rationing before the\n\nend of the month,\n\nC16 Co Her parents may/might/could have influenced her decision to resign\n\nIn affirmative sentences (that is, sentences which are not questions or negatives) we use may,\nmight, or could with a similar meaning to say that there is a possibility of something\nhappening or being true (C15). Can is not used in this way. We sometimes prefer could to\nshow that we are giving an opinion about which we are unsure. We use may/might/could +\nhave + past participle to say that it is possible that something happened in the past (C16).\n\n© ‘While we're in Leeds shall we go and see Mark?’ ‘But it’s been nearly 20 years since we\nlast saw him. He may not/might not remember us.’\n\n0 I think I saw her go out, so she can't/couldn’t be at home.\nIn negative sentences, including sentences with words like only, hardly, or never, we use\nmay not or might not to say it is possible that something is not true (C17), and can’t or\ncouldn't to say that it is not possible that something is true (C18).\n\nO Coats may be left in the cloakroom.\nMay (not ‘might’) is used in formal contexts to say that something is allowed. May not is\nused to say that things are not allowed (e.g. Calculators may not be used in the\nexamination.).\n\nmust and have (got) to (— Unit 18)\n\nC20\ncai\n\nC23\n\n© That’s really good news. I muse tell Steve straight away.\n\nG ‘Can we meet on Thursday morning?’ ‘Sorry, no, | have to go to the dentist at 11.00.\"\nWhen we say that it is necessary to do something, we use must or have (got) to.\nSometimes it doesn’t matter which we use, although have got to is less formal than either\nmust or have to and is particularly common in spoken English. However, we use must\nwhen we want to indicate that the speaker decides that something is necessary (C20) and\nwe use have (got) to to suggest that someone else or some outside circumstances or\nauthority makes something necessary (C21).\n\nO She was bruised quite badly in the accident. It must hurt a lot\nWe normally use must, not have (got) to, when we conclude that something (has)\nhappened or that something is true .\n\n‘I'm seeing Dr Evans next week.’ ‘That can’t be right. He’s on holiday then.”\nWhen we give a negative conclusion we rarely use must not or have (got) to. Instead, we\nuse can’t (cannot) or couldn't.\n\n205\n\nGrammar review\n\n206\n\nC24 OG When I went to school I had to learn Latin.\nTo say that something was necessary in the past we use had to, not must.\n\nneed(n't), don't have to and mustn't (— Units 18 & 19)\n\nC25 © He didn’t cook the meal himself so you needn't/don't have to cat it all.
