Passive verb\nforms are summarised in Appendix 1. The choice between an active and passive sentence allows\nus to present the same information in two different orders. Compare:\n\nactive O The storm damaged the ee passive O The roof was damaged\n) The roof was damaged by the storm.\n\n| This sentence is about the storm, and | These sentences are about the roof and say what\nsays what it did. The subject (The happened to it (in the first sentence) and what did\nstorm) is the ‘agent’ and the object it (in the second). The subject (The roof) is the\n| (the roof) is the ‘done to’. ‘done to’. If it is mentioned, the agent (the storm)\n| goes in a prepositional phrase with by after the verb.\n\nD1. © The building survived the earthquake but then was destroyed by a fire.\n\nVerbs which take an object (transitive verbs) can have a passive form (...vas\ndestroyed). Verbs which do not take an object (intransitive verbs) do not have passive\nforms (The child vanished..., but not ‘The child was vanished...’).\nHowever, many verbs can be used at different times with and without objects — that is,\nthey can be both transitive and intransitive. Compare: ‘Are they meeting him at the\nairport?’ (transitive) and ‘Is he being met at the airport?’ (passive); ‘When shall we\nmeet?’ (intransitive; no passive possible)\n\n02 OT'm really disappointed. | didn’t get picked/wasn't picked for the team again.\n\nD3. Oo The house was owned by an elderly couple before 1 bought it.\n\nIn spoken language we often use get + past participle (...didn’t get picked...) instead\na passive form (...wasn’t picked...) to talk about actions or events that we see as\n\nGrammar review\n\nnegative (D2). Note, however, that we can also use it to talk about positive actions and\nevents (e.g. Great news - I got picked for the team again!). We don’t normally use get +\npast participle to describe states (D3).\n\nQUESTIONS (— Units 26 & 27)\n\nQuestion forms are summarised in Appendix 2.\n\n£1\n\n&3\n\nO What happened to your eye?\n\nIf we use what, which, who or whose as the subject, we don’t use do in the question (E1).\n\nHowever, notice that we can sometimes use do when what, which, who or whose is the\nsubject if we want to add emphasis, or to contrast with what has been said or implied.\nDo is stressed in spoken English: ‘Come on, be honest — who did tell you?’ Don’t confuse\nwhose with who's (short for either who is or who has), which are pronounced the same.\n1 I've got orange juice or apple juice. Which would you prefer?\n© He just turned away when I asked him. What do you think he meant?\nIn these questions the wh-word is the object. We prefer which when we are asking about\nan identified group or range of things or people (E2), and we use what when the possible\nrange of reference is open (E3), Sometimes, however, we can use either which or what\nwith little difference in meaning (e.g. What/Which towns do we go through on the way?).\n© Haven't you finished your homework yet?\nO Why didn’t she pay for the meal?\n\noo\n\nG Who wouldn't like to own an expensive sports car?\nWe can use negative yes/no or wh-questions to make a suggestion, to persuade someone,\nto criticise, or to show that we are surprised, etc, We make a negative yes/no or wh-\nquestion with an auxiliary verb (have, did, would, etc.) + -n’t (E4, ES, E6). We can also\nask a negative question using a negative statement and a positive ‘tag’ at the end (e.g. We\ndon’t have to leave just yet, do we?). Negative questions can be used to sound polite\nwhen giving an opinion (e.g. Shouldn't we offer her a lift?).\n\nVERB COMPLEMENTATION: WHAT FOLLOWS VERBS (— Units 28-31)\n\nAl\nF2\nF3\n\nF4-13\n\nO She described the attacker to the police.\n\nO They arrived at the restaurant an hour late.\n\nOD He gave me a biscuit.\nSome verbs (e.g. describe in F1) are followed by an object (...the attacker...). These are\ncalled transitive verbs. Other verbs that are usually transitive include arrest, avoid, do,\nenjoy, find, force, get, grab, hit, like, pull, report, shock, take, touch, want, warn. Some\nverbs (e.g. arrive in F2) are not usually followed by an object. These are called\nintransitive verbs. Other verbs that are usually intransitive include appear, come, fall, go,\nhappen, matter, sleep, swim, wait. If a verb can’t be followed by an object, it can’t be\nmade passive. Some verbs (e.g. give in F3) are commonly followed by two objects (me\nand a biscuit in F3). Other verbs that are commonly followed by two objects include\nlend, offer, pay, sell, tell, throw.\nA good dictionary will list the meanings of verbs and for cach meaning tell you whether\neach meaning is intransitive, transitive and, if transitive, whether it is followed by one or\nby two objects.\nMany verbs can be followed by another verb in the form of a to-infinitive (e.g. refuse to\neat), -ing (c.g. avoid working), bare infinitive (e.g. help carry). Note that when to comes\nafter a verb it can be part of a to-infinitive (= to + the base form of a verb; ¢.g. He wants\nto go, She hopes to win) or it can be a preposition followed by a noun phrase (c.g. He\nwent to the theatre) or by an -ing form (e.g. He admitted to having a gun). An -ing form\noften behaves like an object (e.g. I regret leaving).\n\n207\n\nGrammar review\n\n208\n\nHere is a summary of common patterns together with examples of verbs that are used\nin this pattern. context from the one in which it was originally produced, we\nsometimes need to make changes to the original words. Of course, differences between the original\nspeech context and the one in which it is reported will influence whether changes are needed and\nwhat they should be. Here are some possible changes:\n\nG1 ‘Jim’s arriving later today.’ — She said that Jim was arriving later that day.\nG2 Go ‘I was sure I’d left it here.’ > He said that he was sure he'd left it there/on the table.\nG30 ‘I grew these carrots myself.’ — He told me that he bad grown those carrots himself.\n\nThe tense we choose for a report is one that is appropriate at the time that we are\nreporting what was said or thought. This means that we sometimes use a different tense in\nthe report from the one that was used in the original statement (G1 & G3) and change\npronouns, references to time and place, and words such as this, that, and these (G1-G3).\n\nG4 =O Martha told me (that) she would be late for the meeting.\n\nG5 OO She said (that) she was feeling ill.\n\nG6 I said to John (that) he had to work harder.\n\nG7 1 She told me about her holiday in Finland.\nSay and tell are the verbs most commonly used to report statements. We use an object\nafter tell (...2e..., G4), but not after say (G5). Notice, however, that we can use to +\nobject after say (...t0 Jobn..., G6), but not after tell, and that we can report what topic\nwas talked about using tell + object + about (G7).\n\nNOUNS (- Units 40-43)\n\nCountable and uncountable nouns\n\nHl The equipment was faulty.\n\nNouns can be either countable or uncountable. Countable nouns are those which can have\nthe word a/an before them or be used in the plural. Uncountable nouns are not used with\na/an or in the plural. Some nouns in English are normally uncountable (like equipment),\nwhile in many other languages they are countable. For example: accommodation, advice,\napplause, assistance, baggage, camping, cash, chaos, chess, clothing, conduct, courage,\ncutlery, dancing, dirt, employment, equipment, evidence, fun, furniture, harm, health,\nhomework, housing, housework, information, jewellery, leisure, litter, luck, luggage,\nmachinery, money, mud, music, news, nonsense, parking, pay, permission, photography,\npoetry, pollution, produce, progress, publicity, research, rubbish, safety, scenery,\nshopping, sightseeing, sunshine, transport, underwear, violence, weather, work.\n\nH2 © The company is/are doing a lot of business in South America.\n\nSometimes a noun is used uncountably when we are talking about the whole substance or\nidea (e.g. business}, but countably when we are talking about units or different kinds (e.g.\nbusinesses}. There are many nouns like this, including beer, coffee, water; fruit,\ntoothpaste, washing powder; cake, chicken, land, paint, space, stone; abuse,\n(dis)agreement, business, difficulty, fear, improvement, language, life, pain, protest,\nresponsibility, success, thought, war. Here are some examples: Three coffees and a\nlemonade, please. - Brazil is a major producer of coffee.; Most toothpastes contain\ncolourings. — Don’t forget to buy some toothpaste.; The chickens have escaped. - I don’t\neat chicken.; I have a fear of spiders - He was trembling with fear.\n\nH3 3 The use of recycled paper is saving thousands of trees from being cut down each year.\nSome nouns (such as paper) usually have different meanings when they are used\ncountably and uncountably. Other nouns like this include accommodation, competition,\nglass, grammar, iron, jam, lace, property, room, sight, speech, time, tin, work. Here are\nsome examples: I just don’t understand grammar. - I looked the answer up in a grammar\n(= a reference book); I got held up in a jam (= traffic jam). - This jam is really sweet.\n(Note that ‘jams’ can also be used to mean types of jam); She made a wonderful speech\nat the wedding. — His speech has been affected by the illness. 209\n\nGrammar review\n\nCompound nouns (—> Unit 43)\n\nH4 ~=© How much pocket money do you give to your children?\n\nHS 0 A new golf courselgolf-course is being built outside the town.\nA compound noun (such as pocket money) is an expression made up of more than one\nword, which functions as a noun in a sentence. For example, we can use a noun +\nnoun combination to say what something is made of, where something is, when\nsomething happens, or what someone does: rice pudding, a glasshouse, the kitchen\ncupboard, hill fog, a night flight, a morning call, a language teacher, a window-cleaner.\nWe sometimes make compounds from nouns which consist of more than two nouns: a\nmilk chocolate bar, an air-traffic controller, a dinner-party conversation.\n\nSome compound nouns are usually written as one word (e.g. a tablecloth), some as\nseparate words (e.g. waste paper), and others with a hyphen (e.g. a word-processor).\nSome compound nouns can be written in more than one of these ways (c.g. a golf\ncourse or a golf-course; HS). A good dictionary will tell you how a particular\ncompound noun is usually written.\n\nH6 OO She got some chewing gum stuck on her shoe.\nSome compound nouns consist of -ing + noun as in: chewing gum, a living room,\ndrinking water, (a pack of) playing cards, a dressing gown, a turning-point, a working\nparty. The -ing form usually says what purpose the following noun has. Other\ncompound nouns consist of a noun + -ing: fly-fishing, film-making, sunbathing, risk-\ntaking, life-saving. These compounds usually refer to actions or processes.\n\nThe possessive form of nouns\n\nH7 Oo The girls’ shoes were covered in mud, so I asked them to take them off before they got\ninto Tom's car.\nTo make the possessive form of nouns in writing — referring to people or groups of\npeople (e.g. companies), other living things, places, times, etc. - we add ’s (‘apostrophe\ns’) to singular nouns and to irregular plurals that don’t end in -s (e.g. Tom’s car; the\ncollege’s administrators; the women’s liberation movement) and add ’ (an apostrophe)\nto regular plurals (e.g. the girls’ shoes; the companies’ difficulties). To make the\npossessive form of names ending in -s pronounced /z/ we can add either ’ or ’s (e.g. It’s\nTom Jones’ (or Tom Jones's) new sports car).\n\nH8 0 That old car of Jo’s is falling apart.\n\nH9 OO It belongs to a friend of his.\nWe can use the pattern noun + of + ’s (H8)}/ possessive pronoun (H9) to talk about\nsomething that someone owns or about a relationship. Notice that when we are\ntalking about relationships between people we can also use a noun without ’s (e.g. an\nuncle of Mark’s (or an uncle of Mark)).\n\nH10 i We're going to Linda’s (house) for the evening.\nThe noun following a possessive form can be left out when we talk about someone’s\nhouse. We don’t use ‘shop’ when we talk about, for example, the newsagent’s/the\nchemist’s or the newsagent/the chemist (but not ‘the newsagent’s shop’/‘the chemist’s\nshop’) where the name of the shop includes the name of the person who works there\n(compare ‘the sweet shop’, but not ‘the sweet’s’).\n\nH11 OC David's guitar playing has improved enormously.\n\nH12 0 The construction of the office block was opposed by protestors.\nOften we can use the possessive ’s or ...of + noun... with very little difference in\nmeaning. However, in general, we are more likely to use the possessive form of a noun\nwhen the noun refers to a particular person or group of people (H11); and when we\nare talking about time (e.g. next year’s holiday prices, rather than the holiday prices of\nnext year).\n\n210\n\nGrammar review\n\nWe are more likely to use the ...of + noun... form with an inanimate noun (H12); when\nwe are talking about a process, or a change over time (e.g. the establishment of the\ncommittee, rather than the committee's establishment); and when the noun is a long noun\nphrase (e.g. She is the sister of someone I used to go to school with. rather than She is\nsomeone I used to go to school with’s sister.).\n\nARTICLES, DETERMINERS AND QUANTIFIERS (— Units 44-52)\n\nDeterminers are words such as this, her, and your which determine or specify what a noun or noun\nphrase refers to, They come before the noun and at the front of the noun phrase. Quantifiers are\nwords such as some, much, and few which identify the quantity of something. Some words can be\nboth determiners and quantifiers (e.g. ‘I sent out invitations to a few friends’ [few = determiner] and\n‘A few of my friends came to the party’ [few = quantifier]) while some are determiners only (c.g.\n‘This is my friend Andrew’ {my = determiner]). Many determiners and quantifiers can be pronouns,\ntaking the place of a noun phrase (e.g. I've invited all my friends and most are coming [most =\npronoun)). Articles (a/an and the) are determiners. They also specify what the noun refers to and\ncome at the beginning of the noun phrase. However, they cannot be quantifiers or pronouns.\n\nThe (— Units 45-47)\n\nif} © Dorothy took a cake and some biscuits to the party, but only the biscuits were eaten.\n12 © Can you shut the door after you, please?\n\n13 © We had a good time on holiday. The beaches were all beautifully clean.\n\n14 0 Give it to the man wearing the red coat.\n\nIs © Look at the moon. It’s very bright tonight.\n\nWe use the with singular, plural or uncountable nouns when we expect the listener or\nreader to be able to identify the thing or person we are referring to in the following noun.\nIt may be that the thing has already been mentioned (11); that it is clear from the\nsituation which person or thing we mean (12); that it is in some other way\nunderstandable from the context which thing or person we mean (13; ‘the beaches’ = ‘the\nbeaches we went to’); that the thing or person is identified in what is said after the noun\n(14; ‘wearing the red coat’); or that there is only one of a particular thing (IS and also, for\nexample, the Great Wall of China, the North Pole, the USA, the world).\n\nAlan (— Units 44-47)\n\n16 © Helen’s just bought a house on Wilson Street.\n\n17 OD Sydney is a beautiful city.\nWe use a/an with singular nouns when we don’t expect the listener or reader to be able\nto identify the thing or person we are referring to in the following noun, We often use\na/an to introduce a new specific person or thing (16); or when the noun refers to a class\nof people or things generally — for example, when we describe someone or something or\nsay what type of thing someone or something is (17).\n\nZero article (—> Units 45-47)\n\n18 Q {-} Water has got into my camera and damaged it.\n\n\\9 O There are /-/ examples of the present continuous tense on page 32.\nWe use zero article /-} with uncountable and plural nouns when we talk generally about\npeople or things rather than about specific people or things. If it is\nused in this way some is pronounced with its weak form /som/.\n\n113. © Some teachers never seem to get bored with being in the classroom.\nWe use some to talk about particular, but unspecified, people or things with the\nimplication ‘some, but not all’. Much is used with\nuncountable nouns (138) and many with plural nouns (139). Before the, my, this, etc. we\nuse much of/ many of. Much of can also be used with a singular countable noun to mean\n‘a large part of’ (e.g. Much of the national park was destroyed in the fire.). We can use\nmuch and many without a noun if the meaning is clear (e.g. Can you get some sugar\nwhen you go shopping? There isn’t much left.). Much and many are often used after as,\nhow, so, and too (e.g. I'd say there were twice as many women at the meeting as men.).\n\nO She didn’t show much interest in what I said.\n\nJohn offered me a lot of money for the car.\n\nMany of my relatives live around Wolverhampton.\n\nMuch (of) and many (of) are used in negative sentences to emphasise that we are talking\n\nabout small (or smaller than expected) quantities or amounts (140) and in questions to\n\nask about quantities or amounts (c.g. In\n\naffirmative sentences we often use a lot of, lots of or plenty of rather than much (of) and\n\nmany to talk about large amounts and quantities, particularly in conversation and\n\ninformal writing (141). Participle clauses (-ing and -ed clauses) can\nbe used like relative clauses, but can also have an adverbial function, giving information about\ntime, cause, etc.\n\nRelative clauses (— Units 53-55)\n\nJ O Andrew stopped the police car that was driving past.\n\nJ2 GO My mother, who is in ber seventies, enjoys hill walking.\nDefining relative clauses (e.g. ...that was driving past; J1) are used to specify which\nperson or thing we mean, or which type of person or thing we mean, Notice that we\ndon’t put a comma between the noun and a defining relative clause.\nNon-defining relative clauses (c.g. ..., who is in her seventies, ...; J2) are used to add\nextra information about a noun, but this information is not necessary to explain which\nperson or thing we mean. We don’t use them often in everyday speech, but we do use\nthem frequently in written English. Notice that we often put a comma before and after\na non-defining relative clause.\n\nJ3 O The house, which ts to the north of the road, is owned by the council.\nAfter a relative clause, we don’t repeat the subject with a pronoun; so, for example, we\nwouldn’t say ‘The house which is to the north of the road it is owned by the council’.\nHowever, this is sometimes found in informal speech; for example, ‘A friend of mine\nwho is a solicitor - she helped me.’\n\n-ing clauses (= present participle clauses) (—> Units 58 & 59)\nJ4 © Glancing over his shoulder, he could see the dog chasing him.\n\nGrammar review\n\nJ5 © Pushing ber way through the crowds, she just managed to get on the bus as it pulled\naway.\nJ6 1 ‘Wait a minute,’ said Frank, renning through the door.\n\nWe can use an -ing clause to talk about something that takes place at the same time as\n(J4) or just before (JS) an action in the main clause. Some\nadjectives are not gradable because they refer to qualities that are completely present\nor completely absent. These non-gradable adjectives (such as ‘impossible’ in L2) are\nnot usually used in comparative and superlative forms or with words such as ‘very’ or\n‘extremely’. They can often, however, be used with words such as ‘absolutely’ or\n\n‘completely’.\nOrder of adjectives\n3 GC I drank some very good Brazilian coffee.\n\nWhen we use more than one adjective before a noun, there is often a preferred\n(although not fixed) order for these adjectives depending on what type of adjective\nthey are: opinion + size/physical quality/shape/age + colour + participle adjectives +\norigin + material + type + purpose + NOUN. Here are some examples showing the most\nlikely order: an old plastic container (= age + material + noun); a hard red ball\n\n(= quality + colour + noun); a frightening Korean mask (= opinion + origin + noun); a\nround biscuit tin (= shape + purpose (for holding biscuits) + noun); a small broken\nplate (= size + participle adjective + noun); a useful digital alarm clock (= opinion +\ntype + purpose + noun).\n\nTo help you to learn this order, it can be useful to remember that gradable adjectives\n(describing opinion, size, quality, shape, and age) usually precede ungradable adjectives\n(participle adjective and adjectives describing origin, material, type and purpose).\n\nEasily confused adjectives\n\nL4 I was surprised to find that the film was quite frightening.\nWhen we use the following adjectives to describe how a person feels about something\nor someone else, generally the -ed adjectives describe how the person feels (e.g. I was\nsurprised...), and the -ing adjectives give an evaluation of the thing or other person\n(e.g. ...the film was quite frightening.): alarmed - alarming, amazed - amazing, bored —\nboring, excited — exciting, frightened - frightening, interested — interesting, pleased -\npleasing, surprised - surprising, tired - tiring, worried — worrying.\n\nAdjectives and adverbs: use (— Unit 71)\n\nLs © The staff in the shop always speak politely to customers.\n\nL6 0 It was strangely quiet as we went into the room.\nWe use an adverb, not an adjective, to say how something happened or was done (LS)\nor to modify adjectives (L6).\n\n216\n\nGrammar review\n\nAdjectives and adverbs: comparative and superlative forms (— Unit 72)\n\n“7 © The building was bigger than I'd expected.\n\nLs 1 It was the most ridiculous thing to say.\nWe usually add the ending -er to one-syllable adjectives and adverbs to make their\ncomparative forms (L7) and -est to make their superlative forms. With two syllables we can usually\nuse either.\n\nQuite\n\n{9} O I was quite satisfied with the result.\n\nL410 © No, you're quite wrong!\n\nLu oT\nQuite has two meanings: to a particular degree, but not ‘very’ (= ‘fairly’) (L9); and to a\nlarge degree, or ‘very much’ (= ‘completely’) (L10). When quite is used with non-gradable\nadjectives it means ‘completely’ (1.11).\n\nADVERBIAL CLAUSES & CONJUNCTIONS (— Units 79-87)\n\nAn adverbial clause is a type of subordinate clause, linked to a main clause. An adverbial clause\nadds extra information to the main clause about such things as time and conditions. Most\nadverbial clauses begin with a conjunction that indicates their link with the main clause. Example\nconjunctions are after, before, and until (time conjunctions); and if and unless (conditional\nconjunctions).\n\n¢ food here is quite superb.\n\nTenses in adverbial and main clauses: general\n\nM1 OC Because I’m overweight, my doctor has put me on a diet.\n\nM2 OI felt unwell when I got up this morning.\nThe verb in the adverbial clause is usually the same tense as the verb in the main clause.\nIn M1 they are both present (present simple + present perfect), and in M2 they are both\npast (past simple + past simple).\n\nTime clauses: tenses (—> Unit 79)\n\nM3 © Have something to eat before you leave.\nTo refer to the future after a time conjunction (...before...) we use present tenses.\n\nM4 © As soon as you see/ have seen her, come and tell me.\n\nMS © She wrote to me after she spoke! had spoken to Jim.\nTo talk about an action in the adverbial clause that is completed before another action\ndescribed in the main clause, we can use either simple or perfect tenses (present as in M4\nor past as in MS), but not will or will have + -ed (the future perfect).\n\nM6 © When I saw Kim, I asked her over for dinner.\nIf the actions in the main clause and the adverbial clause take place at the same time, we\nuse simple, not perfect tenses.\n\nM7 While the children were swimming, their mother kept a watchful eye on them.\n\nM8 I read a book while I waited.\nWhile is mainly used with continuous tenses (M7) and also with simple tenses (M8).\n\nConditional clauses (—> Units 83-86)\n\nReal and unreal conditionals (-> Units 83 & 84)\n\nSome conditional clauses beginning with if suggest that a situation is real — that is, the situation is\nor was true, or may have been or may become true (e.g. If atyone phones, tell them I'll be back at\n11.00; If you really want to learn Italian, you need to spend some time in Italy). Others suggest\nthat a situation is unreal - that is, the situation is imaginary or untrue. (e.g. What would you do if\nyou won the lottery?; If you had started out earlier, you wouldn't have been so late).\n\n217\n\nGrammar review\n\nCompare: If I go to Berlin, I'll travel by train. (= real conditional) and If I went to Berlin, I'd\ntravel by train. (= unreal conditional). In the first, the speaker is thinking of going to Berlin (it\na real future possibility), but in the second, the speaker is not thinking of doing so. The seco\nmight be giving someone advice.\n\nReal conditionals: tenses (> Units 83 & 84)\n\nM9 = OT'll give you a lift if it rains.\n\nM10 Gi If you leave now, you'll be home in two hours.\n\nM11 1 If water fre , it expands,\n\nM12. Ci If I made the wrong decision then I apologise.\nIn real conditionals we use a present tense to talk about the future (M9), the present\n(M10) or unchanging relationships (M11), and past tenses to talk about the past\n(M12).\n\nUnreal conditionals: tenses (> Units 83 & 84)\n\nM13 © If my grandfather was/ were still alive, he would be a hundred today.\nTo talk about present or future situations in unreal conditionals, we use a past tense\n(either simple or continuous) in the if-clause and would + bare infinitive in the main\nclause. In unreal conditionals we don’t use the past simple or past perfect in the main\nclause. In unreal conditionals, we can also use could/might (have) instead of would\n(have) (e.g. If my grandfather was/ were still alive, he might have enjoyed looking aft\nour garden; If I lived out of town, I could take up horse riding.). Notice that we\nsometimes use if...were instead of if...was (see Unit 85).\n\nM14 © If I had known how difficult the job was, | wouldn't have taken it.\nWhen we talk about something that might have happened in the past, but didn’t, then.\nwe use if + past perfect and would have + past participle in the main clause. We can\nalso use might/could have instead of would have in the main clause (e.g. They might\nhave found a better hotel if they had driven a few more kilometres.).\n\nM15 2 If Bob wasn't so lazy, he would have passed the exam easily.\n\nM16 © If the doctor had been called earlier, Mary would still be alive today.\nIn some unreal conditionals we use mixed tenses. That is, a past tense in the if-clause\nand would have + past participle in the main clause (M15), or a past perfect in the if-\nclause and would + infinitive in the main clause (M16). We can use these patterns to\ntalk about possible consequences if situations were or had been different. We can also\nuse might/could (have) in the main clause instead of would (have) (e.g. ...he could have\npassed the exam easily.; ...Mary might still be alive today.).\n\nM17. O If | had a more reliable car, I'd drive to Spain rather than fly\nIn unreal conditional sentences we don’t normally use would in an if-clause (but see\nUnit 84).\n\nOther types of adverbial clause give information about place (M18), contrast (M19 and Unit 82),\ncause or reason (M20 and Unit 80), purpose (M21 and Unit 81), and result (M22 and Unit 81):\n\nM18 Can you put it back where you found it, please?\n\nM19 © My sister is blonde, whereas my brother has dark hair\nM20 © He wasn’t allowed in because be was too young.\n\nM21 © We got up early so that we could watch the sunrise.\n\nM22 © He played so badly that he was easily beaten.\n\n218\n\nactive\nIn an active clause or active sentence, the grammatical subject is the person or thing that performs the\naction given in the verb (e.g. Geoff wrote the book). Compare PASSIVE.\n\nadjective\n\nA word that describes a noun (e.g. an interesting book) or a pronoun (e.g. a red one}. Gradable\nadjectives can be used to say that a person or thing has more or less of this quality (e.g. She’s very\nhappy), while non-gradable adjectives can’t (e.g. It’s impossible. We can’t say ‘It’s very impossible’).\nClassifying adjectives say that something is of a particular type (e.g. atomic, initial). Emphasising\nadjectives stress how strongly we feel about something (e.g. complete nonsense}. Qualitative adjectives\nsay what qualities a person or thing has (c.g. big, rich). See also grading ADVERBS and non-grading\nADVERBS.\n\nadjective phrase\nA group of words where the main word is an adjective (e.g. it’s extremely important; it wasn’t strong\nenough).\n\nadverb\n\nA word that describes or gives more information (when, how, where, etc.) about a verb (e.g. He ran\nquickly), adjective (e.g. an extremely expensive car), another adverb (e.g. She did it very easily), or\nphrase (e.g. They live just across the road.). Types of adverb include: adverbs of manner (e.g. slowly,\nviolently) which we use to say how something is done; connecting adverbs (e.g. consequently, similarly);\ntime adverbs (e.g. tomorrow, already); place adverbs (e.g. upstairs, outside); direction adverbs (e.g.\nbackwards, through); comment adverbs (e.g. apparently, personally) which we use to make a comment\non what we are saying; viewpoint adverbs (e.g. financially, politically) which we use to make clear from\nwhat point of view we are speaking; adverbs of indefinite frequency (e.g. always, never); degree adverbs\n(c.g. completely, quite) which give information about the extent or degree of something; focus adverbs\n(e.g. just, even) which we use to focus on a particular word or phrase. Grading adverbs (e.g. extremely,\nvery) are used with gradable apjectives. Non-grading adverbs (c.g. completely, mainly) are used with\nnon-gradable ADJECTIVES.\n\nadverbial\n\nA word or group of words that says when, how, where, etc. something happens. They may consist of an\nADVERB (c.g. quietly), a PREPOSITIONAL PHRASE (e.g. through the door), a NOUN PHRASE (e.g. next week),\nOf an ADVERBIAL CLAUSE (¢.g. after she left).\n\nadverbial clause\nA type of subordinate CLAUSE that says when, how, where, etc. something happens (e.g. Before I went to\nschool this morning, | did my homework).\n\naffirmative sentence\nA statement (i.e. not a question) that is positive, not negative.\n\nagent\nThe person or thing that performs the action described in a verb. Usually it is the subject in an active\nclause and comes after ‘by...’ in a passive clause.\n\narticle\n\nThe word the is the definite article and the word a (an before vowels) is the indefinite article. When\nthere is no article before a noun we refer to this as the zero article.\n\nauxiliary verbs\n\nThe verbs be, have and do when they are used with a main verb to form questions, negatives, tenses,\npassive forms, etc. MODAL VERBS are also auxiliary verbs.\n\n219\n\nGlossary\n\n220\n\nclause\n\nA group of words that contains a verb. A clause may be a complete sentence or a part of a sentence. A\nmain clause can exist as a separate sentence, while a subordinate clause cannot (e.g. If I see Tony at\nwork [= subordinate clause], I'll invite him over this evening [= main clause]). Types of clause include:\nsince-clause (¢.g. | haven’t seen him since we left school); that-clause (e.g. She said that she was\nthirsty); wh-clause (e.g. | asked Sandra where she was going); it-clause (e.g. It’s not surprising that\nyou're feeling cold); what-clause (e.g. What I want to do is buy a better computer); if-clause (e.g. If\nyou leave now, you'll be home by 10.00); whether-clause (e.g. You have to take the exam whether you\nwant to or not); to-infinitive clause (e.g. To become a doctor takes years of study); present participle\n(-ing) clause (e.g. Feeling hungry, | went into the kitchen); past participle (-ed) clause (e.g. Built during\nthe 1950s, the building is now in need of repair); being + past participle (-ed) clause (e.g. Being\nunemployed, Tom had a lot of time on his hands); having + past participle (-ed) clause (e.g. Having\nseen the doctor, | went straight home). See also CONDITIONAL CLAUSE, NON-FINITE CLAUSE, RELATIVE\nCLAUSE.\n\ncleft sentence\nA sentence in which focus is given to either the subject or object using a pattern beginning ‘It...’ (e.g. It\nwas my brother who lent me the money) or ‘What...’ (e.g. What you need is a holiday).\n\ncomplement\n\nA word or phrase that follows a LINKING VERB and describes the suBJECT (e.g. Linda is a lawyer) or\nOBJECT (e.g. I found the food imedible). A complement may also be an ADVERBIAL or PREPOSITIONAL\nPHRASE which completes the meaning of a verb. Some verbs need a complement (e.g. The disease\noriginated in Britain; ‘The disease originated’ would be incomplete).\n\ncompound\nA compound noun consists of two or more words together used as a noun (c.g. a language school). A\ncompound adjective consists of two or more words together used as an adjective (e.g. They were well-\n\nbehaved).\n\nconditional\n\nA conditional clause usually starts with ‘if’, but other patterns are possible (e.g. Had it not rained,\nEngland would have won). A conditional sentence contains a conditional clause. A distinction can be\nmade between real conditionals, which suggest that the situation is or was true, or may have been or\nmay become true (e.g. If she makes a promise, she keeps it) and unreal conditionals, which suggest\nthat the situation is imaginary or untrue (e.g. If you had asked me, I would have helped).\n\nconjunction\nA word such as and, but, if, while, after, because which connects words, phrases, or clauses in a\nsentence. Compare SENTENCE CONNECTOR.\n\ncountable\nA countable noun can be both singular and plural (e.g. cup/cups). An uncountable noun doesn’t have\na plural form (e.g. electricity, but not ‘electricities’).\n\ndeclarative sentence\nA declarative sentence is a statement. In a declarative sentence the subject is followed by the verb.\n\ndeterminer\n\nA word that goes in front of a noun to identify what the noun refers to (e.g. this, some, the, a/an,\neach, all, my). Possessive determiners (also called possessive adjectives) are words such as my, your\nand their.\n\ndirect speech\nSpeech that is written using the exact words of the speaker, without any changes. Compare REPORTED\nSPEECH.\n\nGlossary\n\ndynamic verb\nA verb that describes an action (e.g. walk, throw). Compare STATE VERB.\n\nimperative\n\nAn imperative clause uses the bare (NFINITIVE form of a verb for such things as giving orders and\nmaking suggestions (e.g. Go to bed!).\n\ninfinitive\n\nThe form of a verb that usually goes after ‘to’, The form can be either the to-infinitive (e.g. to sing, to\neat) or the bare infinitive (e.g. sing, eat).\n\nintransitive verb\nA verb that doesn’t take an object (e.g. She smiled). Compare TRANSITIVE VERB.\n\ninversion\nChanging the usual word order so that the verb comes before the subject (¢.g. Up went the balloon).\n\nlinking verb\nA verb (e.g. be, become, appear} that connects a SUBJECT with its COMPLEMENT.\n\nmodal verbs\n\nA group of verbs (can, could, dare, may, might, must, need, ought to, shall, should, will, would, used\nto) that give information about such things as possibility, necessity, and obligation. Semi-modal verbs\n(ought to, used to, need, dare, had better, have (got) to, be able to) have similar meanings to modal\nverbs.\n\nnon-affirmative\n\nReferring to a lack of positive, affirmative meaning; for example, in most questions and negatives.\nHowever, questions which expect a positive reply are affirmative. The terms ‘non-assertive’ and\n‘assertive’ are sometimes used for ‘non-affirmative’ and ‘affirmative’.\n\nnon-finite clause\n\nA clause with a non-finite VERB; either a to-infinitive form (e.g. | want to leave soon), an -ing (present\nparticiple) form (e.g. I caught him stealing my camera), or -ed (past participle) form (e.g. We cleared up\nthe mess left after the party).\n\nnoun\nA word that refers to a person, place, thing, quality, etc. A collective noun refers to a group of people\nor things (e.g. audience, crowd, herd). See also COUNTABLE NOUN.\n\nnoun phrase\n\nA group of words where the main word is a noun (e.g. I’ve been talking to the woman across the road;\nWe spoke to several small children).\n\nobject\n\nThe direct object is the person or thing affected by the action of the verb (e.g. | put the book\n\n[= direct object] back on the shelf). The indirect object is the person or thing who benefits from the\naction or who receives something (e.g. I gave my mother [= indirect object] some flowers\n\n[= direct object]). Compare susject.\n\nparticiple\n\nThe present participle is the -ing form of a verb (e.g. walking, singing, eating) used, for example, in\ncontinuous tenses. The past participle is the -ed form of a verb (c.g. walked, sang, eaten) used, for\nexample, in perfect tenses. A participle adjective is one formed from the present or past participle of a\nverb (e.g. the candidates applying, a broken plate). A participle clause has a present participle or past\nparticiple verb form (e.g. Feeling unwell, he went to bed; The person appointed to the post will have a\ndifficult job to do).\n\n221\n\nGlossary\n\n222\n\nparticle\n\nAn adverb or preposition that follows a verb in TWO-WORD VERBS and THREE-WORD VERBS (c.g. What\ntime did you get in? [in = adverb]; I flicked through the magazine [through = preposition]; She looks\nup to her mother [up = adverb, to = preposition]}.\n\npassive\nIn a passive clause or passive sentence, the grammatical subject is the person or thing that experiences\nthe effect of the action given in the verb (e.g. The book was written by Geoff}. Compare ACTIVE.\n\nperformative\nA performative is a verb which states the action that is performed when a speaker uses the verb (e.g. I\npromise I'll do it tomorrow; I apologise).\n\nphrasal verb\n\nA verb together with a following adverb and/or a preposition that has a single meaning (e.g. set off,\nlook up to). Compare ‘I ran across Tanya at the concert’ (= met unexpectedly; a phrasal verb) and ‘She\nran across the road’ (= a PREPOSITIONAL VERB).\n\npossessive\nThe possessive form of a noun ends in either -’s (e.g. Mark's car) or -s’ (e.g. the girls’ changing room).\n\npreposition\nA word such as in, on, or by that comes before a noun, pronoun, noun phrase or -ing form (e.g. i\nMarch, above my uncle’s head, by investing).\n\nprepositional phrase\nA group of words that consists of a PREPOSITION and its prepositional object (a noun, pronoun, noun\nphrase or -ing form) (e.g. behind our house, across it).\n\nprepositional verb\nA verb and a following preposition (e.g. believe in, consist of, look after). Compare PHRASAL VERB.\n\npronoun\nA word that is used instead of a noun or noun phrase. Pronouns include personal pronouns (c.g. [,\nshe, me), reflexive pronouns (e.g. ryself, herself), possessive pronouns (c.g. ry, mine, your, yours),\nand RELATIVE PRONOUNS (e.g. who, which).\n\nquantifier\nA word or phrase that goes before a noun or noun phrase to talk about the quantity of something\n(e.g. a little water, many of the women in the room).\n\nquestion\n\nA wh-question begins with a WH-WORD (e.g. Where are you going?). A yes/no question is one that can\nbe answered with ‘yes’ or ‘no’ (e.g. Do you like coffee?). An echo question repeats part of a previous\nutterance and asks for a repetition of all or part of it (e.g. ‘I’m moving to Alaska.’ ‘You're moving\nwhere?’). We can ask an indirect question by putting it into a subordinate CLAUSE beginning with a\nWH-WORD or with if or whether (e.g. Can you tell me where you live?).\n\nrelative clause\n\nA kind of subordinate CLAusE that describes a noun that comes before it in a main CLAUsE. A defining\nrelative clause says which person or thing is being talked about (e.g. A friend who lives in London is\ngetting married). A non-defining relative clause gives more information about the noun (e.g. My\nbicycle, which I've left outside your house, is over 20 years old). A nominal relative clause begins with\na WH-WORD or whatever, whenever, etc. and functions as a NOUN PHRASE in a sentence (e.g. What |\nneed now is a long, hot bath). A reduced relative clause usually begins with an -ing (present participle)\nor -ed (past participle) form and has a similar meaning to a relative clause (e.g. | met the people living\nim our old house [= ...who live in our old house], The new rules only affect people born before 1950\n[= ho were born before 1950]).\n\nGlossary\n\nrelative pronoun\nA pronoun such as who, which, or that which is used at the beginning of a relative clause.\n\nrelative word\nWords including RELATIVE PRONOUNS and others (c.g. whereby, why) that can begin a RELATIVE CLAUSE.\n\nreported speech\n\nSpeech that is reported without using the exact words of the speaker. Sometimes called ‘indirect speech’.\nreporting clause & reported clause\n\nA statement that reports what people think or say is often divided into a reporting clause and a\nreported clause (c.g. She said [= reporting clause] that the building was unsafe [= reported clause]).\nreporting verb\n\nA verb used in a REPORTING CLAUSE that describes what people say or think (e.g. ask, claim, say).\nsentence connector\n\nA word or phrase that shows a connection between two separate sentences (e.g. My car isn’t very\ncomfortable. However, it’s very cheap to run.; The house is large and has a beautiful garden. What's\nmore, it’s very close to the station.). Compare CONJUNCTION.\n\nsimple sentence\nA sentence consisting of one clause.\n\nstate verb\nA verb that is used to describe a state (e.g. believe, think) rather than an action. Compare DYNAMIC\nVERB.\n\nsubject\nThe person or thing that does the action of the verb (e.g. Tommy went home). Compare OBJECT.\n\nsubjunctive\n\nThe subjunctive is a set of verb forms used mainly in rather formal English to talk about possibilities\nrather than facts. The present subjunctive uses the base form of the verb (e.g. We suggest that she leave\nimmediately) and the past subjunctive uses were (e.g. If | were you, I'd go home now).\n\ntransitive verb\nA verb that takes an object (e.g. She was holding a bunch of flowers). Compare INTRANSITIVE VERB.\n\ntwo-word verbs & three-word verbs\n\nVerbs that are commonly used with a particular PARTICLE (adverb or preposition) are referred to here as\ntwo-word verbs (e.g. She looked after her elderly parents), Verbs that are commonly used with two\nparticular particles (adverb + preposition) are referred to here as three-word verbs (e.g. He looked up to\nhis older brothers). See also PREPOSITIONAL VERB and PHRASAL VERB.\n\nverb\n\nA finite verb has a tense (e.g. She waited; She is waiting for you). Non-finite verb forms are INFINITIVE\n(e.g. He came to see me) and ParTICIPLE forms (e.g. Shouting loudly, | was able to make myself heard;\nBuilt in 1980, the tower is still the tallest construction in Europe).\n\nverb phrase\n\nA group of words consisting of one or more verbs (e.g. gives, is giving, has been giving).\n\nwh-words\nA group of words (who, whom, whose, where, when, why, how) that are used in wh-QUESTIONS.\n\n223\n\n224\n\nAppendix 1\n\nIf an agent is mentioned, it goes in a prepositional phrase with by after the verb (see also Unit 24).\n\n| Present simple |\n\nActive: tell(s) soon?\nGS Where will you stay?\n\n2 If a verb phrase includes more than one auxiliary verb, only the first comes before the subject:\n5 Has she been doing her homework?\nSl What should we have told Jack?\n\n3 In present and past simple tenses of verbs (apart from be}, we use do or did:\n5 Does he enjoy school?\n1 Where did you go on holiday?\n\n4 If be is used in a verb phrase without another verb, the verb comes before the subject:\nG Are you happy at work?\n© Where was Jack today?\n\n5 If we use what, which, who or whose as the subject, we use the same word order as in a\nstatement with the subject before the verb phrase:\nG What made that noise?\nC Who can tell me the answer to question 5?\n\n225\n\nAppendix 3\n\nA You put single (*...') or double (“...”) quotation marks at the beginning and end of a report of\nsomeone's exact spoken or written words. This is often referred to as direct speech:\n© ‘It’s a pity you can’t come this weekend.’\n2 “I'm really hungry. I fancy a cheese sandwich.”\n\nB If there is a reporting clause (e.g. she said, exclaimed Tom) after the quotation, you put a\ncomma before the second quotation mark:\nU “Lthink we should go to India while we have the opportunity,” argued Richard.\n\nIf you are quoting a question or exclamation, you use a question mark or exclamation mark\ninstead of a comma:\n1 “Can I make an appointment to see the doctor?” asked Bill.\n© *You must be mad!’ yelled her brother.\n\nIf the reporting clause comes within the quotation, you put a comma before the second\nquotation mark of the first part of the quotation, a comma at the end of the reporting clause,\nand you start the second part of the quotation with a lower case (not a capital) letter:\n\n© “It tastes horrible,” said Susan, “but it’s supposed to be very good for you.”\n\nIf the second part of the quotation is a new sentence, you put a full stop at the end of the\nreporting clause, and start the second part of the quotation with a capital letter:\n© *You should go home,” Sandra advised. “You're looking really ill.”\n\nIf the reporting clause comes before the quotation, you put a comma at the end of the reporting\nclause, and a full stop (or question or exclamation mark} at the end of the quotation:\n5 John said, “Put them all on the top shelf.”\n\nA colon is sometimes used at the end of the reporting clause instead of a comma:\n2 She stood up and shouted to the children: “It’s time to go home!”\n\n€ When you quote what a person thinks, you can either use the conventions described in A and B,\nor separate the quotation from the reporting clause with a comma (or colon) and leave out\nquotation marks:\n\nO “Why did she look at me like that?” wondered Mary.\nO Perhaps the door is open, thought Chris.\n©)
